Prioritizing Action Over Analyzing Motivation
Ghazali refutes an objection about the source of motivation to seek truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali postpones the question of the source of motivation and emphasizes immediate action.

Core Idea
Urgent danger demands action, not analysis of motivation.

This lesson addresses a common objection: how can we be sure our motivation to seek truth comes from reason, nature, or divine law? Ghazali responds that this question, while important, should not delay action. The presence of motivation itself obligates us to act. He illustrates this with a vivid metaphor of a person stung by a scorpion who foolishly hesitates to flee until he determines the direction of the sting.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice that the objector is not denying the motivation, but is uncertain about its source.
The objector is not rejecting the need to seek truth; he wants clarification on the basis of the motivation.
2
Understand Ghazali's postponement strategy
Ghazali does not dismiss the question as unimportant, but postpones it to later in the book. His point is that the immediate obligation to act overrides the need to settle the origin of motivation now.
This is a methodological move—prioritizing urgent action over theoretical inquiry.
3
Reflect on the metaphor
The metaphor of a person stung by a scorpion who, instead of fleeing, tries to identify from which direction the scorpion came. This illustrates the foolishness of delaying urgent action to answer secondary questions.
The metaphor is intended to be striking and memorable, driving home the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: إني لستُ منكراً هذا الانبعاثَ للطلب من نفسي، ولكني تحريجة فماهو لستُ أدري أنَّه ثمرة الجبلة والطبع، أو هو مقتضى العقل، أو هو موجب الشرع ؟ إذ للناس كلام في مدارك الوجوب .. فهذا إنما تعرفه في آخر الكتاب عند تعرضنا لمداركِ الوجوب، والاشتغال به الآن فضول، بل وجود البـاعـث محرض على وجوب لا سبيل بعد وقوع الانبعاث إلا الانتهاضُ لطلب الخلاص؛ فمثال الملتفت الإخلاص في البحث إلى ذلك مثالُ رجل لدغته حَيَّةٌ أو عقرب، وهي معاودة للدغ، والرجلُ قادرٌ على الفرار، ولكنه متوقفٌ ليعرف أنّ الحيَّةَ جاءته من جانب اليمين أو من جانب الشمال ؟ وذلك من أفعال الأغبياء والجهال نعوذ بالله من اشتغال بالفضول، مع تضييع المهمّاتِ والأصول .
If you say: 'I am not denying this motivation for seeking [the truth] from myself, but I am confused [؟] and I do not know whether it is the fruit of innate disposition and nature, or it is required by reason, or it is required by divine law? For people have discourse on the sources of obligation... This you will only know at the end of the book when we discuss the sources of obligation, and to occupy oneself with it now is superfluous. Rather, the existence of the motivator incites to an obligation; after the occurrence of the motivation, there is no way except to rise up to seek deliverance. The example of one who turns away from sincerity in research is like a man stung by a snake or scorpion, and it is recurring to sting, and the man is able to flee, but he hesitates to know whether the snake came from the right side or the left side. That is from the actions of the foolish and ignorant. We seek refuge in Allah from occupation with superfluous matters while neglecting important matters and principles.'
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector says he feels an inner motivation to seek truth but is uncertain whether this motivation stems from innate disposition (jibilla), reason ('aql), or divine law (shar'). He asks for clarification. The author responds that this question will be addressed later in the book and that occupying oneself with it now is superfluous (fudul). The real point is that the motivator itself impels action; after motivation occurs, one must rise to seek deliverance. The author gives a vivid metaphor: a person stung by a snake or scorpion, able to flee, but instead hesitates to determine from which direction the creature came. Such hesitation is foolish. The lesson is that when urgent danger exists, action should not be delayed by investigating the origin of motivation.

شرع
مدارك الوجوب
فضول

Misconception: The objection is dismissed as completely irrelevant and not worth answering.
Correction: Ghazali does not dismiss the question as irrelevant; he postpones it because immediate action is more pressing than theoretical analysis at this point.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Ghazali compares the one who delays to a man stung by a scorpion who stops to check which direction it came from. Where in your daily practice of Islam are you checking the direction instead of fleeing the sting? What one distraction are you willing to abandon today to act on what you already know?

Act on the Urge to Seek Knowledge Without Delay
Ghazali responds to an objection about the source of the motive for seeking knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali insists on acting upon the impulse to seek knowledge rather than analyzing its origin.

Core Idea
When bitten by the urge to learn, flee to knowledge — don't ask where the bite came from.

Ghazali here faces a common intellectual hesitation: before pursuing knowledge, one might feel the need to determine whether the motivation is natural, rational, or religious. But Ghazali argues that such analysis is a distraction from the urgent task itself. He uses a striking analogy to show that delaying action for unnecessary inquiry is foolish.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the objector's doubt (first part) and Ghazali's response (second part).
The passage is a single objection-and-answer unit.
2
Identify the three possible sources of motive
The objector lists: natural disposition (جبلة/طبع), reason (مقتضى العقل), and revelation (موجب الشرع). These reflect debates in Islamic theology.
Ghazali acknowledges the question but postpones it.
3
Understand Ghazali's postponement strategy
Ghazali says this will be clarified later; for now, the priority is action, not analysis.
He calls such inquiry 'superfluous' at this stage.
4
Analyze the snake analogy
The man bitten by a snake who stops to ask which side it came from is like the person who delays seeking knowledge to analyze its origin. Why is this a powerful teaching tool?
Ghazali emphasizes the urgency of the situation.
5
Reflect on the lesson for your own learning
Consider how this applies to your own pursuit of knowledge. Do you ever hesitate or overanalyze instead of taking the first step?
Apply the lesson practically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : إني لستُ منكراً هذا الانبعاثَ للطلب من نفسي، ولكني تحريجة فماهو لستُ أدري أنَّه ثمرة الجبلة والطبع ، أو هو مقتضى العقل ، أو هو موجب الشرع ؟ إذ للناس كلام في مدارك الوجوب .. فهذا إنما تعرفه في آخر الكتاب عند تعرضنا لمداركِ الوجوب ، والاشتغال به الآن فضول ، بل وجود البـاعـث محرض على وجوب لا سبيل بعد وقوع الانبعاث إلا الانتهاضُ لطلب الخلاص ؛ فمثال الملتفت الإخلاص في البحث إلى ذلك مثالُ رجل لدغته حيَّةٌ أو عقرب ، وهي معاودة للدغ ، والرجلُ قادرٌ على الفرار ، ولكنه متوقفٌ ليعرف أنّ الحيَّةَ جاءته من جانب اليمين أو من جانب الشمال ؟ وذلك من أفعال الأغبياء والجهال نعوذ بالله من اشتغال بالفضول ، مع تضييع المهمّاتِ والأصول .
If you say: I do not deny this impulse to seek [knowledge] from myself, but I am in doubt—for I do not know whether it is a result of natural disposition and temperament, or a requirement of reason, or a duty of the Law? Rather, the existence of a motive incites to an obligation; after the impulse has occurred, there is no way except to bestir oneself to seek deliverance. The example of one who turns his attention to sincerity in investigation regarding that is like the example of a man bitten by a snake or scorpion, and it keeps on biting, and the man is able to flee, but he delays to know whether the snake came from the right side or the left. That is the act of fools and ignoramuses. We seek refuge with Allah from occupying oneself with superfluities while neglecting important matters and fundamentals.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector raises a doubt about the motive for seeking knowledge: he feels the impulse but is uncertain whether it is from innate disposition, reason, or revelation. Ghazali responds by postponing that discussion—it will be treated later in the book when he addresses the sources of obligation. He argues that once the motive exists, it incites an obligation; one must act on it immediately rather than analyze its origin. He drives the point home with a vivid analogy: a person bitten by a snake or scorpion, able to flee, but who delays to determine from which direction the creature came, is foolish. Thus, engaging in such superfluous inquiry while neglecting the urgent task of seeking knowledge is blameworthy.

The First Group: Those with Sound Innate Faith
Ghazali classifies people and rules on the first group.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali exempts people of sound innate faith from learning kalam and the principle behind it.

Core Idea
Simple faith is complete; kalam can harm more than help.

Ghazali begins by comparing the proofs of kalam to medicines: they can heal or harm depending on the practitioner. He then divides people into four groups regarding learning kalam, and here he describes the first group—those blessed with sound innate faith. For such individuals, the ruling is clear: they should be left alone, as their faith is already sound and introducing kalam might introduce unnecessary doubts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and notice the medicine analogy
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to how Ghazali compares kalam proofs to medicines—they require a skilled practitioner.
This analogy sets the tone for the entire classification.
2
Identify the four groups mentioned
Ghazali states that people are divided into four groups. Focus on the first group, which is described in detail here.
The other groups are discussed in later lessons.
3
Understand the ruling for the first group
Ghazali says these people should be left alone and not urged to learn kalam. Why? Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) accepted simple affirmation without demonstrative proof.
This is based on prophetic practice, not personal opinion.
4
Reflect on the warning about causing doubts
Ghazali warns that presenting kalam proofs and their solutions might introduce doubts that cannot be removed. This shows his concern for preserving sound faith.
This highlights the potential harm of kalam for the unprepared.
5
Connect to the overall purpose of kalam
Kalam is a cure for hearts, but not everyone needs the same treatment. Just as a medicine can be poison if misused, kalam can harm those who do not need it.
This reinforces the importance of context and individual need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التمهيد الثاني في بيان أن الخوض في هذا العالم - وإن كان مهتماً فهو في فمَنِ بَعْضِ الخَانِي لَيْسَ مهم ، بل المراهم الأهم منزله اعلم : أنَّ الأدلة التي نحرِّرُها في هذا العلم تجري مجرى الأدوية التي يعالج بها مرضُ القلوب ، والطبيب المستعمل لها إن لم يكن حاذقاً ثاقب العقل رزين الرأي.. كان ما يفسده بدوائه أكثر مما يصلحه ، فليعلم المحصل اختلاف الناس بما المضمون هذا الكتاب ، والمستفيد لهذه العلوم : أنَّ الناس أربع فرق : تكن نفوسهم على أربع فرق الفرقة الأولى : طائفة الفطرة السليمة طائفة آمنت بالله ، وصدقت رسولَهُ ، واعتقدت الحق وأضمرته ، واشتغلت وحكم تعلم الكلام إما بعبادة وإما بصناعة ؛ فهؤلاء ينبغي أن يُتركوا على ما هم عليه ، ولا تحرك في حقها عقائدهم بالاستحثاث على تعلم هذا العلم ، فإنَّ صاحب الشرع - صلوات الله عليه ـ لم يطالب العرب في مخاطبته إياهم بأكثر من التصديق ، ولم يفرق بين أن يكون ذلك بإيمان وعقد تقليدي ، أو بيقين برهاني إنباته إيمان وهذا مما عُلمَ ضرورةً من مجاري أحواله في تزكيته إيمان من سبق من أجلاف العرب إلى تصديقه لا يبحث ولا برهان، بل بمجرد قرينة ومخيلة أجلاف الأعراب بوجود مخيلة تدل عليه سبقت إلى قلوبهم ، فقادتها إلى الإذعان للحقِّ والانقياد للصدق ، فهؤلاء مؤمنون حقاً ، فلا ينبغي أن تشوّش عليهم عقائدهم ؛ فإنَّه إذا تليت عليهم هذه البراهين وما عليها من الإشكالات وحلها .. لم يُؤمَنْ أنْ تعلق بأفهامهم مشكلة من المشكلات ، وتستولي عليها ولا تُمحى عنها بما يُذكَرُ من طرق الحل (١)
The Second Introduction: On clarifying that engaging in this science—though important—is not important for some people, but rather the most important of important matters. Know that the proofs we set forth in this science run the course of medicines with which the disease of hearts is treated. And the physician who uses them, if he is not skilled, sharp-minded, and sound in judgment, then what he corrupts by his medicine is more than what he rectifies. So let the student know the difference among people regarding the content of this book and the beneficiary of these sciences: that people are four groups: they are [divided into] four groups. The first group: people of sound innate disposition who believe in Allah, affirm His Messenger, believe the truth and conceal it, and occupy themselves—the ruling on learning kalam either with worship or with a craft. These should be left as they are, and their beliefs should not be disturbed by urging them to learn this science. For the Lawgiver—peace be upon him—did not require of the Arabs when addressing them more than affirmation, and he did not distinguish between whether that [affirmation] was by faith and traditional belief or by demonstrative certainty. He considered as faith the faith of those who preceded from the crude Arabs who affirmed him without investigation or proof, but merely by a sign and a mental image that preceded into their hearts, leading them to submission to truth and compliance with sincerity. These are truly believers, so their beliefs should not be disturbed. For if these proofs and the objections against them and their solutions are recited to them, it is not safe that some problematic doubt might cling to their minds and take hold of them, not to be erased by the methods of solution mentioned.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a classification and ruling. Ghazali begins by comparing the proofs of kalam to medicines: they can heal but also harm if misused. He then divides people into four groups regarding learning kalam, but here he only describes the first group. The first group are those of sound innate disposition who already have faith in Allah and His Messenger, hold the truth, and occupy themselves with worship or livelihood. For them, the ruling is that they should be left alone and not urged to learn kalam, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not require more than simple affirmation from the Arabs, whether that came through traditional belief or through demonstrative certainty. Indeed, many crude Arabs believed solely through a sign or a mental image that led them to submission, and the Prophet considered them true believers. Therefore, presenting these subtle proofs and the objections and solutions to them might introduce doubts that could not be removed. Ghazali warns against disturbing such simple, sound faith.

Key Terms
الفطرة السليمة
تصديق
عقد تقليدي
يقين برهاني
قرينة
مخيلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam is forbidden for all believers.
Correction: Ghazali only exempts those with sound innate faith; others may need kalam to address doubts or defend their faith.

Checkpoint

A) It is obligatory to teach them kalam to strengthen their faith.
B) It is recommended because knowledge is always beneficial.
C) They should be left alone and not pressured to learn it.
D) They must learn it to avoid being ignorant.

Key Takeaway
People of sound innate faith should not be forced to learn kalam, as their simple faith is sufficient and kalam may cause unnecessary doubts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you personally have a 'simple faith' that comes from a sign and a mental image, or have you sought demonstrative certainty? Are you at peace with the Prophet's acceptance of both, or does your heart long for more proof? If a doubt ever arises, what is your first response — do you seek to resolve it, or do you fear it might shake your faith entirely?
?
Have you ever, even unintentionally, disturbed someone else's simple faith by sharing intellectual arguments or doubts? Reflect on a specific instance. Could you have harmed more than helped? What will you do differently now when speaking about matters of belief with those of sound innate faith?
?
If you are someone who engages in kalam or theological argument, ask yourself: Are you truly a 'skilled physician'? Does your study of proofs increase your certainty and humility, or does it breed arrogance? If you were to present an argument to a person of simple faith, would you be willing to accept blame if it sowed a doubt you could not erase?

Kalam as Medicine: The First Group
Ghazali introduces medicine analogy and describes the first group of people.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's analogy of kalam as medicine and why people with sound faith should not be urged to study it.

Core Idea
Kalam is medicine: helpful for some, harmful for others.

Ghazali begins by comparing the proofs of kalam to medicines for the heart. Just as a bad physician can harm more than heal, an unskilled teacher of kalam can corrupt a student's faith. He then identifies the first group of people: those with sound innate faith who already believe in God and His Messenger. Even if their faith is based on imitation, they should be left alone, because the complexities of kalam might disturb their simple certainty. The Prophet himself did not demand demonstrative proof from the rough Arabs; their sincere submission was sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the medicine analogy
Read the opening lines where Ghazali compares kalam proofs to medicines and the scholar to a physician. Notice the warning that an unskilled physician does more harm than good.
2
Identify the first group
Find the description of the first group: those with sound disposition who already believe. Note that Ghazali says they should be left as they are and not urged to learn kalam.
The Prophet used this approach with the Arabs.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Understand why their beliefs should not be disturbed: reciting proofs and counterarguments might introduce doubts that cannot be resolved. Also, the Companions did not engage in kalam; they focused on worship and guidance.

صلوات الله عليه ـ لم يطالب العرب في مخاطبته إياهم بأكثر من التصديق ، ولم يفرق بين أن يكون ذلك بإيمان وعقد تقليدي ، أو بيقين برهاني إنباته إيمان وهذا مما عُلمَ ضرورةً من مجاري أحواله في تزكيته إيمان من سبق من أجلاف العرب إلى تصديقه لا يبحث ولا برهان، بل بمجرد قرينة ومخيلة أجلاف الأعراب بوجود مخيلة تدل عليه سبقت إلى قلوبهم ، فقادتها إلى الإذعان للحقِّ والانقياد للصدق ، فهؤلاء مؤمنون حقاً ، فلا ينبغي أن تشوّش عليهم عقائدهم ؛ فإنَّه إذا تليت عليهم هذه البراهين وما عليها من الإشكالات وحلها .. لم يُؤمَنْ أنْ تعلق بأفهامهم مشكلة من المشكلات ، وتستولي عليها ولا تُمحى عنها بما يُذكَرُ من طرق الحل. هذا ولم يُنقل عن الصحابة الخوض في هذا الفن لا بمباحثة ، ولا بتدريس ، ولا تصنيف ، بل كان شغلهم العبادة والدعوة إليها ، وحمل الخلق على مراشدهم ومصالحهم في أحوالهم وأعمالهم ومعاشهم فقط. الفرقة الثانية : طائفة مالت عن اعتقاد الحقِّ ؛ كالكفرة والمبتدعة : فالجافي الغليظ طائفة أهل الكفر والجحود التي تأبى الضعيف العقل ، الجامد على التقليد ، المتمرن على الباطل من مبدأ اتباع الحق النشوء إلى كبر السن .. لا ينفع معه إلا السوط والسيف ، فأكثر الكفرة أسلموا تحت ظلال السيوف؛ إذ يفعلُ الله بالسيف والسنان ما لا يفعل بالبرهان . وعن هذا إذا استقرأت تواريخ الأخبار .. لم تصادف ملحمة المسلمين والكفار إلا انكشفت عن جماعة من أهل الضلال مالوا إلى الانقياد ، ولم تصادف مجمَعَ مناظرة ومجادلة انكشفت إلا عن زيادة إصرار وعناد ، ولا تظنَّنَّ أنَّ هذا الذي ذكرناه غضٌ من منصب العقل وبرهانه ، ولكن نور العقل كرامة لا يخص الله بها إلا الآحاد من أوليائه والغالب على الخلق القصور والجهل ، فهم لقصورهم لا يدركون براهين العقل ، كما لا تدرك نور الشمس أبصارُ الخفافيش ، فهؤلاء تضرُّ بهم العلوم كما تضرُّ رياحُ الورد بالجُعَل ، وفي مثل هذا قال الشافعي
The Second Preamble: Explaining that engaging in this science - although important - is not important for some people, rather its status is the most important. Know that the proofs we set forth in this science run the course of medicines with which the illness of hearts is treated, and the physician who uses them, if he is not skilled, sharp-minded, and sound in judgment, what he corrupts by his medicine is more than what he rectifies. Let the student know, the differing of people regarding [؟] this book, and the one benefiting from these sciences: that people are four groups: their souls are upon four groups. The first group: the group of sound innate disposition, a group that believed in God, affirmed His Messenger, held the truth inwardly, and occupied themselves - and the ruling of learning kalam is either worship or a craft. These should be left as they are, and their beliefs should not be stirred by urging them to learn this science. For the Lawgiver - God's blessings be upon him - did not demand from the Arabs in his address to them more than affirmation, and he did not distinguish between that being by faith and traditional conviction or by demonstrative certainty. This is known necessarily from the course of his conduct in purifying the faith of those who preceded from the rough Arabs to affirm him, without investigation or proof, but merely by a sign and a notion. The rough Bedouins had a notion that indicated it, which preceded into their hearts, leading them to submission to the truth and compliance with honesty. These are true believers, so their beliefs should not be disturbed. For if these proofs and their difficulties and solutions are recited to them, it is not safe that a problem from the problems might attach to their minds, take hold of them, and not be erased by the methods of solution mentioned. Also, it is not transmitted from the Companions that they engaged in this art, neither by discussion, nor teaching, nor authorship. Rather, their occupation was worship, calling to it, and guiding people to their right paths and interests in their states, works, and livelihood only. The second group: a group that deviated from the belief of truth, like the disbelievers and innovators. As for the coarse stubborn one, the group of disbelief and denial that refuses, weak of intellect, rigid in imitation, practiced in falsehood from the beginning of youth following truth until old age - nothing benefits him except the whip and the sword. For most disbelievers submitted under the shadows of swords; indeed God does by the sword and the lance what He does not do by proof. Because of this, if you examine the chronicles of history, you do not find an encounter of Muslims and disbelievers except it disclosed a company of the misguided who inclined to submission, and you do not find a gathering of debate and argumentation except it disclosed except an increase of obstinacy and stubbornness. Do not suppose that what we have mentioned is a disparagement of the status of intellect and its proof. But the light of intellect is an honor that God does not bestow except upon the elite of His saints. The majority of people are deficient and ignorant; due to their deficiency, they do not perceive the proofs of intellect, just as the eyes of bats do not perceive the light of the sun. These are harmed by sciences, just as the fragrance of roses harms the dung beetle. About such, al-Shafi'i said: ...
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a preamble (tamheed) in which Imam Ghazali discusses the ruling on engaging in the science of kalam and who should learn it. He begins with an analogy: the proofs in this science are like medicines for hearts. Just as a medicine can harm if used by an unskilled doctor, so too can kalam cause harm if taught to the wrong person. He then advises the student (al-muhassil) to understand that people are divided into four groups, though the text details only the first two. The first group consists of people with sound natural disposition who already believe in God and His Messenger with sincere faith, even if it is taqlidi (traditional imitation). They should be left alone and not urged to learn kalam, because their simple faith might be disturbed by the complexities of proofs and counterarguments. The Prophet himself did not demand from the Arabs more than affirmation, without requiring demonstrative certainty. The early companions also did not engage in kalam; they focused on worship and guiding people practically. The second group are those who have deviated from the truth, like disbelievers and innovators. Within this group, the coarse, stubborn, and rigid individuals are not helped by debate but only by force (the sword). History shows that most conversions happened under the threat of the sword, not by arguments. However, the author clarifies that this is not a disparagement of reason itself; rather, reason is a light that God grants only to a few. Most people are deficient and cannot grasp intellectual proofs, just as bats cannot see sunlight. For such people, engaging in kalam is harmful, like the fragrance of roses that harms dung beetles. Al-Shafi'i is quoted in support of this, though the quote is not fully given.

Key Terms
الخوض في هذا العلم
الفرقة الأولى
إيمان تقليدي
يقين برهاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: kalam is beneficial for every Muslim to learn
Correction: Ghazali argues that for those with sound faith, kalam can be harmful and should be avoided.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why should the first group not be urged to learn kalam?
A) They are incapable of understanding it.
B) Their simple faith might be disturbed by complex arguments.
C) It is a waste of time for them.
D) They are already experts in kalam.

Key Takeaway
Kalam is like medicine: it can heal or harm, and for those with sound faith, it is better to leave them undisturbed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever introduced a complex theological argument to someone with simple faith, causing confusion or doubt? How can you apply Ghazali's warning to your own interactions?
?
Are you personally in the first group—content with simple faith—or do you feel the need to engage in kalam? What is the motive of your heart: love of truth or love of intellectual pride?
?
The Prophet did not demand demonstrative certainty from the Arabs. Do you find satisfaction in mere affirmation, or are you restless unless you can prove everything? What would it take for you to be at peace with traditional faith?

The Second Group: Deviants and the Limits of Reason
Ghazali describes the second group and defends reason while cautioning its limited applicability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's treatment of those who have deviated from the truth and his defense of reason as a light for the elite, not the masses.

Core Idea
Reason is a light for the elite, not the masses.

Ghazali now discusses the second group: those who have deviated from the truth, including disbelievers and innovators. He distinguishes between the stubborn who respond only to force and those who might be convinced. He then addresses a potential objection: is he demeaning reason? No, reason is a divine light granted only to a few. Most people are like bats that cannot see sunlight; for them, engaging in intellectual arguments is harmful, like the smell of roses to a dung beetle.

Guided Path
1
Read about the second group
Read the description of the second group, noting the subcategories: the coarse stubborn (helped by sword) and others. Understand that for some, only force works.
History shows that the sword often preceded submission.
2
Understand the bat analogy
Ghazali says most people are like bats that cannot see sunlight. This is not a put-down of reason but an observation of human limitation.
The 'light of intellect' is an honor from God to His saints.
3
Learn from the dung beetle analogy
Just as the fragrance of roses harms a dung beetle, kalam harms those unprepared. This reinforces that knowledge can be harmful in the wrong hands.

Why the Salaf Avoided Engaging in Kalam
Ghazali explains the reason the Salaf avoided kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reason the early Muslims (the Salaf) did not engage in kalam and what occupied their time instead.

Core Idea
The Salaf chose worship and guidance over theological debate.

This passage from Imam al-Ghazali explains a key methodological point: the early generations did not immerse themselves in kalam. It wasn't because they were incapable, but because their priorities lay elsewhere. Let's read the text and unpack its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the author's claim. Notice the contrast between what the Salaf avoided and what they actually did.
The Arabic uses 'بل' to emphasize the shift.
2
Identify the two key points
The passage states two things: 1) The Salaf avoided engaging in kalam through discussion, teaching, or writing. 2) Instead, they occupied themselves with worship, calling to it, and guiding people in their worldly and religious affairs.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why al-Ghazali mentions this. It is not a condemnation of kalam, but an explanation of priorities. The Salaf's focus was on direct action, not speculative theology.
This connects to the broader debate about the legitimacy of kalam.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا وعن لم ينقل عن الصحابة الخوض في هذا الفن سبب ترك السلف الخوض في هذا لا بمباحثة ولا بتدريس ولا تصنيف بل كان شغلهم العبادة والدعوة إليها وحمل الخلق على مراشدهم ومصالحهم في أحوالهم وأعمالهم ومعاشهم فقط
This, and because it was not narrated from the Companions that they delved into this art—the reason for the Salaf’s avoidance of delving into it was not through discussion, nor teaching, nor authorship, but rather their occupation was worship and calling to it, and guiding people to their sound paths and interests in their states and deeds and livelihood only.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage explains why the early Muslims (the Salaf) did not engage in ilm al-kalam. It states that the absence of transmitted reports from the Companions about engaging in this discipline is itself the reason they avoided it: they did not occupy themselves with debate, teaching, or writing on this subject. Instead, their focus was solely on worship, calling people to it, and guiding them in practical matters of life. The passage implies that kalam was not part of their priority, not because it is forbidden, but because their time was devoted to direct action and guidance.

Key Terms
السلف
الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Salaf were incapable of engaging in kalam or that it was forbidden.
Correction: The Salaf were capable but their priorities were worship and guidance; kalam was not a focus, not because it was inherently wrong.

Checkpoint
What was the occupation of the Salaf instead of kalam?
A) Debate
B) Teaching kalam
C) Worship and calling to it
D) Writing books

Key Takeaway
The Salaf's avoidance of kalam was due to their focus on worship and guidance, not inability or prohibition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a heated argument about a fine point of theology, do you honestly consider whether it is pulling you away from worship and guiding others? How often do you choose to end a debate for the sake of more time in prayer or service?
?
If your daily schedule were to be examined, would it more resemble the Salaf's focus on worship and practical guidance, or the pursuit of intellectual debate about religion? What is one change you can make this week to shift your balance toward action?
?
The Salaf did not teach or write about kalam; they dedicated their lives to living Islam. Are you more concerned with knowing about Islam or with practicing it? Answer honestly: have you ever defended a theological position while neglecting a clear duty of worship or character?

The Second Group: The Stubborn Disbelievers and the Role of the Sword
Classifying the second group and explaining the necessity of force for the stubborn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Ghazali classifies some disbelievers as needing coercion rather than rational argument, and how this does not diminish the value of intellectual proof.

Core Idea
Some people are so entrenched in falsehood that only the sword can open their hearts.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali continues his classification of people astray. After discussing those who can be won over by argument, he turns to a more recalcitrant group. He argues that for the coarse disbelievers who are weak-minded and hardened in imitation, rational proofs are useless; only force works. He then carefully clarifies that this does not mean reason is worthless—rather, it is a divine gift for the elect, while the majority lack the capacity for it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali uses a very descriptive style here.
2
Identify the second group
Find the description of the coarse disbelievers: 'الجافي الغليظ'. Note their characteristics: weak-minded, rigid in blind imitation, practiced in falsehood from youth.
This group is distinct from those who may respond to debate.
3
Examine the historical evidence
Ghazali claims that most disbelievers submitted under the sword, whereas debates only increased stubbornness. Think about the historical battles and conversions he might be referencing.
This is an empirical claim about effectiveness, not a theological one.
4
Understand the defense of intellect
Ghazali preempts the objection that he is denigrating reason. He explains that intellectual light is a divine gift for a few, just as sunlight is hidden from bats. Knowledge harms the ignorant like pleasant scents harm dung beetles. Reflect on these analogies.
He quotes al-Shafi‘i to reinforce the point: giving knowledge to the ignorant wastes it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثانية: طائفة مالت عن اعتقاد الحق؛ كالكفرة والمبتدعة: فالجافي الغليظ طائفة أهل الكفر والجحود التي تأبى الضعيف العقل، الجامد على التقليد، المتمرن على الباطل من مبدأ اتباع الحق النشوء إلى كبر السن.. لا ينفع معه إلا السوط والسيف، فأكثر الكفرة أسلموا تحت ظلال السيوف؛ إذ يفعل الله بالسيف والسنان ما لا يفعل بالبرهان. وعن هذا؛ إذا استقرأت تواريخ الأخبار.. لم تصادف ملحمة المسلمين والكفار إلا انكشفت عن جماعة من أهل الضلال مالوا إلى الانقياد، ولم تصادف مجمع مناظرة ومجادلة انكشفت إلا عن زيادة إصرار وعناد، ولا تظنن أن هذا الذي ذكرناه غض من منصب العقل وبرهانه، ولكن نور العقل كرامة لا يخص الله بها إلا الآحاد من أوليائه، والغالب على الخلق القصور والجهل، فهم لقصورهم لا يدركون براهين العقل، كما لا تدرك نور الشمس أبصار الخفافيش، فهؤلاء تضر بهم العلوم كما تضر رياح الورد بالجعل، وفي مثل هذا قال الشافعي رضي الله عنه وأرضاه: [من الطويل] بساحتها الشبه ومن منح الجهال علما أضاعه ومن منع المستوجبين فقد ظلم
The second group: a group that deviated from true belief, such as the disbelievers and innovators. As for the coarse, rough group—the people of disbelief and denial that refuses—the weak-minded, rigid in blind imitation, practiced in falsehood from the beginning of following the truth until old age—nothing benefits him except the whip and the sword. Most disbelievers submitted under the shadows of swords, for God does with the sword and lance what He does not do with proof. And because of this, if you examine historical accounts, you will not find a battle between Muslims and disbelievers except that it revealed a group of the misguided who inclined to submission, and you will not find a gathering of debate and argument except that it revealed only increased stubbornness and obstinacy. And do not think that what we have mentioned denigrates the status of the intellect and its proof. Rather, the light of the intellect is an honor that God bestows only on the elect of His friends. The majority of people are deficient and ignorant; due to their deficiency they do not perceive proofs of the intellect, just as the eyes of bats do not perceive the light of the sun. So knowledge harms them just as the scents of roses harm dung beetles. And concerning this, al-Shafi‘i, may God be pleased with him and grant him satisfaction, said: [in the meter of tawil] 'Its square resemble? And whoever gives knowledge to the ignorant has wasted it, and whoever prevents those who deserve it has wronged.'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage defines the second group of people—those who have deviated from true belief, including disbelievers and innovators. Within this group, the author distinguishes a particularly coarse type: those who are weak-minded, rigid in blind imitation, and have practiced falsehood since childhood. For such individuals, rational proof is ineffective; only coercion through the sword works. The author supports this by observing that historically, most disbelievers converted under threat of the sword, whereas debates only increased their stubbornness. He then addresses a potential objection: does this mean the intellect is worthless? He clarifies that the light of the intellect is a divine gift granted only to a few; most people are deficient like bats that cannot see sunlight. Thus, knowledge can harm the ignorant, just as pleasant scents harm dung beetles. He quotes al-Shafi‘i’s poem to emphasize that giving knowledge to the ignorant is a waste, while depriving those who deserve it is unjust. The overall point is that the method of addressing people depends on their capacity: some need force, others are receptive to argument.

Key Terms
الجافي الغليظ
السوط والسيف
نور العقل
الخفافيش
الجعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage shows that reason is useless and force is the only way to guide people.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that the light of the intellect is an honor for the elect, but most people are deficient and cannot grasp proofs. The sword is only for the stubborn, not all.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the most effective method for the coarse disbelievers?
A) Rational proof
B) Gentle persuasion
C) The whip and the sword
D) Teaching them kalam

Key Takeaway
For stubborn disbelievers, coercion through the sword is more effective than rational argument, but this does not diminish the value of reason for those capable of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you have been like the stubborn group—rigidly following tradition without examining the truth? What would it take for you to submit to the truth even if it requires a 'sword' against your ego?
?
Al-Ghazali says that knowledge can harm the ignorant. Have you ever shared deep knowledge with someone who was not ready, only to see it increase their pride or confusion? How can you better judge the capacity of those you teach?
?
Do you consider yourself among the 'elect' who can grasp rational proofs? If not, are you humble enough to submit to the truth through other means, or do you insist on proof you cannot understand?

The Third Group: Gradual Treatment for Believers with Doubts
Describing the third group of believers with doubts and the gradual treatment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Ghazali's recommended methodology for addressing the doubts of believers, emphasizing gentleness and matching the response to the individual's capacity.

Core Idea
Treat doubts gently: match the response to the person's need and capacity.

Imam al-Ghazali continues his classification of people regarding belief. After describing those who accept truth without question and those who reject it entirely, he now turns to a third group: believers who accepted truth out of tradition but later encountered doubts. These individuals require a careful, graduated response. This passage outlines the principle of 'gradual treatment' (al-talattuf), a cornerstone of Islamic pastoral care.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the descriptive tone and the practical advice it contains.
Pay attention to the conditions that lead to doubt (intelligence, exposure to shubuhat).
2
Identify the two stages of treatment
The passage describes two responses: simple persuasion (e.g., quoting a verse, making doubt seem improbable) and, if needed, true proof (al-dalil al-haqiqi). Identify where each is recommended and why.
3
Understand the principle of gradualism
Reflect on why the author advises against immediately using refined dialectical proofs. Consider the risk of opening 'other doors of problems' (abwaban ukhar min al-ishkalat).
This connects to the core idea: match the response to the person.
4
Apply the methodology
Think of a contemporary scenario where someone has doubts about faith. How would you apply this gradual approach? What would be the 'simple persuasion' and when would you move to deeper arguments?
This step encourages practical reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرقة الثالثة: طائفة مؤمنة اعتقدوا الحق تقليدا وسماعا، ولكن خصوا في الفطرة بذكاء وفطنة، فتنبهوا من أنفسهم لإشكالات شككتهم في عقائدهم، وزلزلت عليهم طمأنينتهم، أو قرع سمعهم شبهة من الشبه، وجالت في صدورهم الشكوك. فهؤلاء يجب التلطف في معالجتهم بإعادة طمأنينتهم وإماطة شكوكهم بما أمكن من الكلام المقنع المقبول عندهم، ولو بمجرد استبعاد وتقبيح، أو تلاوة آية، أو رواية حديث، أو نقل كلام من شخص مشهور عندهم بالفضل. فإذا زال شكه بذلك القدر.. فلا ينبغي أن يشافه بالأدلة المحررة على مراسم الجدال؛ فإن ذلك ربما يفتح عليه أبوابا أخر من الإشكالات، فإن كان ذكيا فطنا لم يقنعه إلا كلام يصير على محك التحقيق.. فعند ذلك يجوز أن يشافه بالدليل الحقيقي، وذلك على حسب الحاجة، وفي موضع الإشكال على الخصوص.
The third group: a believing group who believed the truth through imitation and hearsay, but they were distinguished in their innate disposition by intelligence and acumen, so they became alert by themselves to problems that caused doubt in their beliefs and shook their tranquillity, or a doubt from among the doubts struck their hearing and coursed in their breasts. So these must be treated gently by restoring their tranquillity and removing their doubts with whatever persuasive acceptable speech is possible, even if it is merely by [making the doubt seem] improbable and ugly, or by reciting a verse, or narrating a hadith, or quoting words from a person famous among them for virtue. When his doubt disappears by that amount, he should not be confronted with refined proofs according to the rules of disputation, for that might open for him other doors of problems. If he is intelligent and astute, nothing will convince him except words that stand the test of verification; in that case, it is permissible to confront him with the true proof, and that according to the need, and specifically at the place of the problem.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage describes the third group: believers who accepted truth uncritically but later encountered doubts due to their intelligence or exposure to skepticism. The recommended approach is gradual. They should first be treated with simple arguments—invoking improbability, reciting scripture, or referencing respected authorities. Only if their doubt requires more should deeper dialectical proofs be used, and cautiously, to avoid opening new doubts. The key principle is to match the response to the individual's capacity and the specific doubt.

Key Terms
التلطف
الطمأنينة
الشكوك
الدليل الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All doubts require complex, dialectical rebuttals.
Correction: The author advises starting with simple persuasion and reserving refined proofs for those who are intelligent and not satisfied by simpler means.

Checkpoint
What is the initial recommended response for a believer who has doubts?
A) Present refined dialectical proofs
B) Use simple persuasion like reciting a verse or quote
C) Ignore the doubts
D) Use the sword

Key Takeaway
When addressing doubts, begin with gentle persuasion and only escalate to deep proofs if needed and only for those who can handle them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt in your faith, do you rush to complex, intellectual answers or do you first seek to restore your heart's tranquility with simple reminders? Which approach brings you closer to certainty?
?
Think of someone you tried to guide who had doubts. Did your method match their capacity and need, or did you overwhelm them with arguments? What would you do differently now after learning this lesson?
?
Are you honest about your own intellectual capacity? Do you demand rigorous proofs for matters that might be better settled with a verse or a wise word? What will you change starting today?

The Fourth Group: The Misguided but Receptive
Describing the fourth group: misguided but receptive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the characteristics of the fourth group of misguided people and why gentle persuasion is appropriate for them.

Core Idea
Even among the misguided, some show signs of intelligence and openness to truth.

Imam al-Ghazali continues his classification of misguided groups by describing a fourth category: those who are astray yet display promising signs of receptiveness. Unlike the hopeless groups, these individuals exhibit intelligence and doubt about their own beliefs, making gentle guidance a viable approach. This lesson explores their traits and the recommended method for engaging with them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the group
Read the passage and note that this group is described as 'stray' and from the people of misguidance, but with signs of intelligence and acumen discerned in them.
They are not completely closed off like previous groups.
2
Find the signs of hope
Identify the two reasons why acceptance of truth is hoped for from this group: suspicion in their beliefs or softening of hearts due to doubt from innate disposition.
3
Recognize the appropriate method
Note that the text instructs that these people must be treated gently in winning them over to the truth and guiding them.
The approach is gentle persuasion, not harsh debate.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this approach contrasts with other groups that may require a different method, and why identifying such nuances is important in da'wah.

Treating Doubters: The Third Group's Gentle Care
Ghazali quotes a poem and describes the third group of believers disturbed by doubts, prescribing gentle treatment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's nuanced approach to addressing doubts in believers, distinguishing between those who need simple reassurance and those who require rigorous proof.

Core Idea
Not every doubter needs complex arguments; sometimes a gentle reminder suffices.

Ghazali continues his classification of believers by introducing a third group: those who accepted faith through imitation but are naturally intelligent. When doubts arise, they require a tailored response—gentle persuasion first, and only if needed, rigorous proof. This passage teaches us that pastoral care in theology demands wisdom in choosing one's approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening poem
Begin with the poetic quote to understand the principle that knowledge must be given appropriately: not to the ignorant, nor withheld from the deserving.
This sets the stage for the methodological discussion.
2
Identify the third group's characteristics
Notice that these are believers who accepted truth through imitation (taqlid) but have natural intelligence (futna). They become disturbed by doubts that shake their tranquility.
They are not hardened skeptics but sincere believers seeking clarity.
3
Examine the prescribed treatment
Ghazali recommends restoring tranquility with simple persuasion first—even deeming an idea unlikely, reciting a verse, or quoting a respected figure. Only if the person demands rigorous proof should one provide refined arguments.
Avoid unnecessary debate that might create more problems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رضي الله عنه وأرضاه : [ من الطويل] بساحتها الشبه وَمَنْ مَنَحَ الْجُهَالَ عِلْمًا أَضَاعَهُ وَمَنْ مَنَعَ الْمُسْتَوْجِبِينَ فَقَدْ ظَلَمْ(۱)
الفرقة الثالثة :
طائفة مؤمنة [نزلت طائفة؟] اعتقدوا الحقَّ تقليداً وسماعاً ، ولكن خصوا في الفطرة بذكاء وفطنة ، فتنبهوا من أنفسهم لإشكالات شككتهم في عقائدهم ، وزلزلت عليهم طمأنينتهم ، أو قرعَ سمعهم شبهة من الشبه ، وجالت في صدورهم .
والشكوك
التلطف

الإشكال على الخصوص
May Allah be pleased with him and grant him contentment: [From the long meter] In its courtyard is doubt. And whoever gives knowledge to the ignorant wastes it, and whoever withholds it from those deserving has wronged. (1)
The third faction:
A believing group [?] who believed the truth through imitation and hearsay, but were endowed in their nature with intelligence and acumen, so they became alert by themselves to problems that made them doubt their beliefs, and shook their tranquility, or a doubt from among the doubts struck their ears and surged in their chests.
Gentleness and gradualness in treatment: these must be treated by restoring their tranquility and removing their doubts with whatever persuasive speech is acceptable to them, even if only by deeming something unlikely and objectionable, or reciting a verse, or narrating a hadith, or quoting someone famous among them for virtue.
If his doubt is removed by that amount, he should not be confronted with refined proofs according to the rules of debate, for that might open other doors of problems. If he is intelligent and perceptive, nothing convinces him except speech that stands the test of verification. Then it is permissible to confront him with the true proof, according to need, and specifically in the place of the problem.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage opens with a poetic quotation that advises withholding knowledge from the ignorant and giving it to those deserving. It then describes the third category of people: believers who accepted the truth through imitation (taqlid) and hearsay, but are naturally intelligent and perceptive. When these individuals become disturbed by doubts, they require a gentle approach. The treatment consists of restoring their tranquility with persuasive speech that they accept, even if it's as simple as deeming an idea unlikely, reciting a Quranic verse, or quoting a respected figure. Once their doubt is resolved, they should not be given elaborate argumentation, as that might create new problems. Only if the person is highly intelligent and demands rigorous proof should the teacher provide a true proof tailored to the specific issue.

فطنة
شبهة
تلطف
جدال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All doubters require elaborate theological arguments to resolve their doubts.
Correction: Ghazali teaches that for the third group of believers, simple persuasion—like reciting a verse or quoting a scholar—is often sufficient, and engaging in debate may worsen the problem.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what should be avoided when treating the third group's doubts?
A) Gentle persuasion
B) Reciting verses or hadith
C) Confronting them with refined proofs of debate
D) Quoting a respected scholar

Key Takeaway
Ghazali teaches that the treatment of doubts must be tailored to the individual's background and intelligence, preferring gentle persuasion before advanced argumentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Fourth Group and the Gentle Approach
Ghazali describes the fourth group and prescribes gentle treatment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali identifies a fourth group with potential for guidance and why they require a gentle, non-confrontational approach.

Core Idea
Fanaticism blinds; gentleness opens the heart to truth.

Ghazali continues his classification of misguided groups, now turning to a unique category: people who are misguided yet show intelligence and a softening toward truth. He warns that harsh argumentation only deepens their error, and instead prescribes a compassionate method. This lesson focuses on that group and the recommended approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage description
Read the Arabic passage describing the fourth group. Notice the paradoxical combination: they are astray but show signs of intelligence and receptiveness.
The passage uses the word 'يُتفرَّسُ فيهم مخايل الذكاء' meaning 'in whom signs of intelligence are perceived.'
2
Identify the recommended method
Ghazali says they must be treated gently (التلطف), not with argumentation and fanaticism (المحاجة والتعصب). Find that contrast and understand why.
The method is not to challenge them harshly but to win them over with kindness.
3
Reflect on the warning
Ghazali warns that fanaticism increases the causes of misguidance and stirs persistence. Why does he emphasize this here?
He is contrasting the proper way with the harmful way.

ولولا استيلاء الشيطان بواسطة العناد والتعصب للأهواء . . لما وجد مثل
السبل
هذا الاعتقادِ مستقراً في قلب مجنون فضلاً عن قلب عاقل ، فالمجادلة التلطف والرحمة خير والمعاندة داء محض لا دواء له ؛ فليحترز المتديّن منه جهدَه ، وليترك الحقد والضغينة ، ولينظر إلى كافَةِ خلق الله بعين الرحمة ، وليستعن بالرفق واللطف
الضال
في إرشاد من ضلَّ من هذه الأمة ، وليتحفظ من النكد الذي يحرّكُ من داعية الضلالة ، وليتحقق أنَّ مهيج داعية الإصرار بالعناد والتعصب معين على الإصرار على البدعة ، ومطالب بعهدة إعانته في القيامة
(۱) وهم الحشوية المنتمون إلى الظاهر؛ حيث اعتقدوا بقدم الحرف والصوت والرسم واللون لكلام الله تعالى، وكما قال إمام الحرمين الجويني رحمه الله تعالى : ( ولا خفاء بمراغمتهم لبديهة العقول في حكمهم بانقلاب الحادث قديماً ) . ( الإرشاد، ( ص ۱۲۹ )
The fourth faction:
A misguided faction, not a faction of the people of misguidance, in whom signs of intelligence and acumen are perceived, and from whom stubbornness is expected, yet it gives hope that they will accept the truth due to the doubt that has afflicted their beliefs, or due to what softens their hearts to accept inclination to the truth.
[New line] Questioning by nature and disposition.
These must be treated gently in winning them over to the truth and guiding them to correct belief, not in the context of argumentation and fanaticism. For that increases the causes of misguidance and stirs up the motives of persistence and stubbornness. Most ignorances are due to fanaticism, a pestilent disease and a satanic path that became ingrained in the hearts of the common people through the fanaticism of a group of ignorant people among the people of truth. They manifested the truth in the context of challenge and presentation, and looked at weak opponents with an eye of contempt and belittlement; so from their inner selves arose the motives of opposition and contradiction, and false beliefs became ingrained in their souls, and it became [difficult?] for gentle scholars to erase them despite the manifestation of their corruption.
Until fanaticism led a group to believe that the letters they uttered immediately after remaining silent for a long lifetime are eternal! (1)
Were it not for the devil's domination through obstinacy and fanaticism for whims, such a belief would not find a place in the heart of a madman, let alone a sane heart. So debate with gentleness and mercy is better, and obstinacy is a pure disease with no cure. So the religious person should guard against it as much as possible, abandon hatred and rancor, look at all of Allah's creatures with an eye of mercy, seek help with kindness and gentleness in guiding those who have gone astray from this community, be cautious of the annoyance that stirs the motive of misguidance, and realize that the one who incites the motive of persistence through obstinacy and fanaticism is a helper in persisting with innovation and will be held accountable for his assistance on the Day of Resurrection.
(1) They are the Hashwiyya, those who adhere to the external [meaning]; for they believed in the eternity of the letter, sound, script, and color of the speech of Allah the Exalted. And as Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'There is no hiding their contradiction to the clear intellect in their ruling that the temporal has turned into eternal.' (Al-Irshad, p. 129)
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth category of people: those who are misguided but show signs of intelligence and potential for accepting the truth. They require gentle treatment, not confrontational argumentation, because fanaticism only increases their error. Ghazali then launches into a general warning against fanaticism, describing it as a disease and a satanic path that misleads even the common people. He cites a drastic example: fanaticism led a group (the Hashwiyya) to believe that the letters and sounds they utter are eternal, despite the fact that they are temporal occurrences. He quotes Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who points out the clear contradiction: they claim that the temporal has become eternal. The lesson concludes with an exhortation to adopt gentleness and mercy in debate, to avoid fanaticism and obstinacy, and to guide others with kindness, because the helper in persistence with innovation will be held accountable.

Key Terms
تعصب
تلطف
محاجة
مخايل الذكاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What method does Ghazali recommend for guiding the fourth group?
A) Harsh debate and challenge
B) Gentle persuasion and kindness
C) Ignoring them completely
D) Excommunication and isolation

Key Takeaway
Even misguided people who show signs of intelligence should be guided with gentleness, not fanaticism, which only reinforces error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone smart but stubborn in falsehood, does your instinct lean toward proving them wrong with arguments? How does Ghazali's call to gentleness expose a flaw in your natural reaction? Will you commit to changing your approach the next time you face such a person?
?
Have you ever been guilty of presenting the truth with contempt or a sense of superiority, even unintentionally? Can you recall a specific incident where your tone made someone more resistant? What would it cost you to adopt Ghazali's gentle method instead?
?
Ghazali states that most ignorances are due to fanaticism spread by 'ignorant people among the people of truth.' Examine your own circles: are you part of a group that sometimes belittles others' understanding? How might your conduct be driving people away from truth that you claim to uphold?

The Hashwiyya and the Absurdity of Fanaticism
Ghazali exemplifies fanaticism with the Hashwiyya's belief in eternal letters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how fanaticism led the Hashwiyya to the absurd belief that temporal letters are eternal, and why Ghazali condemns such stubbornness.

Core Idea
Fanaticism can drive even rational people to embrace absurdities.

Having warned against fanaticism, Ghazali gives a striking example: a group (the Hashwiyya) became so entrenched in stubbornness that they believed the letters and sounds they utter after a lifetime of silence are eternal. This lesson examines that belief, its criticism by al-Juwayni, and Ghazali's call to abandon hatred and adopt mercy in debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the example of fanaticism
Read from 'حتى انتهى التعصب بطائفة' to the end. Note the extreme belief: the letters (الحروف) they utter after silence are eternal (قديمة).
This is the Hashwiyya sect, mentioned in the footnote.
2
Understand the criticism
Ghazali quotes al-Juwayni: the Hashwiyya contradict clear reason by judging that something temporal (the uttered letters) is eternal. Reflect on the absurdity.
The footnote explains that they also believed the script and color of the Quranic text are eternal.
3
Apply the lesson on fanaticism
Ghazali concludes with a general exhortation: avoid hatred, look at all creatures with mercy, and use gentleness in guiding the misguided. Why is this the remedy?
Stubbornness is a disease without cure; gentleness is the only way.

?

The Harm of Contentious Debate
Ghazali warns against disputation and bigotry as sources of misguidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali condemns contentious debate and how bigotry leads to entrenched falsehood.

Core Idea
Contentious debate does not clarify truth; it entrenches error.

Ghazali begins this passage by distinguishing the proper pursuit of correct belief from the harmful practice of disputation and bigotry. He warns that presenting truth in a confrontational manner only hardens opposition and plants false beliefs deeper in the heart. This lesson explores that principle and its catastrophic results, using the example of a sect that believed uttered letters are pre-eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation without analysis. Let the author's own warning sink in.
Notice the strong language: 'malignant disease', 'satanic path'.
2
Identify the causal chain
Ghazali describes a progression: presenting truth with challenge and belittling opponents → stirs up stubbornness → false beliefs become entrenched. Trace this chain in the text.
Look for the phrases 'يزيد في دواعي الضلال' and 'رسخت في قلوب العوام'.
3
Reflect on the example of the Hashwiyya
The Hashwiyya believed uttered letters are pre-eternal. How does this illustrate the harm of bigotry? Consider why this belief could only take root due to obstinacy.
Ghazali says even a madman would not hold such belief without satanic influence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتقاد الصحيح لا في معرض المحاجة والتعصب ؛ فإنَّ ذلك يزيدُ في دواعي الضلال ، ويهيج بواعث التمادي والإصرار ، وأكثر الجهالات إنما التعصب داء وبيء وسبيل شيطاني رسخت في قلوب العوام بتعصب جماعة من جهال أهل الحق أظهروا الحق في معرض التحدي والإدلاء ، ونظروا إلى ضعفاء الخصوم بعين التحقير والإزراء ؛ فثارت من بواطنهم دواعي المعاندة والمخالفة ، ورسخت في نفوسهم الاعتقادات الباطلة ، وعشر على العلماء المتلطَّفين محوها مع ظهور حتى انتهى التعصب بطائفة إلى أن اعتقدوا أن الحروف التي نطقوا بها في الحال بعد السكوت عنها طول العمر قديمة ! فسادها ، ولولا استيلاء الشيطان بواسطة العناد والتعصب للأهواء . . لما وجد مثل هذا الاعتقادِ مستقراً في قلب مجنون فضلاً عن قلب عاقل ، فالمجادلة التلطف والرحمة خير والمعاندة داء محض لا دواء له ؛ فليحترز المتديّن منه جهدَه ، وليترك الحقد والضغينة ، ولينظر إلى كافَةِ خلق الله بعين الرحمة ، وليستعن بالرفق واللطف في إرشاد من ضلَّ من هذه الأمة ، وليتحفظ من النكد الذي يحرّكُ من داعية الضلالة ، وليتحقق أنَّ مهيج داعية الإصرار بالعناد والتعصب معين على الإصرار على البدعة ، ومطالب بعهدة إعانته في القيامة وهم الحشوية المنتمون إلى الظاهر ؛ حيث اعتقدوا بقدم الحرف والصوت والرسم واللون لكلام الله تعالى ، وكما قال إمام الحرمين الجويني رحمه الله تعالى : ( ولا خفاء بمراغمتهم لبديهة العقول في حكمهم بانقلاب الحادث قديماً ) .
Correct belief is not in the context of disputation and bigotry; for that increases the motives of misguidance and stirs up the incentives of persistence and obstinacy. And most ignorance is only [due to] bigotry, a malignant disease and a satanic path, rooted in the hearts of the common people by the bigotry of a group of ignorant people of truth who presented truth in the context of challenge and argument, and looked at weak opponents with an eye of contempt and belittlement; so there arose from their innermost motives of stubbornness and opposition, and false beliefs became entrenched in their souls, and the kind scholars found it difficult to erase them despite their apparent corruption. And bigotry led a sect to believe that the letters they uttered at the moment after being silent about them for a long time are pre-eternal! And were it not for Satan's domination through obstinacy and bigotry for desires, such a belief would not find a place in the heart of a madman, let alone a rational person. So argumentation with gentleness and mercy is better, and obstinacy is a pure disease with no cure. So let the religious person guard against it with his effort, and leave hatred and malice, and look at all of God's creation with the eye of mercy, and seek help with kindness and gentleness in guiding whoever has gone astray from this nation, and beware of the mischief that stirs up the motive of misguidance, and realize that the one who stirs up the motive of persistence through obstinacy and bigotry is an assistant in persisting in innovation, and will be held responsible for his assistance on the Day of Resurrection. And they are the Hashwiyya who adhere to the literal; they believed in the pre-eternity of the letter, the sound, the script, and the color for the speech of God Almighty, and as Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni (may God have mercy on him) said: 'There is no concealment of their contradicting the immediate intellect in their judgment that the temporal is transformed into the pre-eternal.'
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a warning against contentious debate and bigotry in matters of theology. Ghazali begins by stating that correct belief is not to be pursued through contentious disputation (muhajja) and bigotry (ta'assub), as these only increase the incentives for misguidance and persistence in error. He identifies bigotry as a malignant disease and a satanic path that becomes rooted in the hearts of the common people through the actions of ignorant pseudo-scholars who present truth in a confrontational manner, belittling opponents. This provokes stubbornness and false beliefs become entrenched. As an extreme example, Ghazali mentions a sect (the Hashwiyya) who came to believe that the letters they utter with their mouths are pre-eternal (qadim), a manifest absurdity. He notes that without Satan's manipulation through obstinacy, such a belief could not persist even in a madman. He contrasts this with gentle argumentation (mujadala bi-l-lutf wa-l-rahma) which is beneficial, while obstinacy is incurable. He advises the religious person to avoid hatred, show mercy to all, and use kindness in guiding the misguided. He emphasizes that provoking stubbornness through bigotry only assists in persisting in innovation and incurs responsibility before God. Finally, he quotes Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni to confirm that the Hashwiyya's belief in the pre-eternity of temporal things contradicts clear reason.

Key Terms
تعصب
محاجة
حشوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All forms of theological debate are beneficial and help clarify truth.
Correction: Ghazali shows that contentious debate with bigotry actually reinforces falsehood and makes it harder to correct, unlike gentle guidance.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the immediate effect of presenting truth with challenge and contempt?
A) It refutes falsehood effectively.
B) It increases motives of misguidance and stirs up persistence in error.
C) It impresses the common people.
D) It is the method of the prophets.

Key Takeaway
Correct belief is not achieved through contentious debate; bigotry only strengthens falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in debates about Islam, have you ever felt a sense of superiority or contempt toward your opponent? How does that contradict Ghazali's warning that such an attitude only entrenches falsehood?
?
Think of a time you persisted in an argument even after you sensed you might be wrong. What was the belief you were defending, and could it have been a product of bigotry and stubbornness rather than truth?
?
Is there a relationship or community conversation where you habitually present truth in a confrontational 'challenge' style? How might you instead embody the gentleness of the 'kind scholars' (المتلطَّفين) Ghazali mentions, so that hearts remain open?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
Gentle Guidance and the Hashwiyya Error
Ghazali prescribes gentle argument and critiques the Hashwiyya's belief in pre-eternal letters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper method of theological argument (gentleness and mercy) and why the Hashwiyya belief in pre-eternal letters contradicts immediate reason.

Core Idea
Gentle argument cures; obstinacy is a disease. The Hashwiyya exemplify the latter.

After warning against contentious debate, Ghazali prescribes the remedy: argumentation with gentleness and mercy, not obstinacy. He then returns to the Hashwiyya as a case study, quoting Imam al-Juwayni to confirm that their belief in pre-eternal letters contradicts clear reason. This lesson focuses on the positive method and the rational refutation of an extreme position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on proper argumentation
Start from 'فالمجادلة بالتلطف والرحمة خير' through to the advice to avoid hatred and show mercy. Note the contrast with the earlier description of contentious debate.
Identify the key verbs: 'ليحترز', 'ليترك', 'لينظر', 'ليستعن'.
2
Understand the Hashwiyya belief as described
Read the final sentences where Ghazali states they believed in the pre-eternity of letter, sound, script, and color for God's speech. Consider why this is problematic.
Al-Juwayni's quote: 'انقلاب الحادث قديماً' – transforming the temporal into the pre-eternal.
3
Analyze the rational contradiction
Al-Juwayni says this contradicts 'بديهة العقول' (immediate intellect). Explain in your own words why claiming that temporal letters are eternal is a logical impossibility.
The letters are created events (hādith) but are said to be pre-eternal (qadīm).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتقاد الصحيح لا في معرض المحاجة والتعصب ؛ فإنَّ ذلك يزيدُ في دواعي الضلال ، ويهيج بواعث التمادي والإصرار ، وأكثر الجهالات إنما التعصب داء وبيء وسبيل شيطاني رسخت في قلوب العوام بتعصب جماعة من جهال أهل الحق أظهروا الحق في معرض التحدي والإدلاء ، ونظروا إلى ضعفاء الخصوم بعين التحقير والإزراء ؛ فثارت من بواطنهم دواعي المعاندة والمخالفة ، ورسخت في نفوسهم الاعتقادات الباطلة ، وعشر على العلماء المتلطَّفين محوها مع ظهور حتى انتهى التعصب بطائفة إلى أن اعتقدوا أن الحروف التي نطقوا بها في الحال بعد السكوت عنها طول العمر قديمة ! فسادها ، ولولا استيلاء الشيطان بواسطة العناد والتعصب للأهواء . . لما وجد مثل هذا الاعتقادِ مستقراً في قلب مجنون فضلاً عن قلب عاقل ، فالمجادلة التلطف والرحمة خير والمعاندة داء محض لا دواء له ؛ فليحترز المتديّن منه جهدَه ، وليترك الحقد والضغينة ، ولينظر إلى كافَةِ خلق الله بعين الرحمة ، وليستعن بالرفق واللطف في إرشاد من ضلَّ من هذه الأمة ، وليتحفظ من النكد الذي يحرّكُ من داعية الضلالة ، وليتحقق أنَّ مهيج داعية الإصرار بالعناد والتعصب معين على الإصرار على البدعة ، ومطالب بعهدة إعانته في القيامة وهم الحشوية المنتمون إلى الظاهر ؛ حيث اعتقدوا بقدم الحرف والصوت والرسم واللون لكلام الله تعالى ، وكما قال إمام الحرمين الجويني رحمه الله تعالى : ( ولا خفاء بمراغمتهم لبديهة العقول في حكمهم بانقلاب الحادث قديماً ) .
Correct belief is not in the context of disputation and bigotry; for that increases the motives of misguidance and stirs up the incentives of persistence and obstinacy. And most ignorance is only [due to] bigotry, a malignant disease and a satanic path, rooted in the hearts of the common people by the bigotry of a group of ignorant people of truth who presented truth in the context of challenge and argument, and looked at weak opponents with an eye of contempt and belittlement; so there arose from their innermost motives of stubbornness and opposition, and false beliefs became entrenched in their souls, and the kind scholars found it difficult to erase them despite their apparent corruption. And bigotry led a sect to believe that the letters they uttered at the moment after being silent about them for a long time are pre-eternal! And were it not for Satan's domination through obstinacy and bigotry for desires, such a belief would not find a place in the heart of a madman, let alone a rational person. So argumentation with gentleness and mercy is better, and obstinacy is a pure disease with no cure. So let the religious person guard against it with his effort, and leave hatred and malice, and look at all of God's creation with the eye of mercy, and seek help with kindness and gentleness in guiding whoever has gone astray from this nation, and beware of the mischief that stirs up the motive of misguidance, and realize that the one who stirs up the motive of persistence through obstinacy and bigotry is an assistant in persisting in innovation, and will be held responsible for his assistance on the Day of Resurrection. And they are the Hashwiyya who adhere to the literal; they believed in the pre-eternity of the letter, the sound, the script, and the color for the speech of God Almighty, and as Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni (may God have mercy on him) said: 'There is no concealment of their contradicting the immediate intellect in their judgment that the temporal is transformed into the pre-eternal.'
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a warning against contentious debate and bigotry in matters of theology. Ghazali begins by stating that correct belief is not to be pursued through contentious disputation (muhajja) and bigotry (ta'assub), as these only increase the incentives for misguidance and persistence in error. He identifies bigotry as a malignant disease and a satanic path that becomes rooted in the hearts of the common people through the actions of ignorant pseudo-scholars who present truth in a confrontational manner, belittling opponents. This provokes stubbornness and false beliefs become entrenched. As an extreme example, Ghazali mentions a sect (the Hashwiyya) who came to believe that the letters they utter with their mouths are pre-eternal (qadim), a manifest absurdity. He notes that without Satan's manipulation through obstinacy, such a belief could not persist even in a madman. He contrasts this with gentle argumentation (mujadala bi-l-lutf wa-l-rahma) which is beneficial, while obstinacy is incurable. He advises the religious person to avoid hatred, show mercy to all, and use kindness in guiding the misguided. He emphasizes that provoking stubbornness through bigotry only assists in persisting in innovation and incurs responsibility before God. Finally, he quotes Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni to confirm that the Hashwiyya's belief in the pre-eternity of temporal things contradicts clear reason.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hashwiyya represent mainstream traditionalism in Islamic theology.
Correction: Ghazali and al-Juwayni consider their belief in pre-eternal letters as an extreme innovation that contradicts clear reason, not a mainstream position.

Checkpoint
According to al-Juwayni, why does the Hashwiyya belief in pre-eternal letters contradict immediate reason?
A) Because letters cannot be eternal by nature.
B) Because it involves judging that a temporal thing is transformed into the pre-eternal.
C) Because the Qur'an is uncreated.
D) Because the letters are human inventions.

Key Takeaway
Gentle guidance is the cure for theological error; obstinacy and bigotry lead to irrational beliefs like the Hashwiyya's pre-eternal letters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a recent religious disagreement you had. Were you approaching it with gentleness and mercy, or did you sense obstinacy rising in your heart? How can you change your approach before your next discussion?
?
The Hashwiyya held an irrational belief because of their bigotry. Is there a belief or practice you cling to more out of loyalty to a group than because it is rationally sound? Are you willing to re-examine it today?
?
Ghazali warns that assisting persistence in innovation through obstinacy makes one accountable on the Day of Resurrection. Have you ever insisted on your view in a way that pushed someone further into error? What would it take for you to be a guide with mercy instead?
