المدلول
الدليل
النظر
الفكر
الطلب
الوظيفتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that simply recalling the premises (fikr) is enough for reasoning.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that reasoning requires both fikr and talab—the active seeking of the logical connection between premises and conclusion.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what are the two functions (وظيفتان) of the researcher in attaining knowledge?
A) Bringing the two roots to mind and seeking the aspect of necessity of the conclusion
B) Formulating the proof and denying the opponent's claim
C) Defining al-dalīl and al-madlūl
D) Combining the first and third methods

Key Takeaway
Reasoning (nazar) requires both bringing premises to mind (fikr) and actively seeking the logical connection between them (talab); neither alone suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a proof for a belief in Islam, do you stop at merely memorizing the premises (fikr), or do you actively strive to see how the conclusion necessarily follows (talab)? What would change in your understanding if you committed to the second function today?
?
Ghazali says that those who define nazar as only fikr have an incomplete view. In your own journey of seeking knowledge, have you been satisfied with just 'thinking’ without pressing further to grasp the logical necessity? How can you move from passive recall to active engagement starting with the next proof you encounter?
?
If you were to hold yourself accountable for the way you reason about religious matters, would you say you truly seek (talab) the aspect of necessity, or do you often settle for a surface-level understanding? What is one concrete step you will take this week to ensure your nazar includes both fikr and talab?

The Three Methods of Proof (Manahij)
Ghazali enumerates three methods of inference and illustrates them with the example of infinite cycles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three methods of rational inference that Ghazali identifies, how they address an opponent's denials, and how the concession of premises leads to a necessary conclusion.

Core Idea
A proof is established through two premises that, when conceded, yield necessary knowledge.

Imam al-Ghazali opens by illustrating the logical structure of a proof using a concrete example. He describes three methods (manahij) of inference that account for possible denials by an opponent. This passage shows how a properly constructed argument compels acceptance when both premises are granted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two types of denial
Notice the first denial: the opponent rejects the implication. The second denial: the opponent rejects the impossibility of the entailment.
2
Find the third method
The third method is when both premises are conceded, leading to a necessary third knowledge.
This third knowledge is the conclusion that the opponent's original claim is impossible.
3
Examine the footnote example
The footnote provides a concrete example: the opponent claims the cycles of the sphere are infinite.
The first principle: the passing away of what has no end follows necessarily. The second principle: this consequence is impossible. The third knowledge: the claim is false and cycles are finite.
4
Reflect on why these methods are 'clear'
Ghazali states that these three methods are clear and yield knowledge that cannot be denied.
This highlights the strength of rational proof in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنكار؛ بأن يقول : لا أسلم أنه يلزم ذلك استحاله
والثاني : قولنا هذا اللازم محال ، فإنَّه أيضاً أصل يتصور فيه إنكار ؛ بأن يقول : سلمت الأصل الأول ، ولكن لا أسلم هذا الثاني وهو انقضاء ما لا نهاية له ، ولكن لو أقرَّ بالأصلين .. كان الإقرار بالمعلوم الثالث اللازم منهما واجباً بالضرورة ، وهو الإقرار باستحالة مذهبه المفضي إلى هذا المحال (۱)
فهذه ثلاثة مناهج في الاستدلال جليّة ، لا يتصور إنكار حصول العلم منها ، فالعلم الحاصل المطلوب هو المدلول ، وازدواج الأصلين الملزمين لهذا العلم هو الدليل ، والعلم بوجه لزوم هذا المطلوب من ازدواج الأصلين علم بوجه دلالةِ الدليل ، وفكرك الذي هو عبارة عن إحضارك الأصلين في الذهن ، وطلبكَ التفطن لوجه لزوم العلم وطلبُكَ التفطن لوجه لزوم العلم الثالث من العلمين الأصلين .. هو النظر
(۱) بيان المسألة : دعوى الخصم : دورات الفلك لا متناهية . الأصل الأول : انقضاء ما لا نهاية له بناءً على هذه الدعوى لزوماً
الأصل الثاني : نفي هذا اللازم، وإثبات استحالة انقضاء ما لا نهاية له .
المعلوم الثالث : الإقرار - بعد تسليم الخصم بالأصلين معاً - باستحالة الدعوى ، وبطلان قوله : إن دورات الفلك غير متناهية ، وإثبات ضدها بأنها متناهية ، وهو المطلوب
And denial by saying: I do not concede that such and such is entailed. The second: our saying this entailment is impossible, for this too is a principle in which denial can be conceived, by saying: I concede the first principle, but I do not concede this second one, which is the passing away of what has no end. But if he concedes both principles, then the affirmation of the third knowledge necessarily required from them becomes obligatory, and it is the affirmation of the impossibility of his doctrine that leads to this impossibility. So these are three clear methods of inference, from which it is inconceivable to deny obtaining knowledge. The obtained sought knowledge is the implied (madlul), the pairing of the two principles necessitating this knowledge is the evidence (dalil), and knowledge of the mode of implication of this sought matter from the pairing of the two principles is knowledge of the mode of indication of the evidence. Your thinking (fikr), which is your bringing the two principles to mind, and your seeking to be attentive to the mode of implication of the knowledge and your seeking to be attentive to the mode of implication of the third knowledge from the two original knowledges, is nazar. (1) Explanation of the matter: The opponent's claim: the cycles of the sphere are infinite. The first principle: the passing away of what has no end follows necessarily from this claim. The second principle: the negation of this necessary consequence, and the proof of the impossibility of the passing away of what has no end. The third knowledge: after the opponent concedes both principles together, the acknowledgment of the impossibility of the claim, and the falsity of his statement that the cycles of the sphere are infinite, and the proof of its opposite, that they are finite, and that is the sought matter.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the structure of a proof using a concrete example. Ghazali describes three methods (manahij) of rational inference. The first method is the opponent's denial of the implication: 'I do not concede that such and such is entailed.' The second method is our assertion that the entailment is impossible, but the opponent might deny that impossibility, e.g., the passing away of what has no end. However, if both premises are conceded, the third knowledge necessarily follows: the impossibility of the opponent's doctrine. Ghazali then explicitly states that these three methods are clear and yield necessary knowledge. He then defines the terms: the sought knowledge is the madlul (the implied), the combination of the two principles that entail this knowledge is the dalil (the evidence), and the knowledge of the mode of implication is the mode of indication of the evidence. Finally, he defines fikr (thinking) as bringing the two premises to mind, and the seeking of the mode of implication is nazar. The footnote provides a concrete application: the opponent claims the cycles of the sphere are infinite. The first principle is the necessary consequence: the passing away of what has no end. The second principle denies that consequence as impossible. The third knowledge is then the acknowledgment that the opponent's claim is impossible and that the cycles must be finite. This illustrates how a proper argument works in theology.

Key Terms
منهج
استدلال
أصل
لازم
معلوم ثالث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three methods might be misunderstood as three separate types of arguments.
Correction: They are actually three steps within a single argumentative strategy: first denial of implication, second denial of impossibility, and third acceptance of both premises leading to necessary conclusion.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the third method of inference?
A) Denying the implication
B) Denying the impossibility of the entailment
C) Accepting both premises and acknowledging the necessary conclusion
D) Introducing a new premise

Key Takeaway
The three methods show that a rational proof in kalam relies on conceding two premises which then necessitate a conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone assert that the universe is eternal, do you instinctively feel confused or unsettled? Have you ever taken the time to identify the two premises Ghazali would demand be conceded before accepting or rejecting that claim? Starting today, will you commit to laying out the premises of every doctrinal challenge you face?
?
You have conceded premises countless times in daily arguments. Have you ever applied the same discipline to your own beliefs about Allah? What is one belief you hold that you have not tested by examining whether its premises truly necessitate its conclusion? Will you do so now?

The Terminology of Inference (Dalil, Madlul, Wajh, Fikr, Nazar)
Ghazali defines the components of a proof: madlul, dalil, wajh al-dalala, fikr, and nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key terms used in kalam for the components of a proof: madlul (the implied knowledge), dalil (the evidence), wajh al-dalala (the mode of indication), fikr (thinking), and nazar (inference).

Core Idea
A proof consists of a sought knowledge (madlul), the combination of premises (dalil), and the awareness of how they necessitate (wajh); the process of bringing this to mind is fikr, and the effort to grasp the entailment is nazar.

After explaining the three methods of proof, Ghazali defines the technical terms that describe the anatomy of an argument. These definitions provide a precise vocabulary for discussing rational inference in Islamic theology. Understanding these terms is essential for following kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Notice how Ghazali defines each term in relation to the others. The madlul is the knowledge sought, the dalil is the pairing of two principles, and wajh is the knowledge of how they necessitate.
2
Distinguish fikr from nazar
Fikr is simply bringing the two principles to mind. Nazar is the active seeking to understand the mode of implication.
This distinction is important: nazar requires effort to discover the logical connection.
3
Relate to the example from the previous lesson
In the infinite cycles example, the madlul is the conclusion that cycles are finite. The dalil is the two principles combined. The wajh is understanding why the principles necessitate the conclusion.
Fikr is thinking about the principles, and nazar is the reasoning process.

وإنكار؛ بأن يقول : لا أسلم أنه يلزم ذلك استحاله
والثاني : قولنا هذا اللازم محال ، فإنَّه أيضاً أصل يتصور فيه إنكار ؛ بأن يقول : سلمت الأصل الأول ، ولكن لا أسلم هذا الثاني وهو انقضاء ما لا نهاية له ، ولكن لو أقرَّ بالأصلين .. كان الإقرار بالمعلوم الثالث اللازم منهما واجباً بالضرورة ، وهو الإقرار باستحالة مذهبه المفضي إلى هذا المحال (۱)
فهذه ثلاثة مناهج في الاستدلال جليّة ، لا يتصور إنكار حصول العلم منها ، فالعلم الحاصل المطلوب هو المدلول ، وازدواج الأصلين الملزمين لهذا العلم هو الدليل ، والعلم بوجه لزوم هذا المطلوب من ازدواج الأصلين علم بوجه دلالةِ الدليل ، وفكرك الذي هو عبارة عن إحضارك الأصلين في الذهن ، وطلبكَ التفطن لوجه لزوم العلم وطلبُكَ التفطن لوجه لزوم العلم الثالث من العلمين الأصلين .. هو النظر
(۱) بيان المسألة : دعوى الخصم : دورات الفلك لا متناهية . الأصل الأول : انقضاء ما لا نهاية له بناءً على هذه الدعوى لزوماً
الأصل الثاني : نفي هذا اللازم، وإثبات استحالة انقضاء ما لا نهاية له .
المعلوم الثالث : الإقرار - بعد تسليم الخصم بالأصلين معاً - باستحالة الدعوى ، وبطلان قوله : إن دورات الفلك غير متناهية ، وإثبات ضدها بأنها متناهية ، وهو المطلوب
And denial by saying: I do not concede that such and such is entailed. The second: our saying this entailment is impossible, for this too is a principle in which denial can be conceived, by saying: I concede the first principle, but I do not concede this second one, which is the passing away of what has no end. But if he concedes both principles, then the affirmation of the third knowledge necessarily required from them becomes obligatory, and it is the affirmation of the impossibility of his doctrine that leads to this impossibility. So these are three clear methods of inference, from which it is inconceivable to deny obtaining knowledge. The obtained sought knowledge is the implied (madlul), the pairing of the two principles necessitating this knowledge is the evidence (dalil), and knowledge of the mode of implication of this sought matter from the pairing of the two principles is knowledge of the mode of indication of the evidence. Your thinking (fikr), which is your bringing the two principles to mind, and your seeking to be attentive to the mode of implication of the knowledge and your seeking to be attentive to the mode of implication of the third knowledge from the two original knowledges, is nazar. (1) Explanation of the matter: The opponent's claim: the cycles of the sphere are infinite. The first principle: the passing away of what has no end follows necessarily from this claim. The second principle: the negation of this necessary consequence, and the proof of the impossibility of the passing away of what has no end. The third knowledge: after the opponent concedes both principles together, the acknowledgment of the impossibility of the claim, and the falsity of his statement that the cycles of the sphere are infinite, and the proof of its opposite, that they are finite, and that is the sought matter.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the structure of a proof using a concrete example. Ghazali describes three methods (manahij) of rational inference. The first method is the opponent's denial of the implication: 'I do not concede that such and such is entailed.' The second method is our assertion that the entailment is impossible, but the opponent might deny that impossibility, e.g., the passing away of what has no end. However, if both premises are conceded, the third knowledge necessarily follows: the impossibility of the opponent's doctrine. Ghazali then explicitly states that these three methods are clear and yield necessary knowledge. He then defines the terms: the sought knowledge is the madlul (the implied), the combination of the two principles that entail this knowledge is the dalil (the evidence), and the knowledge of the mode of implication is the mode of indication of the evidence. Finally, he defines fikr (thinking) as bringing the two premises to mind, and the seeking of the mode of implication is nazar. The footnote provides a concrete application: the opponent claims the cycles of the sphere are infinite. The first principle is the necessary consequence: the passing away of what has no end. The second principle denies that consequence as impossible. The third knowledge is then the acknowledgment that the opponent's claim is impossible and that the cycles must be finite. This illustrates how a proper argument works in theology.

دليل
وجه دلالة

نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fikr and nazar are often thought to be synonymous.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes them: fikr is merely bringing premises to mind, while nazar involves actively seeking the logical connection.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is 'nazar'?
A) The sought knowledge
B) The evidence
C) Bringing premises to mind
D) Actively seeking the mode of implication of the third knowledge

Key Takeaway
The technical vocabulary of proof—madlul, dalil, wajh, fikr, and nazar—provides a precise framework for analyzing rational arguments in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your beliefs about Allah, do you actively seek the logical connections (nazar) between premises, or do you merely bring known premises to mind (fikr) and assume the conclusion? Which approach truly strengthens conviction?
?
Ghazali insists that nazar is a deliberate effort. In your current intellectual habits, where do you engage in genuine nazar? Where do you settle for fikr? What might that mean for the depth of your faith?
?
If dalil is the combination of premises, can you name the two premises that lead you to your most fundamental belief about God? If not, are you satisfied with a belief founded on unexamined premises?

The Two Functions of Nazar: Fikr and Talab
Ghazali identifies the two functions of nazar and the resulting definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two complementary functions of nazar (fikr and talab) and how they lead to different definitions of nazar among scholars.

Core Idea
Fikr and talab are complementary, not separate: seeking the implication relies on bringing premises to mind.

In this passage, Ghazali unpacks the act of nazar by identifying its two constituent mental functions: bringing premises to mind (fikr) and seeking the implication (talab). He then shows how different scholars have defined nazar based on which function they emphasize. Understanding this will clarify the terminology used in subsequent discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the two functions Ghazali identifies.
2
Identify the two functions
Locate the definitions of fikr and talab in the passage. See how they are described as bringing premises to mind and seeking the implication.
Fikr is 'bringing the two principles to mind'; talab is 'your aspiration to be attentive to the mode of implication'.
3
Examine the three definitions
Notice how scholars define nazar differently based on focusing on fikr, talab, or both. Understand why each definition is valid from its perspective.
None of the definitions are wrong; they simply emphasize different aspects.
4
Connect to broader methodology
Reflect on why Ghazali presents this analysis. Recognize that nazar is a composite act, and definitions can vary without contradiction.
This sets the stage for a comprehensive definition later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذن؛ عليك في درك العلم المطلوب وظيفتان :
إحداهما : إحضار الأصلين في الذهن ، وهذا يسمى فكراً
والأخرى : تشوفك إلى التفطن لوجه لزوم المطلوب من ازدواج
الأصلين ، وهذا يسمى طلباً
فلذلك قال من جرَّدَ التفاته إلى الوظيفة الأولى حيث أراد حد النظر : إنه الفكر ، وقال من جرد التفاته إلى الوظيفة الثانية في حد النظر : إنَّه طلب علم أو غلبه ظن ، وقال من التفت إلى الأمرين جميعاً : إنه الفكر الذي يطلب به من قام به علماً أو غلبة ظن
Thus, upon you in attaining the sought knowledge are two functions: one: bringing the two principles to mind, and this is called fikr; and the other: your aspiration to be attentive to the mode of implication of the sought matter from the pairing of the two principles, and this is called talab. Therefore, he who restricted his attention to the first function when intending the definition of nazar said: it is fikr. And he who restricted his attention to the second function in the definition of nazar said: it is talab of knowledge or predominant opinion. And he who attended to both matters said: it is the fikr by which one who undertakes it seeks knowledge or predominant opinion.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage derives the definition of nazar from the two mental functions needed to acquire knowledge. Ghazali states that there are two tasks: first, bringing the two foundational premises to mind (ihdar al-aslayn fi al-dhihn), which is called fikr; second, striving to become attentive to the mode of implication of the sought conclusion from the combination of the premises (tashawwuf ila al-tafattun li-wajh luzum al-matlub min izdiwaj al-aslayn), which is called talab. From these two functions, three definitions of nazar arise among the scholars: some define it solely as fikr (the first function), some solely as talab (the second function), and some as the combined activity of fikr that seeks knowledge or predominant opinion. Ghazali thus presents the conceptual basis for the terminological variations he will discuss later.

Key Terms
نظر
فكر
طلب
وظيفتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fikr and talab are separate independent processes.
Correction: They are complementary aspects of the single act of nazar; talab depends on fikr.

Checkpoint
What are the two functions (wazifatan) required to attain the sought knowledge according to Ghazali?
A) Fikr (bringing premises to mind) and talab (seeking the implication).
B) Dalil (evidence) and madlul (the implied).
C) Al-ilm al-awwal (first knowledge) and al-ilm al-thalith (third knowledge).
D) Nazar and istidlal.

Key Takeaway
Nazar is a composite act comprising fikr (bringing premises to mind) and talab (seeking the implication), and different valid definitions arise from emphasizing one or both functions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a new theological issue, do you deliberately bring both revealed texts and rational principles to mind (fikr) before forming a conclusion, or do you rely on one without the other? What would change if you made both functions a habit?
?
The passage speaks of 'aspiration to be attentive to the mode of implication' (talab). Do you truly desire to see the logical connection between premises and conclusions, or are you content with accepting conclusions without understanding the 'how'? How is your faith affected when you neglect this aspiration?
?
Ghazali shows that scholars emphasize different aspects of nazar. Which function do you naturally lean towards—are you more focused on gathering information (fikr) or on actively seeking implications (talab)? How can you intentionally integrate both to deepen your understanding of your creed?

Ghazali's Warning Against Verbosity in Nazar
Ghazali criticizes lengthy writings and emphasizes concise understanding of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's methodological critique of verbose theological writings and his emphasis on concise, effective instruction in nazar.

Core Idea
Conciseness, not verbosity, unlocks true understanding of nazar.

After defining the key terms of dalil, madlul, wajh al-dalala, and nazar, Ghazali delivers a sharp warning against the wasted effort of reading lengthy, repetitive treatises. He argues that only those who have suffered through such books can truly appreciate the value of his succinct presentation. This lesson examines Ghazali's pedagogical conviction that clarity and brevity serve the seeker better than endless elaboration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the contrast between what Ghazali instructs you to understand and what he tells you to leave aside.
The passage is a single sentence in Arabic, but it contains two parts: an instruction and a critique.
2
Identify the four elements of nazar
Ghazali lists four things you should understand: dalil, madlul, wajh al-dalala, and haqiqat al-nazar. Make sure you know what each refers to from previous lessons.
If you are unfamiliar, review the definitions.
3
Analyze the critique
Ghazali criticizes verbose works as 'lengthy expositions and repeated phrases' that fail to satisfy the seeker. Ask yourself why verbosity is harmful according to Ghazali. What does it fail to do?
The key phrase: 'do not quench the thirst of a seeker nor satisfy the craving.'
4
Appreciate the value of conciseness
Ghazali claims that only someone who has wasted time on many books will appreciate his concise words. Reflect on the pedagogical principle: less can be more.
This is a methodological point about effective teaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهكذا ينبغي أن تفهم الدليل والمدلول ، ووجه الدلالة وحقيقة النظر
ودع عنك ما سُوّدت به أوراق كثيرة من تطويلات وترديد عبارات ، لا تشفي غليل طالب ، ولا تسكنُ نَهُمة متعطش ، ولن يعرف قدر هذه الكلمات الوجيزة إلا من انصرف خائباً عن مقصدِهِ بعد مطالعة تصانيف كثيرة .
So thus you ought to understand the evidence (dalil) and the implied (madlul), the mode of indication (wajh al-dalala) and the reality of nazar. And leave aside what many pages have been blackened with of lengthy expositions and repeated phrases, which do not quench the thirst of a seeker nor satisfy the craving of one eager for knowledge. And the value of these concise words will only be appreciated by one who has returned disappointed from his goal after studying many books.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage serves as a concluding remark and a methodological warning. Ghazali first summarizes what the reader should have understood: the essence of dalil, madlul, wajh al-dalala, and the reality of nazar. He then severely criticizes the verbose and repetitive treatments found in many works, saying they fail to satisfy the genuine seeker. He asserts that only someone who has wasted time on voluminous books will appreciate the succinctness of his own presentation. This reflects Ghazali's pedagogical approach: concise clarity over verbosity.

Correction: Ghazali argues that verbose and repetitive writings often fail to satisfy the seeker and that concise words can be more valuable.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali teaches that concise, precise instruction is more effective than lengthy, repetitive expositions in conveying the reality of nazar.

Resolving the Dispute over the Definition of Nazar
Ghazali resolves the dispute over the definition of nazar by prioritizing meaning over terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the apparent disagreement among mutakallimun over the definition of nazar is merely terminological, and that the true method is to first establish meanings, then consider wording.

Core Idea
The dispute over nazar is about words, not meaning; meaning precedes terminology.

Ghazali cuts through the confusion surrounding the definition of nazar by showing that the underlying cognitive process is agreed upon. The different definitions simply highlight different aspects of the same reality. He then gives a fundamental methodological rule: always establish the meaning before arguing about words.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the danger of seeking definitions without understanding
Ghazali begins by warning that if you still search other books for the correct definition, you have missed the point. The passage aims to correct this.
2
Identify the three sciences and two functions
Understand the basic structure: two premises arranged in a specific order produce a third science (conclusion). The two functions are bringing premises to mind and seeing the implication.
3
See the freedom in naming the process
After understanding the reality, you can call nazar fikr, talab, or both—no substantive dispute.
4
Grasp the principle: meaning precedes wording
Most errors come from seeking meanings from words. Always determine the meaning first, then look at terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن راجعت الآن في طلب الصحيح مما قيل في حد النظر . . دلّ ذلك علامة على فهم المراد من النظر على أنَّكَ لم تحظ من هذا الكلام بطائل ، ولم ترجع منه إلى حاصل ، فإنَّك وتأكيد بيانه إذا عرفت أنه ليس ههنا إلا علوم ثلاثة : علمان هما أصلان يرتبانِ ترتيباً مخصوصاً ، وعلم ثالث يلزم منهما ، وليس عليك فيهما إلا وظيفتان ؛ إحداهما : إحضارُ العِلْمين في ذهنك ، والثانية : التفطن لوجه لزوم العلم الثالث منهما ، والخيرة بعد ذلك إليك في إطلاق لفظ النظر في أن تعبر به : عن الفكر الذي هو إحضار العلمين في ذهنك، أو عن التشوف الذي هو طلب التفطن لوجه لزوم العلم الثالث ، أو عن الأمرين جميعاً ؛ فإن العباراتِ مباحة ، والاصطلاحاتِ لا مُشاحَةً فيها
فإن قلت : فغرضي أن أعرف اصطلاحات المتكلمين ، فإنَّهم عبروا تحريجة: مرادي بالنظر عماذا ؟
فاعلم : أنَّك إذا سمعت واحداً يحد النظر بالفكر ، وآخر بالطلب ، وآخر بالفكر الذي يطلب به .. لم تسترب في اختلاف اصطلاحاتهم على ثلاثة أوجه ، والعجب ممن لا يتفطن لهذا ، ويفرضُ الكلام في حد النظر مسألة خلافية ، ويستدل لصحة واحد من الحدود وليس يدري أن حظ المعنى هذه الأمور لا خلاف فيه ، وأن الاصطلاح لا معنى للخلاف معرفة الاصطلاح؛ فما النظر ؟ المعقول من فيه ، وإذا أنت أنعمت النظر واهتديت للسبيل .. عرفت قطعاً أن أكثر الأغاليط تنشأ من ضلالِ مَنْ طلب المعاني من الألفاظ ، ولقد كان من حقه أنْ في أصول البحث : المعنى مقدم على يقرر المعاني أولاً ، ثم ينظر في الألفاظ ثانياً ، ويعلم أنها اصطلاحات المبنى
For if you now refer to seeking the correct among what has been said regarding the definition of nazar, that indicates a lack of understanding of the intended meaning of nazar, and that you have gained no profit from this discourse and have not obtained any result from it. For if you know that here there are only three sciences: two sciences that are principles arranged in a specific order, and a third science that follows from them, and you have only two functions with respect to them: one: bringing the two sciences to your mind, and the second: being attentive to the mode of implication of the third science from them, and the choice thereafter is yours in applying the word nazar: whether you express by it the fikr which is bringing the two sciences to your mind, or the tashawwuf which is seeking to be attentive to the mode of implication of the third science, or both together; for expressions are permissible, and there is no quarrel over terminologies. If you say: my purpose is to know the terminologies of the mutakallimun, for they have expressed [؟]? What is my intended meaning by nazar? Know that if you hear one defining nazar as fikr, another as talab, and another as the fikr by which one seeks, you should not doubt that their terminologies differ in three ways. And astonishing is one who does not perceive this and treats the discussion of the definition of nazar as a matter of controversy, and argues for the correctness of one of the definitions, not knowing that the share of meaning in these matters is not disputed, and that there is no meaning to dispute over terminology. Knowledge of terminology: What then is nazar? The intelligible from it. And if you ponder deeply and find the way, you will definitely know that most errors arise from the straying of one who seeks meanings from words, whereas it was his right, in the principles of research, that meaning precedes [words]: he should first establish meanings, then look at words secondly, and know that they are terminologies of the structure.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage resolves the apparent disagreement about the definition of nazar. Ghazali says that if after his explanation you still refer to other books seeking the correct definition, it shows you have not understood. The reality is simple: there are only three sciences (two premises and a conclusion) and two functions (bringing premises to mind and seeking the implication). After that, you are free to call nazar either fikr, or talab, or both—since all three labels refer to the same underlying reality. The mutakallimun's different definitions are merely conventional (istilahat) and not substantive disagreements. Ghazali then gives a principle: meaning precedes wording. Most errors come from those who try to derive meanings from words rather than first establishing meanings. This is a key methodological principle in Islamic theology: first determine the conceptual reality, then choose appropriate terms.

فكر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different definitions of nazar represent conflicting doctrines about the nature of reasoning.
Correction: The definitions are merely terminological variations; the underlying cognitive process (two premises leading to a conclusion) is agreed upon. The dispute is about which term (fikr, talab, etc.) best describes that process, not about the reality itself.

Checkpoint
What principle does Ghazali state at the end of this passage to resolve disputes?
A) The strongest argument wins.
B) Meaning precedes wording; establish meanings first, then consider terminology.
C) All definitions are equally correct.
D) Only the definition of the early Muslims is valid.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the definition of nazar is not about the reality of reasoning but about which word to use; meaning should always be established before terminology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How often have you argued about words while missing the common ground? This lesson calls you to prioritize the reality over the label. Will you adopt this principle in your own study?
?
Think of a recent dispute you had with someone about a religious issue. Was the disagreement about the concept itself or about the words used? How might the lesson's principle—that meaning precedes wording—have changed the outcome of that conversation? Are you willing to apply it next time?
?

Understanding Dalil, Madlul, and Nazar: The Three Sciences and Two Tasks
Ghazali explains the three sciences and two tasks of nazar, and resolves terminological disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's framework of three sciences (two premises and a conclusion) and the two functions of nazar, and why terminological disputes over its definition are not substantive.

Core Idea
Nazar is a process with two distinct mental acts; disputes about its definition are just differences in labeling.

Ghazali here cuts through the confusion of lengthy scholarly works by presenting a simple, clear model of reasoning. He argues that all reasoning involves three sciences: two premises and a necessary conclusion. The thinker has two tasks: bringing the premises to mind and discerning the implication. Any disagreement about the definition of nazar is merely terminological, not substantive. This lesson breaks down his essential points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice Ghazali's frustration with lengthy works and his promise of a concise explanation.
2
Identify the three sciences and two functions
In the middle of the passage, Ghazali states that there are only three sciences and two functions. Find and underline these. Reflect on what the two principles and the third science represent in reasoning.
The two sciences are the premises, the third is the conclusion. The two functions are bringing premises to mind and discerning the implication.
3
Reflect on terminological flexibility
Ghazali says you may call nazar the thought (bringing premises to mind), the aspiration (discerning implication), or both. Consider why he permits this freedom.
Terms are just labels; the core meaning is agreed.
4
Consider the objection and response
Read the objector's question and Ghazali's reply about the mutakallimun's terminology. Understand why he criticizes those who treat definition as a point of contention.
5
Understand the final warning
Ghazali concludes that most errors come from seeking meanings from words. He advocates establishing meanings first, then considering words as mere terminologies. Reflect on how this principle applies to your own study.

فهكذا ينبغي أن تفهم الدليل والمدلول ، ووجه الدلالة وحقيقة النظر
دع عنك ما سُوّدت به أوراق كثيرة من تطويلات وترديد عبارات ، لا تشفي غليل طالب ، ولا تسكنُ نَهُمة متعطش ، ولن يعرف قدر هذه الكلمات الوجيزة إلا من انصرف خائباً عن مقصدِهِ بعد مطالعة تصانيف كثيرة .
فإن راجعت الآن في طلب الصحيح مما قيل في حد النظر . . دلّ ذلك علامة على فهم المراد من النظر على أنَّكَ لم تحظ من هذا الكلام بطائل ، ولم ترجع منه إلى حاصل .
فإنَّك وتأكيد بيانه إذا عرفت أنه ليس ههنا إلا علوم ثلاثة : علمان هما أصلان يرتبانِ ترتيباً مخصوصاً ، وعلم ثالث يلزم منهما ، وليس عليك فيهما إلا وظيفتان ؛ إحداهما : إحضارُ العِلْمين في ذهنك ، والثانية : التفطن لوجه لزوم العلم الثالث منهما ، والخيرة بعد ذلك إليك في إطلاق لفظ النظر في أن تعبر به : عن الفكر الذي هو إحضار العلمين في ذهنك، أو عن التشوف الذي هو طلب التفطن لوجه لزوم العلم الثالث ، أو عن الأمرين جميعاً ؛ فإن العباراتِ مباحة ، والاصطلاحاتِ لا مُشاحَةً فيها
فإن قلت : فغرضي أن أعرف اصطلاحات المتكلمين ، فإنَّهم عبروا النظر عماذا ؟
فاعلم : أنَّك إذا سمعت واحداً يحد النظر بالفكر ، وآخر بالطلب ، وآخر بالفكر الذي يطلب به .. لم تسترب في اختلاف اصطلاحاتهم على ثلاثة أوجه ، والعجب ممن لا يتفطن لهذا ، ويفرضُ الكلام في حد النظر مسألة خلافية ، ويستدل لصحة واحد من الحدود وليس يدري أن حظ المعنى هذه الأمور لا خلاف فيه ، وأن الاصطلاح لا معنى للخلاف
معرفة الاصطلاح؛
فما النظر ؟
المعقول من فيه ، وإذا أنت أنعمت النظر واهتديت للسبيل .. عرفت قطعاً أن أكثر الأغاليط تنشأ من ضلالِ مَنْ طلب المعاني من الألفاظ ، ولقد كان من حقه أنْ في أصول البحث : المعنى مقدم على يقرر المعاني أولاً ، ثم ينظر في الألفاظ ثانياً ، ويعلم أنها اصطلاحات
لا تتغير بها المعقولات
Thus you should understand the proof (dalil) and the proven (madlul), and the aspect of indication (wajh al-dalala) and the reality of theoretical reasoning (nazar). Leave aside what many papers have been blackened with of lengthy expansions and repeated phrases that neither quench the thirst of a seeker nor satisfy the craving of one athirst. Only he who has turned back disappointed from his aim after studying many books will know the value of these concise words. For if you now consult in seeking the correct [definition] among what has been said concerning the definition of nazar, that would indicate a sign of understanding the intended meaning of nazar, provided you have not obtained from this speech any profit nor returned from it to any result. For you, and to confirm its clarification, if you know that there are only three sciences: two sciences that are two principles arranged in a particular order, and a third science that necessarily follows from them; and you have only two functions regarding them: one is bringing the two sciences to your mind, and the second is discerning the aspect of the necessary implication of the third science from them. After that, the choice is yours in applying the term 'nazar': whether you express by it the thought (fikr) which is bringing the two sciences to your mind, or the aspiration (tashawwuf) which is the seeking to discern the aspect of necessary implication of the third science, or both matters together; for expressions are permissible, and terminologies are not to be disputed. If you say: 'My purpose is to know the terminologies of the mutakallimun, for they expressed nazar by what?' Then know: if you hear one defining nazar as thought, another as seeking, and another as the thought by which one seeks, you will not doubt the variation of their terminologies in three aspects. And amazing is one who does not discern this and takes speech concerning the definition of nazar as a controversial issue and argues for the correctness of one of the definitions, not knowing that the share of meaning in these matters is not disagreed upon, and that terminology has no meaning for disagreement. Knowledge of terminology; so what is nazar? The intelligible from it. And if you reflect deeply and are guided to the path, you will know for certain that most fallacies arise from the misguidance of one who seeks meanings from words. Indeed it was proper that in the principles of research, meaning takes precedence over [the wording?], so that meanings are established first, then one considers words secondarily, and knows that they are terminologies that do not change intelligibles.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a methodological discussion on the nature of proof (dalil), proven (madlul), and theoretical reasoning (nazar). Ghazali begins by emphasizing the correct way to understand these concepts, criticizing lengthy and repetitive works. He then asserts that in reasoning there are only three sciences: two principles (the premises) arranged in a specific order, and a third science that necessarily follows from them (the conclusion). The thinker has two tasks: first, to bring the two premises to mind, and second, to discern how the conclusion necessarily follows from them. Ghazali then grants freedom in terminology: one may call 'nazar' the thought that brings the premises to mind, or the seeking of the implication, or both. This is because terminological disputes are not substantive. An objector then asks to know the specific terminology of the mutakallimun. Ghazali replies that one will hear different definitions (thought, seeking, etc.) but these are merely variations in terminology, not genuine disagreements. He warns that those who treat the definition of nazar as a point of contention are mistaken, as the underlying meaning is agreed upon. Finally, Ghazali stresses that most errors come from seeking meanings from words, whereas the proper method is to first establish meanings and then consider words as mere terminological labels that do not change the intelligibles.

Key Terms
دليل
مدلول
نظر
فكر
علم
اصطلاح
وظيفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disagreements over the definition of nazar are substantive theological disputes.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that these are merely differences in terminology; the underlying meaning is agreed upon.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how many sciences are there in the process of reasoning?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The essence of nazar is the mental process of combining two premises to deduce a conclusion; labels are secondary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological disagreement, do you instinctively assume a right and wrong side, or do you first ask, 'Could this be merely a difference in terminology?' How can you train yourself to follow Ghazali's advice to seek the meaning first?
?
Ghazali says most fallacies arise from seeking meanings from words. Think of a time you misunderstood a religious concept because you got attached to a label. What did you learn from that experience, and what can you do differently now?
?
If you had to explain the essence of nazar to a fellow Muslim who is confused by conflicting definitions, what would you say is the one core reality behind all the labels? Does your own practice of reasoning in faith reflect this understanding? Commit to one change today.

The Six Sources of Premises: Sensory and Pure Rational
Ghazali lists the six sources of premises and explains the first two: sensory and pure rational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two sources of premises in kalam—sensory perception and pure reason—and how they compel an opponent's acceptance.

Core Idea
Kalam arguments rest on premises that are either sensed or rationally self-evident.

Imam al-Ghazali anticipates a key question: even if a conclusion follows from two premises, what forces the opponent to accept those premises? His answer reveals the foundations of all theological argumentation. In this passage, he enumerates six sources of premises, then explains the first two in detail—sensory observation and pure rational necessity. Understanding these sources equips you to evaluate how any theological claim can be justified.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ghazali's response
Start by reading the objection carefully: the opponent concedes logic but questions the source of the premises. Then read Ghazali's answer that premises come from six sources.
Notice that Ghazali immediately narrows the scope to six specific sources used in this book.
2
Examine the first source: sensory perception
Identify the definition: al-hissiyat includes both outward observation (e.g., animals, clouds, sounds) and inward observation (e.g., pain, joy). Ghazali argues these are undeniable because they are directly perceived.
He emphasizes that even if one imagines change (transfer), the change itself is contingent and observed.
3
Examine the second source: pure reason
Pure rational judgments are those like 'the world is either contingent or eternal'—a self-evident division. Ghazali gives an example argument using the premise that whatever does not precede a contingent is itself contingent.
This source relies on logical necessity that every rational person must accept.
4
Reflect on the overall structure
Notice that Ghazali does not finish listing all six sources here; he only details the first two. Consider why he starts with these—they are the most fundamental and least controversial.
The remaining four sources (tawatur, etc.) appear later in the book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : إني لا أستريبُ في لزوم صحة الدعوى من هذين الأصلين إذا أقرَّ الخصم بهما على هذا الوجه ، ولكن من أين يجب على الخصم الإقرار بهما ؟ ومن أين تقتنص هذه الأصول المسلمة الواجبة التسليم ؟
فاعلم : أن لها مدارك شتى ، ولكن الذي نستعمله في هذا الكتاب نجتهد ألا يعدو ستة مدارك :
الأول : الحسيات : أعني المُدركَ بالمشاهدة الظاهرة أو الباطنة
مثاله : أنا إذا قلنا مثلاً : كل حادث فله سببٌ ، وفي العالم حوادث ؛ فلا بد لها من سبب .. فقولنا : ( في العالم حوادث ) أصل واحد يجب على الخصم الإقرار به ؛ فإنه يُدرك بالمشاهدة الظاهرة حدوث أشخاص الحيوانات والنبات والغيوم والأمطار، ومن الأعراض الأصوات والألوان ، وإن تخيّل أنَّها منتقلةٌ .. فالانتقال حادث ، ونحن لم ندَّع إلا حادثاً ما ، ولم نعين أن ذلك الحادثَ جوهر أو عرض أو انتقال أو غيره ، وكذلك يعلمُ بالمشاهدة الباطنة حدوث الآلام والأفراح والغموم في قلبه وبدنه ، فلا يمكنه إنكاره .
الثاني : العقلي المحضُ : فإنا إذا قلنا : العالم : إما حادث ، وإما قديم ، وليس وراء القسمين قسم ثالثُ . وجب الاعتراف به على كل عاقل
مثاله : أنَّا نقولُ : كلُّ ما لا يسبق الحادث فهو حادث ، والعالم لا يسبق الحادث ؛ فهو حادث ، فأحد الأصلين قولنا : إن ما لا يسبق الحادث فهو حادث
، ويجب على الخصم الإقرار به ؛ لأن ما لا يسبق الحادث : إما أن
If you say: 'I do not doubt the necessity of the correctness of the claim from these two principles when the opponent admits them in this manner, but from where must the opponent admit them? And from where are these conceded and obligatory premises to be extracted?' Know: they have various sources, but that which we use in this book, we strive to not exceed six sources: First: the sensory (al-hissiyat), meaning that which is perceived by outward or inward observation. Example: If we say, for instance, 'Every contingent has a cause, and in the world there are contingents; thus they must have a cause'—our statement 'there are contingents in the world' is a single premise that the opponent must admit, for it is perceived by outward observation the occurrence of individuals of animals, plants, clouds, and rains, and among accidents, sounds and colors; and if he imagines that they are transferred, then the transfer is contingent, and we have only claimed some contingency and have not specified whether that contingency is a substance, accident, transfer, or other. Likewise, by inward observation, he knows the occurrence of pains, joys, and griefs in his heart and body, so he cannot deny it. Second: the pure rational (al-'aqli al-mahd): For if we say, 'The world is either contingent or eternal, and there is no third division beyond the two divisions,' acknowledgment of this is obligatory upon every rational being. Example: We say, 'Everything that does not precede a contingent is itself contingent, and the world does not precede a contingent; therefore it is contingent.' One of the two principles is our statement that 'whatever does not precede a contingent is itself contingent,' and the opponent must admit it, because that which does not precede a contingent is either...
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair concerning the sources of premises. The objector concedes that if the opponent accepts the two premises, the conclusion necessarily follows, but asks how the opponent can be compelled to accept those premises in the first place. Ghazali responds by enumerating six sources (madarik) of premises, though the text only covers the first two in detail. The first source is sensory perception (al-hissiyat), both external (e.g., observing animals, plants, clouds, sounds, colors) and internal (e.g., feeling pain, joy, grief). These are undeniable because they are directly perceived. The second source is pure reason (al-'aqli al-mahd), which includes self-evident rational divisions such as the world being either contingent or eternal, with no third option. As an example, Ghazali gives the argument that whatever does not precede a contingent is itself contingent—a premise that every rational person must accept. The text breaks off mid-explanation of the second source, but the intended enumeration continues with four more sources in the original work.

عقلي
مشاهدة

قديم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all premises in kalam arguments are purely rational and independent of observation.
Correction: Ghazali shows that sensory perception is also a valid source of premises; some premises are based on direct observation (e.g., existence of contingents in the world).

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a premise derived from sensory perception according to Ghazali?
A) The world is either eternal or contingent.
B) Every contingent has a cause.
C) There are contingents in the world.
D) Whatever does not precede a contingent is itself contingent.

Key Takeaway
Sensory observation and pure reason are the first two fundamental sources of premises in kalam, each providing undeniable grounds for argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the constant changes in your own life — your body aging, your moods shifting, your circumstances evolving — do you genuinely see them as contingents demanding a necessary cause, or do you take them for granted? How would your daily awareness change if you started treating every change as a sign that must point to the Creator?
?
Ghazali says that the premise 'whatever does not precede a contingent is itself contingent' is undeniable for any rational person. Yet many people live as if they are self-sufficient, ignoring their own contingency. Where in your heart do you feel a resistance to fully accepting that you are a contingent being, and what hold does that resistance have on your soul?
?
Think of a recent moment of intense joy or deep pain. Did you recognize it as a 'contingent' event that should draw you toward reflection on the Originator, or did it remain merely an emotion? What concrete step can you take this week to view your inner experiences as evidence for the truth of tawhid?

The Six Sources of Theological Principles
Ghazali responds to an objection by announcing six sources for principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali introduces six sources for theological principles and how he responds to a possible objection.

Core Idea
Theological arguments rest on principles whose sources are limited to six.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a key methodological question: how can we compel an opponent to accept the foundational principles of theological argumentation? He responds by explaining that these principles have various sources, and in this book, he will rely on exactly six reliable sources. This sets the stage for the enumeration that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection begins with 'تحريجة' and questions the basis of the two principles. Notice the phrasing: 'من أين يجب على الخصم الإقرار بذلك؟' (From where is it obligatory for the opponent to acknowledge that?).
The objector accepts the logical validity but asks about the foundation.
2
Identify the answer
Ghazali answers with 'فاعلم' (then know) and states that the principles have various sources (مدارك شتى). He specifies that in this book, he will use no more than six reliable sources.
This is a methodological commitment.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why limiting to six sources is important. It provides a clear framework for theological debate, ensuring that arguments are grounded on agreed-upon foundations.
This reflects Ghazali's systematic approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحريجة : فما وجه فإن قلت : إني لا أستريبُ في لزوم صحة الدعوى من هذين الأصلين إذا إلزام الخصم وسيل أقرَّ الخصم بهما على هذا الوجه ، ولكن من أين يجب على الخصم الإقرار ذلك ؟ بهما ؟ ومن أين تقتنص هذه الأصول المسلمة الواجبة التسليم ؟ فاعلم : أن لها مدارك شتى ، ولكن الذي نستعمله في هذا الكتاب المدارك المعتمدة في نجتهد ألا يعدو ستة مدارك :
An objection: So what is the aspect? For you said: I do not doubt the necessity of the validity of the claim from these two principles, if the opponent is compelled through the means [when] the opponent acknowledges them in this manner. But from where is it obligatory for the opponent to acknowledge that? [Acknowledgment] of them? And from where are these principles, which must be accepted, derived? Then know: that they have various sources, but that which we use in this book are the reliable sources; we strive that they do not exceed six sources:
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection (تحريجة) that questions the basis of the two principles used in argumentation. The objector acknowledges that if the opponent concedes the two principles, the claim is valid, but asks how we can compel the opponent to acknowledge those principles in the first place. The answer, introduced by 'فاعلم' (then know), states that these principles have various sources (مدارك شتى), but the method used in this book relies on reliable sources, which are limited to six. The passage thus sets up the enumeration of the six sources that follow. The word 'تحريجة' seems to be a heading for the objection, and the response is concise: there are six sources, each with their own basis for compelling acknowledgment.

Key Terms
مدارك (sources)
أصول (principles)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a denial of the principles themselves.
Correction: The objection is not a denial but a request for the foundation of the principles; the objector accepts their logical necessity if conceded.

B) Five

D) Ten

Key Takeaway
Ghazali establishes that theological principles are derived from a limited set of six reliable sources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm a belief about God, do you know the source from which that belief came? If not, how can you be certain it is reliable? This lesson challenges you to trace every conviction back to one of the six sources—or else admit you may be building on sand.
?
Ghazali deliberately limits his arguments to six sources to ensure reliability. Are you equally careful in choosing what you accept as authoritative, or do you allow hearsay, emotions, or culture to dictate your theology? Today, examine one belief: what is its source?
?
The objector demands the foundation of the principles. If someone asked you 'On what basis do you believe that?', could you point to a clear source from this list? Or would you be embarrassed? Commit today to knowing the source of your most cherished theological claim.

The First Source: Sensory Perception
Ghazali defines and exemplifies the first source of knowledge: sensory perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defines sensory perception as a source of knowledge, encompassing both external and internal observation, and why it is foundational for theological arguments.

Core Idea
Sensory perception is undeniable and forms the bedrock of certain knowledge.

Imam Ghazali begins his epistemological framework by establishing the most basic source of knowledge: sensory perception. He explains that this includes not only what we see and hear externally but also what we feel internally. This foundational principle is crucial for the proof that every originated thing requires a cause. Let's read his definition and example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice how Ghazali defines 'sensory perceptions' broadly.
The passage uses both terms: المشاهدة الظاهرة (external observation) and المشاهدة الباطنة (internal observation).
2
Identify the two types of sensory perception
Find where Ghazali distinguishes external and internal observation. List examples he gives for each type.
External: animals, plants, clouds, rain, sounds, colors. Internal: pains, joys, griefs.
3
Understand the example argument
Ghazali uses the premise 'in the world there are originated things' as a principle established by sensory perception. See how he defends this against the objection of transfer (intiqāl).
Even if one claims things are transferred, the transfer itself is an originated event, so the premise holds.
4
Reflect on the undeniability
Why does Ghazali say the opponent cannot deny this sensory evidence? Consider the role of direct experience.
Sensory perception is immediate and undeniable, making it a solid foundation for further reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : الحسيات : أعني المُدركَ بالمشاهدة الظاهرة أو الباطنة . مثاله : أنا إذا قلنا مثلاً : كل حادث فله سببٌ ، وفي العالم حوادث ؛ فلا بد لها من سبب .. فقولنا : ( في العالم حوادث ) أصل واحد يجب على الخصم الإقرار به ؛ فإنه يُدرك بالمشاهدة الظاهرة حدوث أشخاص الحيوانات والنبات والغيوم والأمطار، ومن الأعراض الأصوات والألوان ، وإن تخيّل أنَّها منتقلةٌ .. فالانتقال حادث ، ونحن لم ندَّع إلا حادثاً ما ، ولم نعين أن ذلك الحادثَ جوهر أو عرض أو انتقال أو غيره ، وكذلك يعلمُ بالمشاهدة الباطنة حدوث الآلام والأفراح والغموم في قلبه وبدنه ، فلا يمكنه إنكاره .
The first: sensory perceptions (al-ḥissiyyāt): I mean that which is perceived by external or internal observation. Its example: when we say, for example: every originated thing (ḥādith) has a cause, and in the world there are originated things; so they must have a cause. So our statement: 'in the world there are originated things' is one principle that must be acknowledged by the opponent, for it is perceived by external observation: the origination of individual animals, plants, clouds, and rains, and among accidents, sounds and colors. And if he imagines that they are transferred, then the transfer is originated (ḥādith). And we only claim some originated thing, and we do not specify whether that originated thing is a substance, accident, transfer, or other. Likewise, it is known by internal observation the origination of pains, joys, and griefs in his heart and body, so he cannot deny it.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage defines the first source: sensory perceptions (الحسيات). Ghazali explains that this includes both external observation (المشاهدة الظاهرة) and internal observation (المشاهدة الباطنة). He provides an example from the proof for the existence of a cause for originated things. The principle 'in the world there are originated things' is established by external observation of the origination of animals, plants, clouds, rain, and accidents like sounds and colors. Even if one imagines these to be merely transferred occurrences, the transfer itself is an originated event. Importantly, Ghazali notes that the claim is only that some originated thing exists, without specifying its category. Internal observation also confirms origination of pains, joys, and griefs. This sensory evidence is so undeniable that the opponent cannot deny it. Thus, this source provides a foundational principle that must be acknowledged.

Key Terms
الحسيات (sensory perceptions)
المشاهدة الظاهرة (external sight)
المشاهدة الباطنة (inner sight)
حادث (originated)
سبب (cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory perception only refers to external senses like sight and hearing.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly includes internal observation (المشاهدة الباطنة) such as feelings of pain, joy, and grief as part of sensory perception.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali give as an example of something perceived by internal observation?
A) Colors and sounds
B) Pains, joys, and griefs
C) Clouds and rain
D) Substances and accidents

Key Takeaway
Ghazali establishes that sensory perception—both external and internal—provides undeniable evidence for the existence of originated things, forming the first foundation of theological knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You cannot escape the reality of your own grief or joy—they are undeniably originated within you. When have you tried to rationalize away a deep inner feeling, treating it as unreal or merely transferred from elsewhere? How does Ghazali's insistence that the feeling itself is a fresh originated event force you to stop running and face it head-on?
?
If sensory perception—both external and internal—is the bedrock of knowledge, then your own body and heart are witnesses to truth. What specific originated thing in your life today (a habit, a pain, a longing) are you ignoring because it's uncomfortable? Will you acknowledge it as a real originated thing that demands a cause?
?
The opponent in Ghazali's example tries to deny origination by claiming things are merely transferred. In your spiritual life, do you ever dismiss clear signs of change (like a growing doubt or a sudden conviction) as just 'transferred' from elsewhere—something old, not new? What would it cost you to admit that this very moment contains something genuinely new from your Lord?

Fourth Source: Reasoning by Syllogism from Other Principles
Ghazali defines the fourth source: a principle established by another syllogism that traces back to basic sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali allows a principle established by another syllogism to serve as a source for further reasoning, as long as it traces back to basic sources.

Core Idea
Derived principles can become new starting points if they trace back to senses, reason, or mutawatir.

Ghazali introduces the fourth source of theological reasoning: syllogisms that rely on principles themselves proven by earlier syllogisms. This creates chains of reasoning that ultimately anchor in the three basic sources. In this passage, he defines this source and gives a partial example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Ghazali defines the fourth source and mentions its reliance on prior syllogisms.
The example is cut off, but the definition is complete.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms أصل (principle), فرع (branch), and قياس (syllogism). Understand how a branch can become a principle in a new syllogism.
These terms are central to Ghazali's methodology.
3
Understand the chain of reasoning
Grasp that the fourth source allows intermediate conclusions to serve as starting points for new proofs, as long as they ultimately trace back to sensory, rational, or mutawatir sources.
This prevents infinite regress by grounding all reasoning in basic sources.

Source Text

This passage defines the fourth source: a principle that is itself established by a syllogism (قياس) that traces back, either directly or through multiple steps, to the three basic sources: sensory perceptions, rational truths, or mutawātir reports. The Fourth Type of Premise: Derived from a Previous Syllogism
Ghazali explains premises established by a prior syllogism used as new premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a premise can be established by a prior syllogism and then used as a building block in further reasoning, as demonstrated by the example of the world's origination leading to the existence of a cause.

Core Idea
Proven conclusions become new premises for deeper arguments.

In this passage, Ghazali introduces the fourth type of premise: one that is itself derived from a previous syllogism. This shows that theological arguments are not isolated; they build upon each other. The example of proving the world's temporality then using it to prove a cause illustrates this chain of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ما هو فرع لأصلين يمكن أن يجعل أصلاً في قياس آخر'.
2
Identify the definition of the fourth premise type
Locate the sentence defining the fourth type. What does it mean for a premise to be established by another syllogism?
The definition includes the idea of degrees (درجات) and foundations (حسيات، عقليات، متواترات).
3
Examine the example
Look at how the world's origination is used as a premise in a new syllogism. What is the major premise? What is the conclusion?
The example is: after proving the world is originated, we say 'every originated thing has a cause; the world is originated; therefore it has a cause.'
4
Reflect on the cumulative nature of arguments
Consider why this method is important in theology. How does it allow building a case step by step?
Think about how this prevents opponents from rejecting conclusions once premises are established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: أن يكون الأصل مثبتاً بقياس آخر يستند بدرجة واحدة أو درجات كثيرة؛ إما إلى الحسيات، أو العقليات، أو المتواترات: فإن ما هو فرع لأصلين يمكن أن يجعل أصلاً في قياس آخر. مثاله: أنا بعد أن نفرغ من الدليل على حدث العالم يمكننا أن نجعل أصلاً حدث العالم والعالم حادث؛ فإذن له سبب، فلا يمكنهم إنكار كون العالم حادثاً بعد أن أثبتناه بالدليل في نظم قياس مثل أن نقول: كل حادث فله سبب، أثبتناه بالدليل.
The fourth: that the premise be established by another syllogism, which relies on one degree or many degrees, whether on sense-perceptions, rational principles, or mutawatirat. For what is a branch of two premises can be made a premise in another syllogism. Its example: after we finish the proof of the world's temporal origination, we can take as a premise the world's origination and that the world is originated; so it has a cause, so they cannot deny that the world is originated after we have proven it by the proof, in the arrangement of a syllogism such as saying: every originated thing has a cause—we have proven it by the proof.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage describes the fourth type of premise: one that is established by a previous syllogism. Such a premise can be based on sense, reason, or recurrent transmission, and it can be used as a building block in further arguments. The example: after proving that the world is originated, we can then take that conclusion and use it as a premise in a new syllogism: 'Every originated thing has a cause; the world is originated; therefore it has a cause.' This shows how a proven conclusion can become a premise for further reasoning.

Key Terms
قياس (syllogism)
أصل (premise)
حدث العالم (origination of the world)
حادث (originated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only axioms or self-evident truths can serve as premises in theological arguments.
Correction: Ghazali shows that conclusions from valid syllogisms can also be used as premises, as long as they are properly established by prior reasoning.

Key Takeaway
A conclusion from one syllogism can serve as a premise for another, allowing arguments to be built step by step.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Sixth Type of Premise: From the Opponent's Beliefs
Ghazali explains using premises accepted by the opponent as a dialectical tool.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how premises can be taken from the opponent's own beliefs and used in syllogistic argumentation, even without independent proof.

Core Idea
Use your opponent's own beliefs as premises to build an argument.

Imam Ghazali introduces the sixth type of premise in dialectical reasoning: premises derived from the opponent's accepted doctrines. This strategy allows you to argue effectively even when you lack independent empirical or rational proof. The key is that the opponent cannot deny such a premise without contradicting his own position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Ghazali's description of the sixth type of premise. Notice how he defines it and explains its advantage.
2
Understand the dialectical value
Ask yourself: Why is it beneficial to use a premise from the opponent's belief? What does Ghazali say about denial?
Denial would demolish the opponent's own doctrine.
3
Consider the scope
Ghazali notes that examples are many. Think about real or hypothetical situations where you could use this technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: أن يكون الأصل مأخوذاً من معتقد الخصم ومسلماته: فإنه وإن لم يقم لنا عليه دليل، ولم يكن حسياً، ولا عقلياً، انتفعنا باتخاذه أصلاً في قياسنا، وامتنع عليه الإنكار؛ لأن الإنكار هادم لمذهبه، وأمثلة هذا مما يكثر؛ فلا حاجة إلى تعيينه.
The sixth: that the premise be taken from the opponent's belief and his accepted doctrines. For if no proof has been established for us for it, nor is it sensory or rational, we benefit by adopting it as a premise in our syllogism, and denial becomes impossible for him, because denial would demolish his own doctrine. Examples of this are many; so there is no need to specify them.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth type of premise: one taken from the opponent's own beliefs and accepted principles (musallamat). Even if we have no independent proof for that premise, and it is neither empirical nor rational, we can still use it in a syllogism because the opponent cannot deny it without contradicting his own position. Denying it would undermine his own doctrine. Ghazali notes that examples are plentiful and thus does not list them.

Key Terms
معتقد الخصم (opponent's belief)
مسلمات (accepted doctrines)
قياس (syllogism)
هاد لمذهبه (demolishes his doctrine)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the advantage of using a premise taken from the opponent's own beliefs?
A) It is always rationally proven
B) The opponent cannot deny it without contradicting his own doctrine
C) It is sensory knowledge
D) It is based on consensus

Key Takeaway
Using premises accepted by the opponent enables effective dialectical argument even without independent proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Fifth Source: Scriptural Proofs (Sam'iyyat)
Ghazali defines sam'iyyat and gives a syllogistic example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of sam'iyyat and how it is used in a syllogism that combines a sensory fact with a scriptural principle.

Core Idea
Scriptural proofs rely on sensory facts plus a revealed principle, not on pure reason alone.

In this passage, Ghazali introduces the fifth source of proof in kalam: scriptural proofs (sam'iyyat). He illustrates with the example of proving that sins occur by God's will. The argument combines a sensory observation (sins happen) with a scripturally grounded principle (everything that happens is by God's will). This shows how revealed texts can be used deductively in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two premises
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Identify the major premise (everything that occurs is by God's will) and the minor premise (sins occur).
Notice that the major premise is scriptural, and the minor premise is sensory.
2
Understand the role of consensus (ijma')
Ghazali argues that if an opponent denies the scriptural premise, we can defend it by citing the consensus of the Muslim community on the saying 'What God wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen.' This shows how consensus can establish a scriptural principle.
Consensus is itself a scriptural proof.
3
Recognize the epistemological distinction
Note how Ghazali distinguishes between knowledge by sense (that sins occur) and knowledge by Sharia (that they are sins). The syllogism marries these two types of knowledge.
This is a deliberate methodological move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : السمعياتُ : مثاله : أنا ندعي مثلاً أن المعاصي بمشيئة الله ؛ فنقول : كلُّ كائن فهو بمشيئة الله ، والمعاصي كائنة ؛ فهي إذن بمشيئة الله. فأما قولنا : هي كائنة فمعلوم وجودها بالحس ، وكونها معصية معلوم بالشرع. وأما قولنا : كلُّ كائن بمشيئة الله فإذا أنكر الخصم ذلك مهما كان مقراً بالشرع أو كان قد أثبت عليه بالدليل فإنا نثبت هذا الأصل بإجماع الأمة على صدق قول القائل : « ما شاء الله كان، وما لم يشأ لم يكن » فيكون السمع مانعاً من الإنكار.
The fifth: scriptural proofs: its example: we claim, for instance, that sins are by God's will; so we say: everything that occurs is by God's will, and sins occur; therefore they are by God's will. As for our statement 'they occur' – their existence is known by sense, and their being sins is known by the Sharia. And as for our statement 'everything that occurs is by God's will' – if the opponent denies that, whenever he is affirming the Sharia or the Sharia has been established for him by proof, then we establish this principle by the consensus of the community on the truthfulness of the saying: 'What God wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen' so the scripture prevents (him) from denial.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage classifies the fifth source (sam'iyyat) for constructing proofs. Ghazali gives an example: claiming that sins are by God's will. The syllogism has two premises: 'Everything that occurs is by God's will' (a scriptural principle) and 'Sins occur' (sensory fact). The opponent might deny the first premise. Ghazali shows how to defend it: if the opponent accepts the Sharia, or if the Sharia has been proven to him, we use the consensus of the ummah on the truth of the prophetic saying 'What God wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen' as evidence. Thus scripture itself prevents the opponent from denying that principle.

Key Terms
السمعيات
المشيئة
إجماع الأمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Scriptural proofs are purely based on texts and do not involve empirical observation.
Correction: Ghazali's example shows that scriptural proofs combine a revealed principle (from scripture) with a sensory fact (that sins occur). Thus, they are not purely textual but also incorporate empirical evidence.

Checkpoint
What two things are combined in the example of sam'iyyat to conclude that sins occur by God's will?
A) A sensory fact and a scriptural principle
B) Two sensory facts
C) Two rational axioms
D) A scriptural fact and a rational proof

Key Takeaway
Scriptural proofs are not merely textual; they combine empirical observation with revealed principles, making them a bridge between reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that it occurred by God's will? How does that belief change the way you view your own sinfulness and your repentance? Will you commit to acknowledging both that God's will prevails and that you are accountable?
?
The lesson shows that scriptural proofs combine empirical observation with revelation. In your own life, do you treat scripture as disconnected from your everyday experience? What one step can you take today to align your observation of the world with the truth of revelation?
?
The hadith 'ما شاء الله كان، وما لم يشأ لم يكن' is used as a proof. Do you live as if this is true, worrying less about outcomes? Or do you still struggle with control? Will you trust God's will more deeply today?

The Sixth Source: Opponent's Own Beliefs (Musallimat)
Ghazali describes using the opponent's own accepted premises as a source for proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the opponent's own accepted premises as a valid source for argumentation in kalam, even without independent proof.

Core Idea
Your own beliefs can become the proof against you.

In this passage, Ghazali introduces a clever dialectical method: instead of proving a premise yourself, you can borrow it from your opponent's own creed. This is not a weakness but a strategic tool for debate. The opponent cannot reject it without undermining his entire position. Let's see how this works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, then the English translation. Notice that Ghazali says the premise is taken from the opponent's own 'creed and accepted principles' (ma'taqad al-khasm wa musallimatihi).
The term 'musallimat' refers to things the opponent accepts as true.
2
Understand the logical condition
Ghazali explains that we may lack independent proof for this premise, and it may not be based on sense or reason. Yet we can still use it because the opponent already accepts it.
3
Reflect on the dialectical benefit
The opponent cannot deny the premise without contradicting his own doctrine. Denial would 'destroy his own madhhab,' making the argument effective and irrefutable from within his own framework.
This method is common in inter-sectarian debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : أن يكون الأصل مأخوذاً من معتقد الخصم ومسلماته : فإنه وإن لم يقم لنا عليه دليل ، ولم يكن حسياً ، ولا عقلياً .. انتفعنا باتخاذه أصلاً في قياسنا ، وامتنع عليه الإنكار ؛ لأنَّ الإنكار هادم لمذهبه ، وأمثلة هذا مما يكثرُ ؛ فلا حاجة إلى تعيينه.
The sixth: that the original (premise) be taken from the opponent's creed and his accepted principles. For even if we have no proof for it, and it is not sensory nor rational, we benefit by taking it as a premise in our syllogism, and denial is prevented from him because denial would destroy his own doctrine. Examples of this are many, so there is no need to specify them.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth source: using the opponent's own beliefs and accepted premises (musallimat) as the original or major premise in a qiyas (syllogism). Even if we do not independently have a proof for that premise, and it is not known by sense or reason, we can adopt it as a premise in our argument. The opponent cannot deny it without contradicting his own doctrine. Ghazali notes that examples are numerous, so he does not list them.

Key Terms
معتقد الخصم
مسلمات
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think using the opponent's premises is a logical fallacy or a sign of weakness.
Correction: It is a valid dialectical method for refutation from the opponent's own principles, not a fallacy. It is a strategic tool widely used in Islamic theology.

Checkpoint
