A) Because it is proven by sense
B) Because it is rationally necessary
C) Because it would destroy his own doctrine
D) Because it is from the Quran

Key Takeaway
The opponent's own accepted principles can serve as a valid premise in argumentation, preventing denial without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Scope of Different Sources in Theological Argument
Ghazali enumerates the six sources and their conditions of utility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the six sources of knowledge vary in their applicability in theological arguments, and why one must tailor proofs to an opponent's accepted sources.

Core Idea
Not all sources are universal; arguments must fit the audience.

Ghazali now addresses a practical question: Are all sources equally useful when constructing a syllogism for a specific opponent? He explains that each source has a scope of benefit. Rational and sensory proofs work for almost everyone, but even they have limits. Transmitted reports, analogical premises, school-specific axioms, and scriptural proofs all depend on the interlocutor's situation. This passage reveals the dialectical sensitivity required in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a question followed by an answer that systematically covers each source.
The passage uses a question-answer format common in kalam texts.
2
Identify each source and its condition
As you read, list each of the six sources mentioned. For each source, note the specific condition that limits its usefulness according to Ghazali. Compare the conditions.
The sources appear in this order: rational/sensory, mutawatir, qiyas-derived, musallimat, sam'iyyat.
3
Reflect on the dialectical implication
Ask yourself: Why does Ghazali emphasize these distinctions? How does this affect how a theologian should argue? Consider how you might adapt your proofs for different audiences.
This passage shows that theological reasoning is not one-size-fits-all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : فهل من فرق بين هذه المداركِ في الانتفاع بها في المقاييس النظرية ؟ فاعلم : أنها متفاوتة في عموم الفائدة ، فإنَّ المدارك العقلية والحسية عامة مع كافة الخلق ، إلا من لا عقل له ، أو لا حس له وكان الأصل معلوماً بالحس الذي فقده ؛ كالأصل المعلوم بحاسة البصر إذا استعمل مع الأكمه .. فإنه لا ينفع ، والأكمه إذا كان هو الناظر .. لم يمكنه أن يتخذ ذلك أصلاً ، وكذلك المسموع في حق الأصم . فأما المتواتر .. فإنَّه نافع ، ولكن في حقٌّ من تواتر إليه ، فمن وصل إلينا من مكان بعيد لم تبلغه الدعوة ، وأردنا أن نبينَ له بالتواتر : أنَّ محمداً صلى الله عليه وسلم تحدَّى بالقرآن .. لم يُقدر عليه ما لم نمهلهُ مدَّةً حتى يتواتر عنده ، وربَّ شيء يتواتر عند قوم دون قوم . فقولُ أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه في مسألة قتل المسلم بالذمي متواتر عند الفقهاء من أصحابه دون العوام من المقلدين ، وكم من مذاهب له في آحاد المسائل لا تتواتر عند أكثر الفقهاء . وأما الأصل المستفاد من قياس آخر .. فلا ينفعُ إلا مع من قُرَّر معه ذلك القياس . وأما مسلمات المذاهب .. فلا تنفعُ الناظر ، وإنَّما تنفع الناظر مع من يعتقد ذلك المذهب . وأما السمعيات.. فلا تنفع إلا مع من ثبت السمع عنده.
If you say: Is there a difference between these sources in their utility in theoretical syllogisms? Know: they vary in generality of benefit. Indeed, the rational and sensory sources are general with all creatures, except one who has no intellect, or has no sense, and the original is known by the sense that he lacks; like the original known by the sense of sight when used with a blind person – it does not benefit; and if the blind person is the viewer, he cannot take that as a premise; likewise hearing in the case of a deaf person. As for the mutawatir (widely transmitted report), it is beneficial, but only for one to whom it has been transmitted. So if someone comes to us from a distant place that the call did not reach, and we want to prove to him by mutawatir that Muhammad (peace be upon him) challenged with the Quran – he will not be able to do so unless we give him time until it becomes mutawatir for him. And many things are mutawatir among some people but not among others. For example, the statement of Abu Hanifa (may God be pleased with him) on the issue of killing a Muslim for a dhimmī is mutawatir among his jurist followers but not among the common blind followers. And how many of his positions on particular issues are not mutawatir among most jurists. As for the original derived from another qiyas (analogy), it does not benefit except with one with whom that qiyas has been established. As for the accepted premises of schools (musallimat al-madhahib), they do not benefit the investigator; rather they benefit the investigator only with one who believes in that school. And as for scriptural proofs, they do not benefit except with one for whom scripture is established.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage responds to a hypothetical question about differences among the six sources (madarik) in their usefulness for theoretical syllogisms. Ghazali explains that they vary in generality. Rational and sensory sources are universal, except for someone lacking the relevant faculty (e.g., blind person cannot use sight-based premise). Mutawatir is useful only for those to whom the report has reached; it may need time to become known. He gives an example: Abu Hanifa's ruling on retaliation is mutawatir among his followers but not among commoners. An original premise derived from another analogy is only useful if the interlocutor accepts that analogy. Musallimat (accepted premises) only work with those who follow that school. Sam'iyyat (scriptural proofs) only work with those who accept scripture. This demonstrates the dialectical nature of kalam: arguments must be tailored to the audience's accepted sources.

Key Terms
المدارك
المقاييس النظرية
العقلية
الحسية
المتواتر
مسلمات المذاهب
السمعيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sources of knowledge are equally valid and useful for any opponent.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the utility of each source depends on the interlocutor's epistemic access and acceptance. Rational/sensory sources are nearly universal but fail if the person lacks the faculty; mutawatir requires transmission; school-specific and scriptural proofs rely on prior acceptance.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which two sources are generally beneficial for all people except those lacking the relevant faculty?
A) Rational and scriptural
B) Rational and sensory
C) Mutawatir and rational
D) Sensory and mutawatir

Key Takeaway
The utility of each source depends on the epistemic circumstances of the opponent; effective theological arguments must be audience-aware.

?

This lesson forces you to evaluate your own reliance on different sources: do you often use qiyas without first establishing the original ruling with your interlocutor? Write one concrete step you will take to ensure your arguments are built on premises your listener actually accepts.

The Varied Utility of Cognitive Sources in Proofs
Ghazali distinguishes types of premises and their applicability in proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why different types of cognitive sources (such as sensory, rational, transmitted, and sectarian premises) have different scopes of utility in logical reasoning.

Core Idea
Not all premises are equally useful; each source depends on the audience's capacity and context.

Imam al-Ghazali begins a crucial methodological discussion: when we use premises in arguments, are all sources equally beneficial? He answers with a nuanced classification. This passage establishes that the usefulness of a premise depends on the nature of the source and the circumstances of the person receiving it. Understanding this will prevent us from mistakenly assuming that one type of evidence works for everyone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the sources
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Identify each type of cognitive source Ghazali mentions: intellectual/sensory, mass-transmitted (mutawatir), analogical, sectarian postulates, and revelation-based.
Notice that Ghazali uses a question-and-answer style.
2
Understand the principle of 'generality of benefit'
Ghazali says sources vary in 'عموم الفائدة' (generality of benefit). The most universal are intellect and senses, but even they have limitations (e.g., blind cannot use visual premises).
This is the key organizing idea: a source may be objective but not universally accessible.
3
Examine each source's limitation
For each source, consider the condition Ghazali gives. For mutawatir, it requires time to reach the recipient. For analogical premises, the underlying analogy must be accepted. For sectarian postulates, they only work within that school. For revelation (sam‘iyyat), the recipient must accept revelation as authoritative.
4
Study the concrete example
Ghazali gives the example of Abu Hanifa's ruling on killing a Muslim for a dhimmi. This ruling is mutawatir among Hanafi jurists but not among laymen. Reflect on how the same piece of knowledge has different reach.
This illustrates that 'mass-transmission' is relative to the audience.
5
Reflect on the overall purpose
Ghazali concludes this section as a preliminary before entering the main topics (أقطاب) of the book. Why is it important to know these distinctions before engaging in theological arguments?
This connects the lesson to the larger goal of correct reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : فهل من فرق بين هذه المداركِ في الانتفاع بها في المقاييس تحريجة فهل المدارك في الانتفاع النظرية ؟
فاعلم : أنها متفاوتة في عموم الفائدة ، فإنَّ المدارك العقلية والحسية عامة مع كافة الخلق ، إلا من لا عقل له ، أو لا حس له وكان الأصل معلوماً بالحس الذي فقده ؛ كالأصل المعلوم بحاسة البصر إذا استعمل مع الأكمه .. فإنه لا ينفع ، والأكمه إذا كان هو الناظر .. لم يمكنه أن يتخذ ذلك أصلاً ، وكذلك المسموع في حق الأصم .
فأما المتواتر .. فإنَّه نافع ، ولكن في حقٌّ من تواتر إليه ، فمن وصل إلينا من مكان بعيد لم تبلغه الدعوة ، وأردنا أن نبينَ له بالتواتر : أنَّ محمداً صلى الله عليه وسلم تحدَّى بالقرآن .. لم يُقدر عليه ما لم نمهلهُ مدَّةً حتى يتواتر عنده ، وربَّ شيء يتواتر عند قوم دون قوم .
فقولُ أبي حنيفة رضي الله عنه في مسألة قتل المسلم بالذمي متواتر عند الفقهاء من أصحابه دون العوام من المقلدين ، وكم من مذاهب له في آحاد المسائل لا تتواتر عند أكثر الفقهاء
وأما الأصل المستفاد من قياس آخر .. فلا ينفعُ إلا مع من قُرِّر معه ذلك القياس .
وأما مسلمات المذاهب .. فلا تنفعُ الناظر ، وإنَّما تنفع الناظر مع من يعتقد ذلك المذهب
وأما السمعيات.. فلا تنفع إلا مع من ثبت السمع عنده
فهذه مدارك علم هذه الأصول المفيدة بترتيبها ونظمها العلم بالأمور المجهولة المطلوبة
وقد فرغنا من التمهيدات ، فلنشتغل بالأقطاب التي هي مقاصد الكتاب
If you say: Is there a difference among these sources in benefiting from them in measurements/analogies? A [؟] investigation, so are the sources theoretical in benefit? Know: they vary in generality of benefit. Indeed, intellectual and sensory sources are common to all creation, except one who has no intellect, or one who has no sense while the premise is known by the sense he lacks; like a premise known by the sense of sight if used with a blind person — it does not benefit. And if the blind person is the viewer, he cannot take that as a premise. Similarly, audible [premises] regarding a deaf person. As for mass-transmitted (al-mutawatir) — it is beneficial, but only for one to whom it is transmitted. So if someone comes to us from a distant place that the call did not reach, and we want to prove to him by mass-transmission that Muhammad ﷺ challenged with the Qur'an — we cannot do so unless we give him time until it becomes mass-transmitted to him. And perhaps something is mass-transmitted among some people but not others. So the statement of Abu Hanifa (may God be pleased with him) regarding the issue of killing a Muslim for a dhimmi is mass-transmitted among the jurists of his school but not among the common laymen. And how many of his opinions on individual issues are not mass-transmitted among most jurists. As for a premise derived from another analogy — it does not benefit except with one with whom that analogy has been established. As for the accepted postulates of schools (muslimat al-madhahib) — they do not benefit the viewer, but benefit the viewer only with one who believes that school. As for revelation-based premises (al-sam‘iyyat) — they do not benefit except with one for whom revelation is established. These are the sources of knowledge of these useful principles by their arrangement and organization: knowledge of unknown desired matters. And we have finished the preliminaries, so let us occupy ourselves with the poles (al-aqtāb) which are the purposes of the book.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a classification and evaluation of the different types of cognitive sources (madarik) used in theoretical reasoning (maqayis). It begins with a rhetorical question asking whether there is a difference among these sources in terms of their usefulness. Ghazali then explains that they vary in the generality of their benefit. First, intellectual and sensory sources are universal in principle, but their application is limited by the deficiency of the knower: a blind person cannot benefit from visual premises, and a deaf person cannot benefit from auditory ones. Second, mass-transmitted (mutawatir) reports are beneficial only to those to whom the transmission has reached; it requires time to become established for someone far away. An example is given of a juristic ruling of Abu Hanifa that is mutawatir among his followers but not among the common people. Third, a premise derived from another analogy only benefits those who accept that analogy. Fourth, accepted postulates of a school benefit only those who adhere to that school. Fifth, revealed premises (sam‘iyyat) only benefit those who accept revelation as valid. Ghazali concludes by stating that these are the sources of knowledge for the principles of this science, arranged systematically to lead to knowledge of unknown desired matters. He then announces the end of the preliminaries and the beginning of the main topics (al-aqtāb) of the book.

متواتر
سمعيات
مسلمات المذاهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational or sensory premises are always universally applicable to all audiences.
Correction: Ghazali shows that even intellectual and sensory premises depend on the presence of the relevant faculty in the knower (e.g., a blind person cannot use visual premises).

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The usefulness of a premise depends not only on its type but also on the capacity and context of the audience.

Transition to the First Pole: Examining God's Essence
Ghazali concludes preliminaries and introduces the first pole with ten claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali transitions from preliminaries to the main poles of his book, specifically the first pole concerning Allah's essence.

Core Idea
The structure of a theological work reveals its priorities.

This short passage marks a pivotal moment in Ghazali's al-Iqtiṣād. Having laid the foundational principles for acquiring knowledge of theological matters, he now announces the core subject: the poles (aqṭāb) of the book. The first pole focuses on the essence of Allah, and Ghazali tells us it will contain ten claims. This brief statement sets the agenda for the detailed arguments to follow.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the transition
Notice the phrase 'we have finished the preliminaries, so let us engage in the poles.' This is a clear structural marker.
2
Understand the definition of the first pole
The passage defines it as 'the examination of the essence of Allah'. This is the subject.
3
Note the mention of ten claims
Ghazali says the first pole contains ten claims. This prepares the reader for the upcoming sections.
The claims themselves are not listed here.

فهذه مدارك علم هذه الأصول المفيدة بترتيبها ونظمها العلم بالأمور المجهولة المطلوبة وقد فرغنا من التمهيدات، فلنشتغل بالأقطاب التي هي مقاصد الكتاب. حد القطب الأول: النظر في ذات الله عز وجل، وفيه عشر دعاوى.

He then declares that they will now engage in the poles which are the actual objectives of the book. Specifically, he defines the first pole as the examination of the essence of Allah (ʿazza wa jalla), emphasizing that it includes ten specific claims (daʿāwā). This sets the stage for the detailed arguments to follow.

The Futility of Overcomplicating Obvious Matters
Ghazali comments on the methodological principle that overcomplicating evident truths is counterproductive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's warning that overelaborating proofs for already clear matters is counterproductive and obscures rather than clarifies.

Core Idea
Overproofing the obvious only muddies the water.

Imam al-Ghazali offers a crisp methodological reminder: when something is already evident, piling on complicated arguments does not help—it only creates confusion. This principle guides the theologian to know when to be brief and when to elaborate. Let's explore this important caution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Notice how concise Ghazali is—his style matches his message.
The Arabic uses 'تكلف' which implies unnecessary effort.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on three words: الدليل (proof), الواضحات (obvious matters), and الغموض (obscurity). Understand their roles in the statement.
3
Grasp the principle
Ghazali asserts that overelaborating proof for obvious matters increases obscurity, not clarity. Ask yourself why this might be true.
Think about how excessive detail can distract from a simple truth.
4
Consider the application
Reflect on when this principle applies in theology and everyday reasoning. Can you think of a topic where less proof is more?
Ghazali uses this to justify brevity on foundational issues like the temporality of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الجملة : فتكلفُ الدليل على الواضحات يزيدها غموضاً ، ولا يفيدها وضوحاً .
And in general: overelaborating proof for obvious matters increases their obscurity and does not add clarity.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This is a brief methodological remark. Ghazali observes that when something is already clear, constructing elaborate proofs only makes it more obscure, not clearer. This serves as a justification for brevity in treating certain foundational principles—like the temporality of motion and rest—which are evident enough without extended argumentation.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Ghazali is not rejecting proofs altogether; he is warning against unnecessary complexity when clarity already exists. Proofs are still needed for non-obvious matters.

Key Takeaway
Overcomplicating what is already clear only creates confusion—true clarity often needs no elaborate proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Linguistic Ruling on Metaphors for God
Ghazali explains the linguistic ruling on metaphorical names for God and limits its relevance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic criteria for using metaphorical names for God and why such linguistic disputes are not central to theological inquiry.

Core Idea
Metaphors for God must suit the borrowed meaning; linguistic error is not a theological matter.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali addresses the case where someone claims a name for God is a metaphor. He explains that linguistically, if the metaphor is suitable, it is acceptable; if not, it is a linguistic error. However, he cautions that such detailed linguistic analysis is not appropriate for the science of theology (kalam), which deals with rational proofs about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the two conditional scenarios.
The passage uses 'فإن صلح' and 'وإن لم يصلح'.
2
Identify the two linguistic scenarios
Find where Ghazali describes suitable metaphor and unsuitable metaphor. Note the outcome for each.
3
Understand Ghazali's dismissal
Notice the phrase 'والنظر في ذلك لا يليق بمباحث العقول'. Why does he say this?
Think about the scope of 'ilm al-kalam.
4
Reflect on separation of disciplines
Consider why Ghazali distinguishes between linguistic correctness and theological reasoning.
This is a key methodological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن زعم أنه استعارة .. نُظِرَ إلى المعنى الذي به شارك المستعار منه : فإن صلح للاستعارة .. لم ينكر عليه بحق اللغة ، وإن لم يصلح .. قيل له : أخطأت على اللغة ، ولا يستعظم ذلك إلا بقدر استعظام صنيع من يُبْعِدُ في الاستعارة ، والنظر في ذلك لا يليق بمباحث العقول . وأما حق الشرع وجواز ذلك وتحريمه : فهو بحث فقهي يجب طلبه على الفقهاء ؛ إذ لا فرق بين البحث عن جواز إطلاق الألفاظ من غير إرادة معنى رأيان في إطلاق فاسد ، وبين البحث عن جواز الأفعال ، وفيه رأيان : الأسماء عليه سبحانه التي لم يرد بها السمع إما أن يقال : لا يطلقُ اسمٌ في حق الله تعالى إلا بالإذن ، وهذا لم يرد فيه إذن ؛ فيحرم . كان يوهم وإما أن يقال : لا يحرم إلا بالنهي ، وهذا لم يرد فيه نهي ، فينظر : فإن خطأ . . فيجب الاحتراز منه ؛ لأنَّ إيهام الخط في صفات الله تعالى حرام ، وإن لم يوهم خطاً .. لم نحكم بتحريمه ، وكلا الطريقين محتمل ، ثم الإيهام يختلفُ باللغات وعادات الاستعمال ، فربَّ لفظ يوهم عند قوم ، ولا يوهم عند غيرهم
And if he claims that it is a metaphor, then one looks to the meaning by which it shares with the thing from which it is borrowed: if it is suitable for metaphor, he is not objected to on linguistic grounds; and if it is not suitable, it is said to him: you have erred in language. And that is not considered grave except to the extent of the gravity of the act of one who goes far in metaphor; and considering that is not appropriate for intellectual investigations. As for the right of the Shari'a and its permissibility and prohibition: that is a jurisprudential inquiry that must be sought from jurists, for there is no difference between investigating the permissibility of uttering words without intending a meaning? two opinions on a corrupt utterance, and between investigating the permissibility of actions. And concerning it there are two opinions: regarding the names of Him, exalted, that are not reported by revelation: either it is said: no name is applied to God Almighty except by permission, and this one has no permission, so it is forbidden. Or it is said: it is not prohibited except by prohibition, and this one has no prohibition, so one looks: if it implies error? then it must be avoided; because implying error concerning the attributes of God Almighty is forbidden. And if it does not imply error, we do not rule it forbidden. And both ways are possible. Then the implication varies with languages and customary usages; so perhaps a word implies something for some people and does not imply it for others.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the permissibility of using metaphorical names or attributes for God that are not explicitly mentioned in revelation. It begins by addressing the linguistic aspect: if someone claims a word is a metaphor, one examines whether the borrowed meaning is suitable. If suitable, it is linguistically acceptable; if not, it is a linguistic error. However, Ghazali notes that delving into such metaphorical considerations is not appropriate for rational theological investigations. He then turns to the Shari'a ruling, which is a jurisprudential matter. He presents two opinions: (1) No name can be applied to God without explicit permission from revelation; since such permission is absent, it is forbidden. (2) Only names explicitly forbidden are prohibited; since no prohibition is found, the name may be permissible unless it implies something erroneous about God. The implication of error (iiham al-khat') in God's attributes is strictly forbidden. The likelihood of implication varies by language and custom, so a word may be problematic in one context but not another. Ghazali does not decide between the two opinions, acknowledging both as possible.

Key Terms
استعارة
حق اللغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that linguistic accuracy is essential for theological claims about God.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that linguistic errors in metaphor are not central to rational theology; such scrutiny is beyond the scope of kalam.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what happens if a metaphorical name for God is linguistically unsuitable?
A) It is forbidden by Shari'a.
B) It is a linguistic error but not a grave matter.
C) It invalidates faith.
D) It must be punished.

Key Takeaway
Linguistic suitability of metaphorical names for God is a secondary issue; theology focuses on rational meaning, not rhetoric.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself focused on the precise wording of God's descriptions rather than the meaning they convey about His greatness? How does Ghazali's statement that linguistic analysis is 'not appropriate for intellectual investigations' call you to reprioritize your focus in understanding God?
?
When you hear a metaphor for God that seems imperfect, do you feel compelled to correct it immediately? According to Ghazali, not every linguistic error is grave—what does this reveal about the weight you should place on your own judgments of such descriptions?
?
Ghazali warns against going 'far in metaphor.' Is there a way you describe God in your personal supplications or thoughts that might be too human-like or casual? How can you ensure your descriptions remain reverent while still feeling intimate?

5 min
The Shari'a Ruling on Unrevealed Names for God
Ghazali presents the Shari'a ruling on unrevealed names for God with two opinions and the principle of avoiding implication of error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two juristic opinions on using names for God that are not found in revelation, and the principle that implying error about God is forbidden.

Core Idea
Implication of error in God's attributes is forbidden; permissibility depends on context and custom.

Ghazali now shifts from linguistic analysis to the Shari'a ruling. He presents two prevailing opinions among scholars: some require explicit permission from revelation, while others only forbid what is explicitly prohibited. Both opinions are plausible, but all agree that any name implying error in God's attributes is forbidden. The determination of implication varies with language and custom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice the two opinions and the condition about implying error.
The text says 'فيه رأيان' indicating two positions.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find the first opinion: permission required; second opinion: prohibition required. Understand the reasoning.
3
Note the principle of iiham al-khat'
Implication of error in God's attributes is forbidden. This is the key constraint under the second opinion.
The term 'إيهام الخط' is crucial.
4
Understand the role of custom
Notice that implication varies by language and customary usage; a word may imply error for some but not others.
This shows Ghazali's sensitivity to context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن زعم أنه استعارة .. نُظِرَ إلى المعنى الذي به شارك المستعار منه : فإن صلح للاستعارة .. لم ينكر عليه بحق اللغة ، وإن لم يصلح .. قيل له : أخطأت على اللغة ، ولا يستعظم ذلك إلا بقدر استعظام صنيع من يُبْعِدُ في الاستعارة ، والنظر في ذلك لا يليق بمباحث العقول . وأما حق الشرع وجواز ذلك وتحريمه : فهو بحث فقهي يجب طلبه على الفقهاء ؛ إذ لا فرق بين البحث عن جواز إطلاق الألفاظ من غير إرادة معنى رأيان في إطلاق فاسد ، وبين البحث عن جواز الأفعال ، وفيه رأيان : الأسماء عليه سبحانه التي لم يرد بها السمع إما أن يقال : لا يطلقُ اسمٌ في حق الله تعالى إلا بالإذن ، وهذا لم يرد فيه إذن ؛ فيحرم . كان يوهم وإما أن يقال : لا يحرم إلا بالنهي ، وهذا لم يرد فيه نهي ، فينظر : فإن خطأ . . فيجب الاحتراز منه ؛ لأنَّ إيهام الخط في صفات الله تعالى حرام ، وإن لم يوهم خطاً .. لم نحكم بتحريمه ، وكلا الطريقين محتمل ، ثم الإيهام يختلفُ باللغات وعادات الاستعمال ، فربَّ لفظ يوهم عند قوم ، ولا يوهم عند غيرهم
And if he claims that it is a metaphor, then one looks to the meaning by which it shares with the thing from which it is borrowed: if it is suitable for metaphor, he is not objected to on linguistic grounds; and if it is not suitable, it is said to him: you have erred in language. And that is not considered grave except to the extent of the gravity of the act of one who goes far in metaphor; and considering that is not appropriate for intellectual investigations. As for the right of the Shari'a and its permissibility and prohibition: that is a jurisprudential inquiry that must be sought from jurists, for there is no difference between investigating the permissibility of uttering words without intending a meaning? two opinions on a corrupt utterance, and between investigating the permissibility of actions. And concerning it there are two opinions: regarding the names of Him, exalted, that are not reported by revelation: either it is said: no name is applied to God Almighty except by permission, and this one has no permission, so it is forbidden. Or it is said: it is not prohibited except by prohibition, and this one has no prohibition, so one looks: if it implies error? then it must be avoided; because implying error concerning the attributes of God Almighty is forbidden. And if it does not imply error, we do not rule it forbidden. And both ways are possible. Then the implication varies with languages and customary usages; so perhaps a word implies something for some people and does not imply it for others.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the permissibility of using metaphorical names or attributes for God that are not explicitly mentioned in revelation. It begins by addressing the linguistic aspect: if someone claims a word is a metaphor, one examines whether the borrowed meaning is suitable. If suitable, it is linguistically acceptable; if not, it is a linguistic error. However, Ghazali notes that delving into such metaphorical considerations is not appropriate for rational theological investigations. He then turns to the Shari'a ruling, which is a jurisprudential matter. He presents two opinions: (1) No name can be applied to God without explicit permission from revelation; since such permission is absent, it is forbidden. (2) Only names explicitly forbidden are prohibited; since no prohibition is found, the name may be permissible unless it implies something erroneous about God. The implication of error (iiham al-khat') in God's attributes is strictly forbidden. The likelihood of implication varies by language and custom, so a word may be problematic in one context but not another. Ghazali does not decide between the two opinions, acknowledging both as possible.

Key Terms
إيهام
خطأ
حق الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All names for God that are not in the Qur'an or Sunnah are equally prohibited.
Correction: Ghazali shows there are two valid opinions, and permissibility depends on implication and custom.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the key factor that can render a name for God forbidden under the second opinion?
A) It is not found in revelation.
B) It implies an error about God.
C) It is a metaphor.
D) It is linguistically inappropriate.

Key Takeaway
The Shari'a ruling on unrevealed names for God depends on whether permission is required or prohibition is required, but all agree that implying error about God is forbidden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you refer to God by a name not found in the Quran or Sunnah, do you ever pause to consider whether that name might imply something erroneous about Him? What does this lesson teach you about the responsibility of every word you use for your Creator?
?
Ghazali emphasizes that implication varies by language and custom. Think of a word in your own language that is sometimes used for God. Could it imply error to some people? How will you check before using it?
?
This is a fiqhi issue — one that requires consulting jurists. Have you ever sought scholarly guidance on how to speak about Allah? What is one way you can implement this lesson in your daily speech starting today?

Ghazali's Method: Prioritizing Difficult Points
Ghazali comments on the balance between brevity and thoroughness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali chose to go beyond brevity in his creedal work and focus on difficult theological issues.

Core Idea
True theological depth requires focusing on obscurity, not restating the obvious.

In this methodological note, Ghazali explains his approach in writing this book. He acknowledges that he has exceeded the usual brevity, but he justifies it by pointing out the shortcomings of existing concise creeds. He criticizes them for being verbose on clear matters while neglecting the truly difficult points. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for theological study.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify Ghazali's criticism of existing works
Find the three flaws he lists: verbose on clear matters, including unnecessary additions, and lenient on difficult points. The passage has these three elements.
The Arabic key terms help: إطناب, واضحات, تساهل, مضائق الإشكالات.
3
Understand his solution
Ghazali proposes transferring verbosity from clear points to obscure ones. Ask yourself: why is this more important? How does this shape the rest of his book?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد جاوزنا حد الاختصار ، ولكنَّ المعتقدات المختصرة في هذا الفن أراها مشتملة على الإطناب في الواضحات ، والشروع في الزيادات الخارجة عن المهمات ، مع التساهل في مضائق الإشكالات ، فرأيتُ نقل الإطناب من مظان الوضوح إلى مواقع الغموض أهم وأولى
And we have exceeded the limit of brevity, but I see that the abbreviated creedal works in this craft contain verbosity on clear matters, and embarking on additions that are beyond the important matters, along with leniency regarding the narrow points of difficulties. So I considered that transferring the verbosity from the loci of clarity to the locations of obscurity is more important and more worthy.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This is a methodological note. Ghazali acknowledges that his treatment has gone beyond the usual brevity. He criticizes existing short creedal summaries for being verbose on obvious matters, introducing irrelevant additions, and being lax on difficult issues. He therefore decided to shift the emphasis from clear points to obscure ones, prioritizing the difficult matters that require careful explanation.

The Scholar's Duty and the Nature of Mutashabihat
Ghazali contrasts lay and scholarly approaches and critiques a misconception about mutashabihat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali distinguishes between the approaches of common people and scholars toward mutashabihat, and why he rejects equating them with the disconnected letters of the Quran.

Core Idea
For Ghazali, the duty is tanzih, not exhaustive understanding—and not everything ambiguous is mutashabihat.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a key methodological issue: how should different audiences engage with ambiguous Quranic verses? He explains that the common people have limited capacity, while scholars must pursue understanding—but with the recognition that the core obligation is to affirm Allah's transcendence. He then critiques a common mistake: lumping all difficult expressions together as 'mutashabihat'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali contrasts the common people ('awamm) with the scholars ('ulama).
The passage ends abruptly with a heading; we will focus on the complete argument before it.
2
Identify the two key claims
Ghazali makes two main points: first, that scholars are not individually obligated to understand every meaning; second, that verses like istiwa are not like the huruf muqatta'a. Identify these in the text.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Ask yourself: if the duty is tanzih, then what is the real purpose of engaging with mutashabihat? How does Ghazali's view protect against anthropomorphism?
This connects to the broader Islamic theological principle of transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا ؛ لأنَّ عقول العوام لا تتسع لقبول المعقولات ، ولا إحاطتهم باللغات تتسع لفهم توسعات العرب في الاستعارات وأما العلماء : فاللائقُ بهم تعرُّفُ ذلك وتفهمه ، ولستُ أقول : إن ذلك فرضُ عين ، إذ لم يرد به تكليف ، بل التكليف التنزيه عن كل ما يشبهه بغيره ، فأما معاني القرآن .. فلم يُكلف الأعيان فهم جميعها (٥) ولكن لسنا نرتضي قول من يقول (٦) : إنَّ ذلك من المتشابهات كحروف وأمثالها ليست من أوائل السور ؛ فإنَّ حروف أوائل السور ليست موضوعة باصطلاح سابق المنشـــــابــــه، ورأي المؤلف في تعريف المتشابه
And this is because the intellects of the common people do not accommodate the acceptance of rational matters, nor does their mastery of languages accommodate understanding the Arabs' expansions in metaphors (al-isti'arat). As for the scholars, what befits them is to acquaint themselves with that and understand it. And I do not say that it is an individual obligation (fard 'ayn), for no such obligation has come; rather the obligation is to declare [Allah] free from anything that resembles Him. As for the meanings of the Quran, the [religious] authorities were not obligated to understand all of them. But we do not approve of the statement of those who say: 'That is from the mutashabihat (ambiguous verses) like the letters at the beginnings of the surahs which are not of that kind.' For the letters at the beginnings of the surahs are not established by prior convention.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage explains why the lay approach is appropriate: common people cannot grasp rational arguments or the subtleties of Arabic metaphorical language. Scholars, however, should strive to understand the meanings, but it is not an individual obligation; the essential duty is tanzih (declaring transcendence). Ghazali then criticizes those who claim that these texts (like istiwa and nuzul) are mutashabihat similar to the disconnected letters (huruf muqatta'a) at the start of surahs. He argues that the huruf muqatta'a are not conventional language, whereas the istiwa verse is in plain Arabic. The passage ends mid-sentence, perhaps continuing to define mutashabihat.

Key Terms
العوام
العلماء
الاستعارات
التنزيه
المتشابهات
حروف أوائل السور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all difficult Quranic verses (e.g., about Allah's attributes) are mutashabihat similar to the disconnected letters (huruf muqatta'a).
Correction: Ghazali argues that the huruf muqatta'a are not conventional language, whereas verses like istiwa are in Arabic and have meanings that scholars can investigate, even if their precise modality is unknown.

Checkpoint

A) Because scholars are too busy with other duties
B) Because the primary obligation is tanzih (declaring Allah's transcendence)
C) Because the Quran's meanings are entirely unknowable
D) Because scholars already know all meanings

Key Takeaway
Ghazali emphasizes that the duty regarding mutashabihat is tanzih, and that huruf muqatta'a are unlike other ambiguous expressions because they lack conventional meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a verse like 'The Most Merciful istawa over the Throne,' does your heart rest with saying 'Allah is not like that in a way I can picture' (tanzih), or do you feel restless until you 'understand' it? What does that restlessness reveal about your trust in Allah's words?
?
Ghazali says scholars are not obligated to grasp every Quranic meaning. If you are a student of knowledge, do you approach the Quran as a book to be mastered or a guide to humble yourself before? What would change if you truly believed that tanzih is enough?
?
Some might search for hidden meanings in the disconnected letters (huruf muqatta'a) as if they hold secret knowledge. Have you ever fallen into that trap? Why do you think we prefer the allure of secrets over the simple duty to declare Allah’s transcendence?

The Mirror Analogy: How Unfamiliarity Limits Rational Imagination
Ghazali argues that if one had never seen a mirror, they would deem self-vision impossible, showing the poverty of intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the mirror analogy to argue that rational reasoning alone can lead to false conclusions when it relies on unfamiliar premises, and how this applies to the debate on seeing God.

Core Idea
Unfamiliarity can blind reason to truth.

Ghazali presents a powerful argument against those who rely solely on rational divisions without considering empirical evidence. Using the example of a person who has never seen a mirror, he shows how even a logically valid reasoning chain can lead to a false conclusion if one premise is untrue. This passage challenges us to examine the limits of our own rational imagination.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the argument
Ghazali is responding to the Mu'tazila who deny the vision of God based on rational impossibility. He presents an entailment (ilzam) they cannot escape.
This sets the stage for the thought experiment.
2
Analyze the thought experiment
Read the description of the person unfamiliar with mirrors and his logical division of possibilities. Notice how the Mu'tazila would accept this division as correct.
The division seems exhaustive to the Mu'tazili.
3
Identify the false premise
Ghazali points out that the entire division is built on the premise 'I am not facing myself', which is empirically false because mirrors allow us to see ourselves indirectly.
Thus the conclusion of impossibility is invalid.
4
Extract the lesson about rational capacity
Ghazali concludes that the Mu'tazila's narrow capacity (hawṣala) prevents them from accepting what they have not encountered. Reflect on how this applies to theological debates.
This is the core objection against relying solely on a priori reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يُسْتَحْقَرَ هَذَا الْإِلْزَامُ؛ فَإِنَّهُ لَا مَخْرَجَ عَنْهُ لِلْمُعْتَزِلَةِ، وَنَحْنُ نَعْلَمُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ: أَنَّ الْإِنْسَانَ لَوْ لَمْ يُبْصِرْ نَفْسَهُ قَطُّ، وَلَا عَرَفَ الْمِرْآةَ، وَقِيلَ لَهُ: إِنَّهُ يُمْكِنُ أَنْ تُبْصِرَ نَفْسَكَ فِي مِرْآةٍ .. لَحَكَمَ بِأَنَّهُ مُحَالٌ، وَقَالَ: لَا يَخْلُو إِمَّا أَنْ أَرَى نَفْسِي وَأَنَا فِي الْمِرْآةِ.. وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ، أَوْ أَرَى مِثْلَ صُورَتِي فِي جِرْمِ الْمِرْآةِ، أَوْ فِي جِرْمٍ وَرَاءَ الْمِرْآةِ .. وَهُوَ مُحَالٌ؛ إِذْ لِلْمِرْآةِ فِي نَفْسِهَا صُورَةٌ، وَلِلْأَجْسَامِ الْمُحِيطَةِ بِهَا صُورَةٌ، وَلَا تَجْتَمِعُ صُورَتَانِ فِي جِسْمٍ وَاحِدٍ؛ إِذْ مُحَالٌ أَنْ يَكُونَ فِي جِسْمٍ وَاحِدٍ صُورَةُ إِنْسَانٍ وَحَدِيدٍ وَحَائِطٍ، وَإِنْ رَأَيْتُ نَفْسِي حَيْثُ أَنَا . . فَهُوَ مُحَالٌ؛ إِذْ لَسْتُ فِي مُقَابَلَةِ نَفْسِي، فَكَيْفَ أَرَى نَفْسِي وَلَا بُدَّ مِنَ الْمُقَابَلَةِ بَيْنَ الرَّائِي وَالْمَرْئِيِّ؟! وَهَذَا التَّقْسِيمُ صَحِيحٌ عِنْدَ الْمُعْتَزِلِيِّ، وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّهُ بَاطِلٌ، وَبُطْلَانُهُ عِنْدَنَا لِقَوْلِهِ: (إِنِّي لَسْتُ فِي مُقَابَلَةِ نَفْسِي.. فَلَا أَرَاهَا)، وَإِلَّا .. فَسَائِرُ أَقْسَامِ كَلَامِهِ صَحِيحٌ؛ وَبِهَذَا يَسْتَبِينُ ضِيقُ حَوْصَلَةِ هَؤُلَاءِ عَنِ التَّصْدِيقِ بِمَا لَمْ يَأْلَفُوهُ، وَلَمْ تَأْنَسْ بِهِ حَوَاشِيهِمْ.
And this entailment should not be belittled, for the Mu'tazila have no way out of it. We know by necessity that if a person had never seen himself at all, and did not know the mirror, and it was said to him: 'It is possible for you to see yourself in a mirror,' he would judge it impossible. He would say: 'It must be either that I see myself while I am in the mirror—impossible; or I see the like of my form in the body of the mirror, or in a body behind the mirror—impossible, because the mirror itself has an image, and the surrounding bodies have an image, and two images cannot coexist in one body; it is impossible for the image of a human, iron, and a wall to be in one body. And if I see myself where I am, that is impossible because I am not facing myself. How can I see myself when opposition between the seer and the seen is necessary?' This division is correct according to the Mu'tazili, and it is known to be false. Its falsehood according to us lies in his saying: 'I am not facing myself, so I do not see it.' Otherwise, the rest of his statements are correct. And by this the narrow capacity of these people to believe what they have not encountered and what their faculties are not accustomed to becomes clear.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's rational limitation. Ghazali argues that his mirror entailment (ilzām) is unanswerable. He illustrates with a thought experiment: if a person had never seen his own face or a mirror, and was told he could see himself in a mirror, he would consider it impossible and propose a rational division of possibilities (taqsīm): either the image is in the mirror (impossible because he is not inside), or in the mirror's substance or behind it (impossible due to multiple images in one body), or he sees himself directly where he is (impossible because he cannot face himself). The Mu'tazili would accept this division as exhaustive and conclude impossibility. However, this very division is false because the premise 'I am not facing myself, so I do not see it' is false, as experience shows. The Mu'tazila's error is that they reject what they have not experienced, showing the narrowness (ḍīq ḥawṣala) of their intellect. Thus, the denial of God's vision is due to unfamiliarity, not rational proof.

Key Terms
إِلْزَام (entailment/necessary consequence)
ضَرُورَة (necessity/intuition)
تَقْسِيم (division/categorization)
حَوْصَلَة (capacity/understanding)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A logically exhaustive division of possibilities proves impossibility.
Correction: A logical division is only as sound as its premises; an empirically false premise invalidates the conclusion.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali identify as the root cause of the Mu'tazila's denial of God's vision?
A) Their adherence to logical divisions
B) Their inability to believe what they have not encountered
C) Their rejection of the mirror analogy
D) Their ignorance of the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
Rational division is only as reliable as its premises; empirical evidence can reveal false premises, showing the limits of pure reason in theological matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What truth have you dismissed as impossible simply because you had no experience with it? How does this mirror analogy challenge you to reconsider that dismissal today?
?
When you encounter a doctrine that seems rationally impossible (like the vision of God), do you humbly admit your own ignorance, or do you confidently declare it false? What would change if you treated your 'impossibility' judgment as a sign of your own limited familiarity?
?
Ghazali says the Mu'tazila's error came from a false premise they assumed was self-evident. What premise in your own life might be just as false but feels so obvious? Will you expose it to the light of experience or revelation?

Affirming Hearing and Sight: Why Revealed Terms Matter
Ghazali clarifies that rational reduction does not excuse creedal omission of scriptural terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational reduction of certain attributes does not dispense with the need to affirm them by their specific scriptural terms in creed.

Core Idea
In creed, revealed words take precedence over rational reduction.

Imam Ghazali has argued that hearing and sight reduce to knowledge in terms of perception. But now he clarifies a crucial creedal principle: even if rational analysis merges them, the creedal obligation requires explicit affirmation using the terms revealed in the Qur'an and Sunnah. This passage establishes the methodological priority of scriptural expression over rational simplification.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Notice the phrase 'وههنا تحقيق' — this signals an important investigation is being presented.
The ellipsis after 'الإدراك' may indicate a break; focus on the main argument.
2
Recall the Rational Reduction Context
Remember from previous lessons that Ghazali argued hearing and sight go back to the attribute of knowledge in the sense of perception. Now he is addressing a possible misconception from that reduction.
This contextual reminder helps appreciate why the current point is needed.
3
Understand the Creedal Principle
The core insight: affirming 'knowledge' in general does not suffice in creed. One must explicitly affirm hearing and sight using the specific terms 'sam'' and 'basar' as revealed. Ask yourself why the general affirmation is insufficient.
The answer lies in the transmitted nature of creed—it follows revealed wording, not rational distillation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (وههنا تحقيق؛ وهو أنهما وإن رجعا إلى صفة العلم بمعنى الإدراك.. فإثبات صفة العلم إجمالاً لا يغني في العقيدة عن إثباتهما تفصيلاً بلفظيهما الواردين في الكتاب والسنة)
He said: (And here is an investigation: it is that even if they go back to the attribute of knowledge in the sense of perception, affirming the attribute of knowledge in general does not suffice in creed for affirming them in detail by their two terms occurring in the Book and the Sunnah.)
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is an investigation (tahqiq) that clarifies a crucial point in creedal formulation. While the author has stated that hearing and sight reduce to knowledge, he now emphasizes that in terms of actual belief (aqida), it is not enough to merely affirm knowledge as a general attribute. One must explicitly affirm hearing and sight using the specific terms (sam' and basar) that are revealed in the Qur'an and Sunnah. This is because the detailed wording carries specific meanings and is part of the transmitted faith. Thus, even if a rational analysis might merge them into knowledge, the creedal obligation requires separate affirmation by their scriptural names.

Key Terms
تحقيق
إجمالاً
تفصيلاً
الكتاب والسنة
العقيدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational reduction of attributes to knowledge means we only need to affirm knowledge generically.
Correction: Creed requires explicit affirmation by the revealed terms, even if rational analysis reduces them.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that affirming knowledge in general does not suffice in creed?
A) Because knowledge is not a real attribute.
B) Because hearing and sight are not related to knowledge.
C) Because the revealed terms require detailed affirmation.
D) Because the majority holds them as independent.

Key Takeaway
Even if attributes are rationally reducible, creed must explicitly affirm them using the exact terms revealed in the Qur'an and Sunnah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever dismissed a specific attribute of Allah because you thought the general concept of His knowledge covers it? After reading this, what will you change about how you speak of Allah's attributes in your daily dhikr or du'ā?
?
What other teachings from the Qur'an and Sunnah have you implicitly reduced or generalized to the point of losing their explicit, detailed place in your faith? What one step will you take this week to reclaim their specific revelation in your heart and practice?

Critique of Using Consensus to Prove God's Speech
Ghazali critiques consensus and prophetic word as proofs for God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that using consensus or the Prophet's word to prove God's attribute of speech leads to circular reasoning.

Core Idea
To prove speech with consensus is to circle back to the same speech.

Imam al-Ghazali examines two faulty approaches to proving that Allah has the attribute of speech. The first assumes what it tries to prove; the second relies on consensus or the Prophet's own statement, which itself depends on Allah's speech. In this passage, he exposes the circularity inherent in such arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Ghazali presents two distinct critiques: one about assuming the point of dispute, and the other about circularity.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the circularity argument
Focus on the second part: 'Whoever wants to prove speech by consensus or by the saying of the Prophet...' Ghazali says consensus is based on the Prophet's saying, so using it to prove speech is circular.
Think about why this is a logical flaw.
3
Reflect on the methodological lesson
Consider why Ghazali emphasizes this point. It shows that in theology, proofs must not presuppose what they aim to establish.
This is a key principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مأمورين من جهة الخلْقِ الذين يتصوَّرُ منهم الكلام .. فمسلَّم ، وإن أردت جوازه على العموم من الخلق والخالق.. فقد أخذت محل النزاع مسلماً في نفس الدليل وهو غير مسلم (1) إثبات الكلام ومن أراد إثبات الكلام بالإجماع، أو بقول الرسول .. فقد سام نفسَهُ بالإجماع وقول (2) خطة خسف؛ لأن الإجماع يستند إلى قول الرسول (3) . الرســـــول مـــلـــك ضعيف كذلك
Commanded from the perspective of the created beings from whom speech is conceivable... is conceded. And if you intend its permissibility in general for the created and the Creator, then you have taken the point of dispute as conceded within the proof itself, and it is not conceded. (1) Proving speech: Whoever wants to prove speech by consensus or by the saying of the Prophet has doomed himself with consensus and saying (2) a disastrous plan; because consensus is based on the saying of the Prophet (3). The Prophet is a weak king as well.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage critiques two methods of proving God's attribute of speech. First, if one tries to argue that since created beings are commanded, therefore God must be a commander (i.e., speaker), this assumes the conclusion if applied to God. Second, using consensus or the Prophet's word is circular: consensus itself depends on the Prophet's speech, so it cannot be used to prove that speech exists. The phrase about the Prophet being a 'weak king' is obscure but likely continues the critique.

Key Terms
إجماع (consensus)
قول الرسول (saying of the Prophet)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consensus is an independent proof for theological claims.
Correction: Ghazali shows that consensus depends on the Prophet's speech, so it cannot be used to prove speech without circularity.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali reject using consensus to prove God's speech?
A) Consensus is not a valid source in theology.
B) Consensus itself depends on the Prophet's speech, leading to circularity.
C) Consensus requires agreement of all Muslims, which is impossible.
D) Consensus only applies to legal matters, not attributes of God.

Key Takeaway
Consensus cannot prove God's speech because consensus itself relies on prophetic speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accept a religious argument because it is widely held by scholars, do you ever pause to examine whether that argument might itself rely on the very thing it is trying to prove? How will you look for hidden circularity in your own beliefs from now on?
?
Think of a time you trusted a teacher or book's authority without questioning its foundation. How does Ghazali's warning about the 'weak king' (the Prophet's dependence on God) challenge you to seek the ultimate source of your convictions, not just the intermediary?
?
If the consensus of scholars or even a prophetic hadith cannot independently prove an attribute of God without circular reasoning, what does that demand from you personally in how you approach theological proofs? Will you commit to tracing every argument back to its first principles, even when it is uncomfortable?

The Fallacy of Deriving Attributes from Verbal Nouns
Ghazali criticizes the mistake of deriving attribute names from verbal nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that deriving attribute names like 'knowing' from 'knowledge' does not imply a real, distinct state in God's essence.

Core Idea
Linguistic derivation does not create real distinctions in God.

Ghazali exposes a common methodological error: assuming that because an attribute name is derived from a verbal noun, there must be a real state distinct from the attribute itself. He uses the analogy of calling someone 'shod' to show the confusion and then applies it to divine attributes, warning against being misled by language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of Ghazali's argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the analogy of 'shod' (munta'il) at the beginning.
2
Identify the example and its application
Ghazali first gives a simple worldly example: being 'shod' means having a shoe, not an extra state called 'shoddiness'. Then he applies the same logic to the divine attribute of knowledge.
3
Understand the linguistic error
The error arises from derivation (ishtiqaq): just as 'alim' is derived from 'ilm', some wrongly think a distinct state called 'alimiyya' exists. Ghazali calls this pure nonsense.
4
Grasp the consequences
This fallacy, if accepted, leads to invalid concepts of cause and effect (al-illa wa al-ma'lul). Ghazali says the falsity is clear to a sound mind not rusted by repetitive terms.
Those entrenched in this error require a longer refutation beyond this summary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول : هو منتعل ، فلا معنى لكونه منتعلاً إلا أنه ذو نعل وما يُظَنُّ من أن قيام العلم بالذات يوجب للذات حالة تسمى عالمية .. هوس محض ، بل العلم هو الحالة (۱) ، فلا معنى لكونه عالماً إلا كونُ الذات على صفة وحال ، تيك الصفةُ والحال هي العلم فقط ، ولكن مَنْ يأخذُ المعاني من الألفاظ ؛ فإذا تكررت الألفاظ بالاشتقاقات .. لا بدَّ أن يغلط ؛ فاشتقاق صفة العالم من لفظ العلم أورثَ هذا الغلط ، فلا ينبغي أن يغتر به ، وبهذا يبطل جميع ما قيل وطُوِّل من العلة والمعلول ، وبطلان ذلك جلي بأوَّلِ العقل لمن لم يتكرز على سمعه ترديد تلك الألفاظ ، ومن عَلِقَ ذلك بفهمه.. فلا يمكن نزوعه منه إلا بكلام طويل لا يحتمله هذا المختصر .
He says: He is shod, so the meaning of him being shod is only that he has a shoe. And what is supposed, that the subsistence of knowledge (ilm) in the essence (dhat) necessitates for the essence a state (hala) called being knowing (alimiyya) – it is pure nonsense; rather, knowledge is the state, so there is no meaning to him being knowing except that the essence is upon a description (sifa) and state, and that description and state are knowledge only. But whoever takes meanings from words: when words are repeated by derivations (ishtiqaqat), he must err; for the derivation of the attribute (sifa) of 'alim from the word 'ilm has produced this error, so one should not be deluded by it. And by this, all that has been said and prolonged about the efficient cause and the effect (al-illa wa al-ma'lul) is invalidated, and its falsity is clear at the beginning of reason for one whose hearing is not rusted by repetition of those words. And whoever has that clinging to his understanding cannot be weaned from it except by lengthy discourse that this summary cannot bear.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a methodological critique of a linguistic fallacy. Ghazali begins with an example: calling someone 'shod' (munta'il) simply means he has a shoe, not that some extra entity called 'shoddiness' subsists in him. He then directly applies this to the attribute of knowledge (ilm). Those who think that the subsistence of knowledge in God's essence produces a distinct 'state' called 'being knowing' (alimiyya) are mistaken. Knowledge itself is the state; there is no third thing. The error arises from linguistic derivation (ishtiqaq): just as 'munta'il' is derived from 'na'l' (shoe), 'alim' is derived from 'ilm'. But one should not be misled into thinking that derivative words necessitate a real distinction between the attribute and the state. This invalidates much philosophical talk about cause and effect (al-illa wa al-ma'lul). For anyone not hardened by such terms, the falsity is evident. Those who have internalized this linguistic confusion require a long refutation beyond this brief work.

Key Terms
العلم (al-ilm, knowledge)
الاشتقاق (al-ishtiqaq, derivation)
الحالة (al-hala, state)
الذات (al-dhat, essence)
الصفة (al-sifa, attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the derivation of an attribute name (e.g., 'alim' from 'ilm') implies a real, separable state distinct from the essence itself.
Correction: Ghazali argues that 'alim' simply means the essence has knowledge; there is no intermediate state called 'alimiyya'.

Checkpoint
What example does Ghazali use to illustrate the fallacy of linguistic derivation?
A) The example of 'shod' (munta'il) from 'shoe' (na'l).
B) The example of 'knowing' (alim) from 'knowledge' (ilm).
C) The example of 'powerful' (qadir) from 'power' (qudra).
D) The example of 'commanding' (amir) from 'command' (amr).

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the derivation of 'alim' from 'ilm' does not necessitate a distinct state of 'being knowing' separate from knowledge itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's knowledge, do you ever subconsciously imagine it as a thing or a quality added to His essence, like a picture in a frame? If so, how does that subtle mental image reduce the Living God to a composite being? Will you actively catch and dismantle that image the next time you say 'Allah knows'?
?
Ghazali warns that repeating theological terms can rust the hearing so that one cannot see plain truth. Is there any term about God you have heard so often that you no longer feel its weight or question its meaning—like 'all-knowing' or 'all-powerful'? What would it take for you to hear that term as if for the first time today, and let its reality pierce your heart?
?
You likely use derived words about God every day: 'the Knower,' 'the Wise,' 'the Seeing.' But do you ever pause to realize that these words do not add new parts to God but merely refer to His perfect and simple reality? How might your worship change if you consciously connected each attribute directly to His essence, without any intermediate concepts? Will you practice this tonight in your du'a?

Ghazali's Response to the Persistent Difficulty
Ghazali answers an objection that his explanation leaves the problem unresolved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends his explanation of divine attributes by arguing that the goal is to show preference among limited options, not to eliminate all difficulty.

Core Idea
The best explanation does not need to eliminate all difficulty, only to be superior to alternatives.

Ghazali has argued that differences in divine attributes are due to differences in their relations. Now an objector presses that this leaves the fundamental problem unresolved. In this passage, Ghazali redefines what a successful argument must achieve, showing that the preferred view is the least problematic among limited options. This is a key methodological move in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening lines of the passage where the objector states that the problem remains even after admitting different relations. Understand the objection.
Notice how the objector uses 'إشكال' (difficulty/problem) as a key term.
2
Identify Ghazali's redefinition of the goal
Ghazali states that the goal is to show definitively the preference of one belief over others, not to remove all difficulty. Find that statement.
Look for the phrase 'غاية الناصر لمذهب معين أن يُظهر على القطع ترجيح اعتقاده على اعتقاد غيره'.
3
Understand the limited options argument
Ghazali says there are only three possible views, and a fourth is inconceivable. Consider why limiting options strengthens his case.
4
Compare the difficulty levels
Ghazali admits that his view still has some difficulty, but the alternatives have greater difficulty. This relative comparison is key.
He says 'واللازم على غيره أعظم منه' (what follows from others is greater than it).
5
Reflect on the limits of theological inquiry
Ghazali concludes that complete removal of difficulty is impossible because the attributes are transcendent. Think about the humility this implies.
He says 'أما قطعه بالكلية ... فهو أمر ممتنع'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحريجة : إثباتكم فإن قيل : فليس في هذا قطع دابر الإشكال ؛ لأنك إذا اعترفت باختلاف اختلافِ المتعلَّق .. فالإشكالُ قائمٌ ، فما لك وللنظر في سبب للمختلفات لا يرفع الإشكال ما بسبب الاختلاف بعد وجود الاختلاف ؟ فأقولُ : غاية الناصر لمذهب معين أن يُظهر على القطع ترجيح اعتقاده على اعتقاد غيره ، وقد حصل هذا على القطع ؛ إذ لا طريق إلا واحد من هذه الثلاث أو اختراع رابع لا يعقل ، وهذا الواحد إذا قوبل بطرفيه المقابلين له .. علم على القطع رجحانه ، وإذا لم يكن بد من اعتقاد ولا معتقد إلا هذه الثلاث ، وهذا أقرب الثلاث.. فيجب اعتقاده ، فيبقى ما يحيك في الصدر من إشكال يلزم على هذا ، واللازم على غيره أعظم منه ، وتقليل الإشكال ممكن ، أما قطعه بالكلية والمنظور فيه هو الصفاتُ القديمة المتعالية عن أفهام الخلق .. فهو أمر ممتنع إلا بتطويل لا يحتمله هذا الكتاب ، هذا هو الكلام العام
Objection: your proof—if it is said: There is not in this a cutting off of the root of the problem; because if you admit the difference of the difference of the relation, then the problem remains, so what is the point of looking for a cause for the different things that does not remove the problem after the existence of the difference? I say: The utmost goal of the supporter of a particular doctrine is to show definitively the preference of his belief over the belief of others, and this has been obtained definitively; for there is no way except one of these three or the invention of a fourth that is inconceivable. And this one, when compared with its two opposites, it is known definitively that it is preferable. And since belief is necessary and there is no belief except these three, and this is the closest of the three, so it must be believed. Then what remains in the heart from a problem that follows from this, and what follows from others is greater than it, and reducing the problem is possible. As for cutting it off entirely, and the object of consideration is the eternal attributes that are exalted beyond the understanding of creatures, that is impossible except with a lengthy discussion that this book does not bear. This is the general discourse.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is an objection (taḥrīja) and its answer. The objector argues that Ghazali's explanation—that attribute differences are due to relation variation—does not fully resolve the problem because admitting different relations still leaves the issue of why the attributes themselves are distinct. Ghazali responds by redefining the goal: the supporter of a doctrine aims to show definitively the preference of his belief over alternatives. He posits that there are only three possible views (presumably concerning the attributes), and the one he advocates is the closest to truth. Although some difficulty may remain, the alternative views have greater problems. Complete removal of difficulty is impossible because the attributes are transcendent, and a full resolution would require an extended discussion beyond the scope of this book. Thus, the argument is about choosing the best explanation among limited options, not eliminating all perplexity.

Key Terms
إشكال
اعتقاد
ترجيح
قطع
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A correct doctrine must have no difficulties at all.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the correct doctrine is the one with the least difficulty among available options; complete removal of difficulty is impossible due to the transcendence of the attributes.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali respond to the objection that his explanation does not fully remove the difficulty?
A) He admits defeat and offers no answer
B) He says the difficulty is actually beneficial
C) He argues that the preferred view has less difficulty than alternatives, and complete removal is impossible
D) He invokes a fourth option to solve the problem

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends his position by arguing that the goal is to show the preference of his view over alternatives, and that the preferred view, while not free of all difficulty, has less difficulty than the other options.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt in your faith that you cannot fully resolve, do you tend to abandon the entire belief or do you compare it to other worldviews and see which has greater difficulties? How can Ghazali's method help you in your own spiritual struggles?
?
Are you willing to commit to the most probable truth even if some questions remain unanswered in your heart? What might this reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom versus your need for complete human understanding?
?
In which area of your life do you demand absolute certainty before acting, and how might that be a form of pride that refuses to accept the limits of human knowledge that Ghazali humbly acknowledges?

Focus on Fundamentals in Debate
Ghazali advises focusing on three core issues when debating deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's strategic advice to limit theological debates with disingenuous opponents to three core issues: the origin of action, the temporality of the world, and divine power.

Core Idea
Debate deniers by sticking to foundational issues, not endless details.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a practical challenge: some opponents use Islamic phrases to avoid persecution without genuine belief. He warns against getting lost in detailed theological arguments and instead prescribes discussing only the most essential matters: the origin of human actions, the createdness of the world, and God's power. This lesson explains his reasoning and shows how to apply this focus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Ghazali frames the opponents as insincere and then gives a clear directive.
The phrase 'يتجملون بإطلاق عبارات' is key — they use words to look good, not from belief.
2
Identify the three core topics
Ghazali lists three topics: 'أصل الفعل' (origin of action), 'حدث العالم' (temporality of world), and 'قدرة' (power). Write them down.
These are the fundamentals he considers non-negotiable.
3
Reflect on why details are avoided
Ghazali says 'فلا نشتغل معهم بهذه التفصيلات' — we should not engage in details. Consider: what might happen if you start with details before settling the basics?
This is a strategic move, not dismissiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : هؤلاء لا يعتقدون الدين والإسلام ، وإنما يتجملون بإطلاق عبارات احترازاً من السيف ، والكلام معهم في أصل الفعل وحدث العالم والقدرة ؛ فلا نشتغل معهم بهذه التفصيلات
In summary: these people do not believe in religion and Islam; they only adorn themselves with uttering phrases to protect themselves from the sword. Speech with them is [to be] about the origin of action, the temporality of the world, and power; so we should not occupy ourselves with them concerning these details.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a methodological directive. Ghazali states that certain opponents (likely the philosophers mentioned previously) do not genuinely believe in Islam; they merely use Islamic terminology to avoid persecution. Therefore, one should not argue with them about detailed theological matters (like the specifics of attributes). Instead, discussion should be confined to foundational issues: the origin of human actions (whether created by God or not), the temporality of the world (whether it is created or eternal), and the nature of divine power. Only after these fundamentals are settled should details be addressed. This is a practical approach to debate: establish common ground on core principles before moving to finer points.

Key Terms
أصل الفعل
حدث العالم
قدرة
تفصيلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is being dismissive or rude towards opponents.
Correction: He is strategically prioritizing fundamental issues to first establish common ground, not simply dismissing the opponents.

Checkpoint

A) Prophethood, miracles, and the afterlife
B) The origin of action, the temporality of the world, and power
C) God's essence, attributes, and names
D) Faith, disbelief, and the Quran

Key Takeaway
When debating deniers of religion, Ghazali advises focusing on three foundational topics—origin of action, temporality of world, and divine power—and avoiding premature detailed discussions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Role of Word and Meaning in Ghazali's Conclusion
Ghazali sums up his argument by affirming the equal importance of wording and meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali insists that both the literal word (اللفظ) and the intended meaning (المعنى) are equally necessary in theological arguments about divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Only by respecting both word and meaning can we correctly affirm God's transcendence.

In this brief conclusion, Ghazali sums up his approach to proving that God cannot be a locus for temporal events. He emphasizes that the entire discussion hinges on two elements: the precise wording used and the deeper theological meaning. This lesson unpacks that methodological statement and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the two sentences. Notice how Ghazali uses the phrase 'لا نظر إلا فيهما' (there is no consideration except in both).
2
Identify the two elements
Find the terms اللفظ and المعنى. Understand that he is not prioritizing one over the other, but insisting on both.
3
Reflect on the methodological message
Ask yourself: Why would a theologian need to care about both literal expression and meaning? How does this protect against errors about God's nature?
This connects to the learning objective about balancing word and meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد انكشف من هذا حظ اللفظ والمعنى جميعاً، ولا نظر إلا فيهما. فهذا ما أردنا أن نذكره في استحالة كونه محلاً للحوادث إجمالاً وتفصيلاً
So from this is disclosed the share of the word and the meaning together, and there is no consideration except in both of them. Thus this is what we intended to mention concerning the impossibility of His being a locus for events, in summary and in detail.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage serves as the conclusion to a preceding argument about whether it is impossible for God to be a locus of events (i.e., a substrate for accidents). Ghazali states that the entire discussion has now revealed the roles of both the literal expression (اللفظ) and the intended meaning (المعنى). There is nothing left to examine beyond these two aspects. He then declares that this summary and detailed treatment fulfills what he set out to explain regarding the impossibility of God being a place where temporal events occur.

?
The conclusion of this passage is that God cannot be the locus of events—He is beyond change and accident. Do you truly live as if you believe this, or do you sometimes act as if God is affected by your actions? How can you adjust your heart to rely on His immutability?

Summary of the Pole of Attributes and Transition to the Pole of Actions
Ghazali enumerates the components of the Pole of Attributes and transitions to the next pole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali summarizes the Pole of Attributes and transitions to the Pole of Actions, and you will grasp the structural methodology of his work.

Core Idea
Ghazali's summary shows that each major claim rests on many supporting claims.

After a detailed treatment of the divine attributes in the second pole, Ghazali now takes stock. He enumerates the main types of claims presented: seven claims corresponding to the essential attributes, three branches from power, five improbabilities from speech, and four common rulings. This adds up to about twenty principal claims, each built upon subsidiary ones. Then he announces the next pole: the actions of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the summary and transition.
Notice the numbers and the shift from summarizing to announcing the next topic.
2
Identify the enumeration
Identify the seven claims, three branches, five improbabilities, four common rulings, and the total of about twenty claims.
These numbers reflect the structure of the previous section.
3
Understand the transition
Notice how Ghazali moves from summarizing the Pole of Attributes to initiating the Pole of Actions with the phrase 'فلنشتغل بالقطب الثالث'.
This is a clear structural marker in his work.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Think about why Ghazali provides this summary: it shows that each principal claim is built upon subsidiary claims, highlighting the nested argumentation of kalam.
This methodology is key to understanding the depth of his treatise.
