فهذا تمام ما أردنا ذكرَهُ في قطب الصفات ، وقد اشتمل على سبع دعاوى ، وتفرع عن صفة القدرة ثلاثة فروع ، وعن صفة الكلام خمسةً استبعادات ، واجتمع من الأحكام المشتركة بين الصفات أربعة أحكام ، فكان المجموع قريباً من عشرين دعوى هي أصول الدعاوى ، وإن كانت تنبني كل دعوى على دعاوى بها يتوصل إلى إثباتها فلنشتغل بالقطب الثالث في أفعال الله عز وجل
So this is the completion of what we intended to mention in the Pole of Attributes, and it comprised seven claims, and from the attribute of power branched three branches, and from the attribute of speech five improbabilities, and from the common rulings among the attributes four rulings came together, so the total was close to twenty claims which are the principal claims, although each claim is built upon claims by which one arrives at its proof. So let us occupy ourselves with the third pole concerning the actions of Allah, Mighty and Majestic.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a summary and transition. It enumerates the contents of the previous section (the Pole of Attributes). Ghazali states that it contained seven main claims (likely corresponding to the seven essential attributes: power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, sight, speech). From the attribute of power, three branches were derived; from the attribute of speech, five improbabilities were derived; and from the common rulings among the attributes, four rulings were gathered. The total number of principal claims is about twenty. Each such claim is itself built upon subsidiary claims that lead to its proof. Finally, Ghazali announces the next topic: the third pole concerning the actions of Allah.

The Seven Claims About Divine Acts
Ghazali enumerates seven claims regarding permissibility of divine acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven claims Ghazali makes about divine acts and their underlying principle that nothing is obligatory upon God.

Core Idea
Ghazali defends absolute divine freedom: no act of God is obligatory.

In this opening of the third pole, Ghazali lays out the foundational principle that all divine acts are permissible (ja'iz) and none are obligatory. He then enumerates seven specific claims that will be defended in this section. These claims challenge common theological assumptions about God's obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening principle
Begin by reading the first sentence: 'The totality of the acts of God are permissible; none is described as obligatory.' Reflect on the significance of this starting point.
Ghazali is asserting God's absolute freedom from any external compulsion.
2
Identify the seven claims
Locate the passage that begins with 'seven matters' and list each claim. Notice how they progressively widen divine discretion: from not obligating, to causing pain, to no reward or punishment.
Each claim challenges a common conception about what God 'must' do.
3
Reflect on the unity of the claims
Consider why Ghazali groups these seven claims together. How do they all relate to the core principle that nothing is obligatory upon God?
They are all instances where some would argue God has an obligation.

القطب الثالث
في أفعال الله تعالى
وجملة أفعال الله تعالى جائزة لا يُوصفُ شيءٌ منها بالوجوب ، وندعي لا يجب على الله
في هذا القطب سبعة أمورٍ : ندعي : أنَّه
يجوز الله تبارك وتعالى ألا يكلف عباده ، وأنه يجوز أن
يكلفهم ما لا يُطاق ، وأنه يجوز منه إيلام العباد بغير عوض وجناية ، وأنه لا يجب عليه رعاية الأصلح لهم ، وأنه لا يجب عليه ثواب الطاعة وعقاب المعصية ، وأن العبد لا يجب عليه شيء بالعقل بل بالشرع ، وأنه لا يجب على الله تعالى بعثة الرسل ، وأنه لو بعثَ .. لم يكن قبيحاً ولا محالاً ، بل أمكن إظهار صدقهم بالمعجزة .
وجملة هذه الدعاوى تنبني على البحث عن معنى الواجب والحسن والقبيح ، ولقد خاص الخائضون فيه وطوَّلوا القول في أن العقل : هل يحسنُ ويقبحُ ، وهل يوجب ؟
وإنما كثر الخبط لأنهم لم يحصلوا معنى هذه الألفاظ واختلافِ الاصطلاحات فيها ، وكيف يتخاطب خصمان في أن الفعل واجب أم لا وهما بعد لم يفهما معنى الواجب فهماً محصلاً متفقاً عليه بينهما ؟!
تعالى شيء
فلنقدم البحث عن الاصطلاحات ، ولا بدَّ من الوقوف على معنى ستة بیان مصطلحات ألفاظ ؛ وهي الواجب والحسنُ والقبيحُ والعَبَتُ والسَّفَه والحكمة ؛ فإنَّ هذه دائرة في هذا القطب
الألفاظ مشتركةً ، ومثار الأغاليط إجمالها ، والوجه في مثل هذه المباحث
أن نطرح الألفاظ ونحصل المعاني في العقل بعبارات أخرى ، ثم نلتفت إلى
The Third Pole: On the Acts of God, Exalted is He.
The totality of the acts of God, Exalted is He, are permissible (ja'izah); none of them is described as obligatory (wujub). We assert that nothing is obligatory upon God. In this pole [there are] seven matters: we assert that it is permissible for God, Blessed and Exalted, not to impose obligations (taklif) upon His servants, and that it is permissible for Him to impose upon them what they cannot bear, and that it is permissible from Him to cause pain to servants without compensation or transgression, and that it is not incumbent upon Him to observe what is most beneficial (al-aslah) for them, and that it is not incumbent upon Him to reward obedience and punish disobedience, and that nothing is obligatory upon the servant by reason ('aql) but rather by revelation (shar'), and that it is not obligatory upon God, Exalted is He, to send messengers (rusul), and that if He did send [them], it would not be ugly (qabihan) nor impossible (muhalan); rather, it is possible to manifest their truthfulness through miracles (mu'jizah).
All these claims are built upon inquiry into the meaning of the obligatory (al-wajib), the good (al-hasan), and the ugly (al-qabih). Those who have waded into this have argued and prolonged speech about whether reason ('aql) approves and disapproves, and whether it imposes obligations. The confusion has only increased because they have not arrived at the meaning of these terms and the differences of conventions (istilahat) regarding them. How can two adversaries debate whether an act is obligatory or not while they have not yet understood the meaning of 'obligatory' with a precise and mutually agreed understanding?!
Therefore, let us present an investigation of the conventions (istilahat). It is necessary to stop at the meaning of six terms: al-wajib (the obligatory), al-hasan (the good), al-qabih (the ugly), al-'abath (pointlessness), al-safah (foolishness), and al-hikmah (wisdom); for these circulate in this pole. These terms are shared (mushtarakah), and the source of errors is their ambiguity (ijmal). The correct approach in such investigations is to set aside the terms and grasp the meanings in the mind with other expressions, then we turn to...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to the third pole of the book, which deals with the acts of God. Ghazali begins by stating the overarching principle: all divine acts are permissible (ja'iz) and none is obligatory. He then enumerates seven specific claims that will be defended in this pole: (1) God may choose not to impose obligations on servants; (2) He may impose unbearable obligations; (3) He may cause pain without compensation or prior sin; (4) He is not required to do what is most beneficial for creatures; (5) He is not required to reward obedience or punish disobedience; (6) the servant does not become obliged by reason alone but only by revelation; (7) God is not obliged to send messengers, but if He does, it is neither ugly nor impossible, and He can support them with miracles.

Ghazali then points out that all these claims depend on understanding the meanings of key terms: the obligatory (wajib), the good (hasan), and the ugly (qabih). He criticizes previous discussants for arguing at length about whether reason can judge good and evil without first clarifying what these terms mean. He says the confusion arises because people have not settled on shared definitions. Therefore, before proceeding to the seven claims, he insists on a methodological step: clarifying the conventional meanings of six key terms—wajib, hasan, qabih, 'abath (pointlessness), safah (foolishness), and hikmah (wisdom). These terms are ambiguous because they are used in multiple senses. The proper method, he says, is to set aside the words themselves and first grasp the meanings in the mind using other expressions, then return to the terms. This passage sets the stage for the terminological analysis that follows.

Key Terms
أفعال الله تعالى
جائزة
الواجب
تكليف
أصلح
ثواب
عقاب
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the overarching principle regarding divine acts?
A) God must do what is most beneficial for His servants.
B) God is obligated to send messengers.
C) All divine acts are permissible; none is obligatory.
D) God must reward obedience and punish disobedience.

Key Takeaway
The seven claims collectively assert that God is not subject to any external obligation in His acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God has no obligation to reward obedience, and you will still be held accountable, does that move you to worship Him out of love rather than expectation? How would your prayer change if you removed all thought of reward from your mind?
?
The passage teaches that God may impose what you cannot bear. Have you ever been crushed by a trial that seemed beyond your capacity? How does the absolute freedom of God in that trial reshape your trust in His wisdom, even when you don't understand?
?
God is not obliged to send messengers—yet He did. Does that truth fill you with gratitude for the prophets and the Qur'an? What would your life be like if you treated every revelation as an undeserved gift rather than a burden?

Clarifying Key Terms: Wajib, Hasan, Qabih, and More
Ghazali stresses the need to clarify six key terms before debating divine acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali insists on clarifying six key terms (wajib, hasan, qabih, 'abath, safah, hikmah) before discussing divine acts, and his method for doing so.

Core Idea
Confusion in theology arises from ambiguous terms; Ghazali prescribes terminological clarity.

Having listed his claims, Ghazali now diagnoses a root problem: previous debates about divine acts have been muddled because key terms like 'obligatory' and 'good' were used without agreed meanings. He proposes to clarify six terms that are central to this discussion and outlines a method to avoid equivocation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the dependence on terms
Read the first sentence, which states that all the claims are built on understanding the meaning of 'obligatory' (wajib), 'good' (hasan), and 'ugly' (qabih). Notice how Ghazali ties the entire pole to these concepts.
This shows that Ghazali considers conceptual clarity foundational.
2
Understand the problem of ambiguity
Ghazali criticizes previous scholars for arguing without shared definitions. He gives the example of two opponents debating whether an act is obligatory without agreeing on the meaning of 'obligatory'. Reflect on how this leads to confusion.
The core issue is that terms like 'wajib' are used in multiple senses.
3
Identify the six terms and the method
Ghazali lists six terms: al-wajib, al-hasan, al-qabih, al-'abath, al-safah, al-hikmah. He notes they are shared (mushtarakah) and ambiguous. Then he proposes a method: set aside the words, grasp the meanings in the mind using other expressions, then return to the terms.
This method aims to avoid being misled by linguistic equivocation.

The Need to Define Key Terms Before Debate
Ghazali diagnoses confusion in debates due to undefined terms and proposes a method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali insists on defining key terms like wājib, hasan, and qabīḥ before theological debate, and how his method of setting aside ambiguous terms to focus on underlying meanings resolves confusion.

Core Idea
Clarity of terms is the foundation of sound theological debate.

Ghazali begins by diagnosing a critical flaw in earlier theological arguments: debaters used key terms like 'obligatory' and 'good' without agreeing on their precise meanings. He argues that this lack of conceptual clarity leads to endless confusion. In this passage, he outlines his remedy—first clarifying the six pivotal terms that will form the core of his discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Ghazali sets up the problem before proposing his solution.
The passage contains a key phrase 'تعالى شيء' which may be a scribal addition; ignore it for now.
2
Identify the six key terms
Ghazali lists six terms that need clarification: wājib, hasan, qabīḥ, ʿabath, safah, ḥikma. Find them in the Arabic and note their English equivalents.
These terms will be the focus of the next sections of the book.
3
Understand Ghazali's method
Ghazali advises setting aside the words and focusing on the underlying meanings using alternative expressions, then examining different technical usages. Why does he think this is necessary?
This method avoids confusion caused by ambiguous terminology.

تعالى شيء
فلنقدم البحث عن الاصطلاحات ، ولا بدَّ من الوقوف على معنى ستة بیان مصطلحات ألفاظ ؛ وهي الواجب والحسنُ والقبيحُ والعَبَتُ والسَّفَه والحكمة ؛ فإنَّ هذه دائرة في هذا القطب

All of these claims are based on investigating the meaning of the obligatory, the good, and the evil. Indeed, those who have delved into this have argued at length about whether reason can deem things good or evil, and whether it can impose obligation. The reason for so much confusion is that they have not grasped the meanings of these terms and the differences in their technical usages. For how can two opponents debate whether an act is obligatory or not when they have not yet understood the meaning of 'obligatory' in a conceptual, agreed-upon manner? Therefore, let us first present an inquiry into the technical usages. It is necessary to understand the meaning of six key terms, and the clarification of these terms: the obligatory, the good, the evil, futility, foolishness, and wisdom. For these terms recur throughout this pole. These terms are ambiguous, and the source of errors is their being used in a general way. The correct approach in such investigations is to set aside the terms and derive the meanings in the mind using other expressions, then turn to the terms under investigation and examine the differences in their technical usages. So we say:
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage explains why Ghazali will first define key terms. He argues that previous debates on divine actions went astray because the debaters lacked a precise, agreed-upon understanding of the words 'obligatory', 'good', and 'evil'. He notes that they argued over whether reason can determine goodness, evil, or obligation, but without fixing the meanings, the discussion is futile. He then announces that he will clarify six terms: obligatory (wājib), good (ḥasan), evil (qabīḥ), futility (ʿabath), foolishness (safah), and wisdom (ḥikma). These terms are ambiguous, so his method is to first set aside the words and focus on the underlying meanings using alternative expressions, then look at how the terms are used in different technical contexts. This underscores Ghazali's methodological emphasis on conceptual clarity before argumentation.

Misconception: One might think that the terms like 'obligatory' and 'good' are self-evident and need no definition.
Correction: Ghazali shows that disagreement arises precisely from the lack of agreed definitions. Without clarifying the meanings, debate is futile.

The First Error: Judging by Personal Interest
Ghazali identifies the first error: judging actions as absolutely bad based on one's own interest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali identifies the first error that leads people to mistakenly ascribe absolute moral badness to actions based solely on their own personal interest, and why this is a failure of rational judgment.

Core Idea
What benefits me is not necessarily good in itself; what harms me is not necessarily bad in itself.

In this passage, Ghazali begins to expose three errors that cause confusion in moral reasoning. The first error is the most natural and widespread: we tend to judge actions as absolutely bad simply because they conflict with our own desires. Ghazali dissects this tendency, showing that it stems from a self-absorption that blinds us to others and even to our own changing circumstances. This lesson explores the anatomy of that error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English versions. Notice how Ghazali introduces three errors and immediately elaborates the first one. Pay attention to his precise language: he distinguishes between deeming something bad in a relative sense and judging it absolutely.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two mistakes within the error
Ghazali says the person is correct in originally deeming the act bad (relative to his interest) but makes two mistakes: (1) he judges it as absolutely bad, and (2) he ascribes badness to the essence of the thing. Identify these in the text.
3
Recognize the root cause
Ghazali traces the error back to heedlessness: the person ignores the purposes of others and even his own changing states. Find the phrase that describes this and reflect on why such heedlessness leads to false absolute judgments.
This is key to avoiding the error.
4
Reflect on personal application
Think of a situation where you or someone else might have called something 'bad' simply because it conflicted with personal wishes. How does Ghazali's analysis reveal the flaw in that judgment?
This connects the lesson to real life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن ههنا ثلاث غلطات الكشف عن ثلاث غلطات تخلص من إشكالات غرت الكثير
للوهم، يستفاد من الوقوف عليها الخلاص من إشكالات تغتر بها طوائف كثيرة : تحكيم الأهواء الغلطة الأولى : أنَّ الإنسان قد يطلقُ اسم القبيح على ما يخالف غرضَهُ وإن كان يوافقُ غرض غيره ، ولكنَّهُ لا يلتفتُ إلى الغير ، فكلُّ طبع مشغوفٌ بنفسه ومستخقر ما عداه ، فلذلك يحكم على الفعل مطلقاً بأنه قبيح ، فقد يقول : إنه قبيح في عينه وسببه : أنه قبيح في [؟] بمعنى أنه مخالف لغرضه ، ولكن أغراضه كأنها كلُّ العالم في حقه ، فيتوهم أن المخالف لحقه مخالف في نفسه ، فيضيف القبحَ إلى ذات الشيء ويحكمُ بالإطلاق ، فهو مصيب في أصل الاستقباحِ ، ولكنه مخطىء في حكمه بالقبح على الإطلاق وفي إضافة القبح إلى ذات الشيء ، ومنشؤه غفلته الالتفات إلى غيره ، بل عن الالتفات إلى بعض أحوال نفسه ؛ فإنه قد يستحسن في بعض أحواله عين ما يستقبحُهُ مهما انقلب موافقاً لغرضه
But here there are three errors; revealing them yields deliverance from problems that have deceived many. Due to illusion, from pausing at them one obtains deliverance from problems that many groups are deceived by: the subjection of desires. The first error: that a man may apply the name 'bad' to what contradicts his purpose, even if it agrees with the purpose of another, but he does not pay attention to the other. For every nature is absorbed with itself and considers what is other than it as insignificant. Therefore he judges the act absolutely as bad. He may say: 'It is bad in itself,' and its cause is that it is bad in [؟] meaning that it contradicts his purpose. But his purposes are as if they are the whole world in his view, so he imagines that what contradicts his right is contradictory in itself. So he ascribes badness to the essence of the thing and judges absolutely. Thus he is correct in the origin of deeming it bad, but he is mistaken in his judgment of badness absolutely and in ascribing badness to the essence of the thing. The source of this is his heedlessness of paying attention to others, nay, even of paying attention to some states of himself; for he may deem as good in some of his states the very thing he deems bad whenever it becomes agreeable to his purpose!
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three errors that lead people to mistakenly ascribe absolute moral qualities to actions. Ghazali explains that a person often calls something 'bad' simply because it conflicts with his own personal interest, ignoring that it may serve the interest of another. Since human nature is self-absorbed and dismissive of others, one judges the act as intrinsically bad. He might say it is bad 'in itself,' when in reality it is only bad relative to his own purpose. However, he imagines his own purposes are universal, so he mistakenly believes that what opposes him is absolutely bad. Ghazali notes that such a person is correct in the basic act of deeming it bad (since it does conflict with his interest), but he errs in two ways: (1) in judging it as absolutely bad without qualification, and (2) in ascribing badness to the thing's essence. The root cause is his failure to consider others or even his own varying states—for he may, at other times, deem the very same thing good when it aligns with his interest. Thus, Ghazali exposes the fallacy of projecting one's subjective reaction onto objective reality.

الإطلاق

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something contradicts my interest, it is objectively and absolutely bad.
Correction: An action may be bad relative to one person's purpose but good relative to another's; absolute badness requires a different, non-subjective criterion.

D) Following the opinions of the majority

Key Takeaway
Actions are not absolutely good or bad just because they match or oppose our personal interests; true moral judgment requires looking beyond one's own perspective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

The Second Error: Generalizing from Common Cases
Ghazali identifies the second error, using lying as an example to show how absolute judgments arise from overlooking rare exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second error: how we mistakenly judge an act as absolutely bad based on its usual harm, overlooking rare beneficial exceptions.

Core Idea
Our nature blinds us to rare exceptions, turning conditional judgments into absolutes.

Ghazali continues his critique of common moral errors. Here he highlights how our upbringing and habitual judgments can make us treat a conditional rule as absolute. He uses the example of lying to show that even something usually bad can be justified in rare cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words introduce the error.
The passage is incomplete but the key idea is present.
2
Identify the second error
Notice that Ghazali defines the error as generalizing a judgment from most cases to all cases. Find his formulation.
The error is 'تعميم الأحكام' (generalization of judgments).
3
Understand the cause
Ghazali says the cause is heedlessness of the rare case. Understand why a rare exception matters for moral judgment.
He uses the word 'ذهوله' (heedlessness).
4
Examine the example of lying
See how Ghazali explains that lying is usually bad but can be beneficial in rare situations. Note the psychological conditioning from childhood that makes us view it as absolutely bad.
The example shows how a general rule can become an absolute in our minds.
5
Reflect on the conditional nature
Ghazali concludes that the judgment 'lying is bad' is correct but conditional, not absolute. Understand the nuance.
The condition (harm) is present in all times except the rare beneficial case.

تعميم الأحكام الغلطة الثانية : أنَّ ما هو مخالف للأغراض في جميع الأحوال إلا في حالة نادرة .. فقد يحكمُ الإنسانُ عليه مطلقاً بأنه قبيح ؛ لذهوله عن الحالة النادرة ، ورسوخ مثلاً على الكذب بأنه قبيح مطلقاً في كل حال ، وأن قبحه لأنه كذب لذاته فقط ، لا لمعنى زائد ، وسبب ذلك غفلته عن ارتباط مصالح كثيرة بالكذب في بعض الأحوال ، ولكن لو وقعت تلك الحالةُ . . ربما نفرَ طبعُهُ عن استحسان الكذب لكثرة ألفه باستقباحه ؛ وذلك لأن الطبع ينفرُ عنه من أول الصبا بطريق التأديب والاستصلاح ، ويُلقى إليه أن الكذب قبيح في نفسه وأنه لا ينبغي أن يكذب قط غالب الأحوال في نفسه ، واستيلائه على ذكره ؛ فيقضي وهو قبيح كما قيل ، ولكن بشرط يلازمُهُ في كل الأوقات ، وإنما يفوتُ
Generalization of judgments. The second error: that what is contrary to purposes in all circumstances except in a rare case... a man may judge it absolutely as bad, due to his heedlessness of the rare case. For example, [the notion] that lying is absolutely bad in every case, and its badness is because it is lying in itself, not for an additional meaning. And the cause of that is his heedlessness of the connection of many benefits to lying in some circumstances. But if that rare situation were to occur... perhaps his nature would recoil from approving lying due to his frequent familiarity with deeming it bad; because nature recoils from it from early childhood through discipline and reform, and it is instilled in him that lying is bad in itself and that he should never lie. [This] dominates his mind as if it were inherently bad, so he judges it as bad as has been said, but with a condition that accompanies it at all times, and [this condition] is missed.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents the second error, which concerns the generalization of judgments. Ghazali explains that when something is usually contrary to people's purposes, but only rarely beneficial, a person may judge it as absolutely bad in all cases, forgetting the rare exception. He gives the example of lying: many hold that lying is absolutely bad in every situation, and that its badness is intrinsic to lying itself, not due to any additional factor. The cause is that they fail to see the benefits that lying can bring in some rare circumstances. However, Ghazali notes that even if such a circumstance arises, the person's nature might recoil from approving lying because from early childhood he has been trained to consider it absolutely bad. This training makes the judgment 'lying is bad in itself' so ingrained that it dominates his mind. He concludes that the judgment 'lying is bad' is correct, but it is conditional, not absolute—the condition that makes it bad is present in all times except the rare case, and that condition is missed by the person making the absolute judgment. The passage is cut off, but the core point is clear: absolute judgments ignore exceptions.

The Third Fallacy: Reversing Associations
Ghazali defines the third fallacy and provides examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the psychological fallacy of reversing associations, how the imagination overrides intellect, and three illustrative examples from Ghazali.

Core Idea
The mind's imagination often reverses associations, causing irrational judgments.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali identifies a subtle error of the mind: the tendency to reverse associations. He explains how the imagination, based on prior experience, can lead us to judge things incorrectly, even when our intellect knows better. Through three vivid examples, he shows how this fallacy operates in everyday life, from fearing a striped rope to recoiling from a name. This lesson will help you recognize this fallacy in yourself and others.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the opening sentence carefully. Ghazali states the fallacy: the imagination leaps to the conclusion that because two things have been associated, the relationship must be universal and reversed.
The key point is that the specific is always associated with the general, but not vice versa.
2
Explore the snakebite example
Consider the case of a snakebite victim who fears a striped rope. The imagination associates the rope with harm because it resembles the snake, even though the intellect knows a rope is harmless. Notice how the nature follows the imagination.
3
Reflect on the yellow sweet example
Think about a person who almost vomits when eating yellow sweet because it resembles feces. Even when reason confirms it is clean, the imagination overrides. This shows the power of association beyond mere fear.
4
Analyze the ethnic names example
Ghazali extends the fallacy to social contexts: names associated with ugliness cause aversion even when applied to beautiful individuals. This illustrates how associations can lead to prejudice.
The intellect knows the person is beautiful, but the imagination still recoils.
5
Connect to modern life
Ask yourself: where do you see this fallacy today? Examples might include brand associations, stereotypes, or irrational fears. Understand that recognizing the fallacy is the first step to overcoming it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغلطة الثالثة : سبق الوهم إلى العكس ؛ فإنَّ ما رُئِي مقروناً بالشيء سبق الوهم إلى أن يظَنَّ أنَّ الشيء أيضاً - لا محالة - يكون مقرونا به مطلقاً ، ولا يدري أن الأخص أبداً يكون مقروناً بالأعم ، وأما الأعم فلا يلزم أن يكون مقروناً بالأخص. ومثاله : ما يقال مِنْ أن السليم - أعني : الذي نهشته الحيَّةُ - يخاف من الحبل المبرقش اللون ، وهو كما قيل ؛ وسببه : أنه أدرك المؤذي وهو متصور بصورة حبل مبرقش ، فإذا أدرك الحبل سبق الوهم العقل إلى العكس وحكم بأنه مؤذ ، فينفرُ الطبع تابعاً للوهم والخيال وإن كان العقلُ مكذباً به. بل الإنسان قد ينفرُ عن أكل الخبيص الأصفر لشبهه بالعذرَة ، فيكاد يتقيَّأ عند قول القائل : إنه عذرةٌ ، ويتعذَرُ عليه تناوله مع كون العقل مكذباً به ؛ وذلك لسبق الوهم إلى العكس ، فإنَّهُ أدرك المستقذَرَ رطباً أصفر ، فإذا رأى الرطب الأصفر حكم بأنه مستقذر. بل في الطبع ما هو أعظم من هذا ؛ فإن الأسامي التي تطلق على الهنود والزنوج لما كان يقترن بها قبْحُ المسمّى بها ربما يؤثر في الطبع إلى حد لو سمي به أجملُ الأتراك والروم لنفر الطبع عنه؛ لأنه أدرك الوهم القبيح مقروناً بهذا الاسم ، فيحكم بالعكس ، فإذا أدرك الاسم حكم بالقبح على المسمَّى ونفر الطبع.
The third fallacy: the precedence of the imagination (al-wahm) to the opposite. For when something is seen associated with a thing, the imagination leaps to the conclusion that the thing also — necessarily — is associated with it absolutely, and it does not know that the more specific is always associated with the more general, but the more general is not necessarily associated with the more specific. Example: It is said that the one bitten by a snake (i.e., the one whom a snake has stung) fears a striped rope, and it is as said. Its cause is that he perceived the harmful thing in the form of a striped rope. So when he perceives the rope, the imagination precedes the intellect to the opposite and judges it to be harmful, so the nature recoils following the imagination and fancy, even if the intellect belies it. Rather, a person may recoil from eating yellow sweet (khabis) due to its resemblance to feces, so that he almost vomits when someone says 'it is feces,' and it becomes difficult for him to eat it even though the intellect belies it. That is due to the precedence of the imagination to the opposite: he perceived the loathsome thing as yellow and moist, so when he sees the yellow moist thing, he judges it loathsome. Rather, in nature there is something greater than this: the names given to Indians and Blacks, since ugliness of the named is associated with them, may affect nature to the extent that if the most beautiful of Turks or Romans were given that name, nature would recoil from it; because the imagination perceived the ugly associated with this name, so it judges by the opposite, so when it perceives the name, it judges ugliness of the named and nature recoils.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage defines the third fallacy, which is the mind's tendency to reverse associations (سبق الوهم إلى العكس). Ghazali first states the general principle: when something is perceived in association with another, the imagination immediately assumes the reverse—that the second thing is necessarily associated with the first, without realizing that the specific is always associated with the general, but not vice versa. He then gives three examples to illustrate. The first is the snakebite victim who fears a striped rope because he previously experienced harm from a snake that appeared like a striped rope; the imagination judges the rope as harmful, overriding the intellect. The second example is a person who recoils from yellow sweet (خبيص) because it resembles feces, to the point of nausea even when reason knows it is not feces. The third example concerns ethnic names: names like 'Indian' or 'Black' become associated with ugliness in the mind, so that even a beautiful person given that name would evoke aversion. In each case, the imagination (الوهم) overrides intellect (العقل) due to prior association.

Key Terms
سبق الوهم إلى العكس
الوهم
الخيال
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are observed together, they are necessarily related in both directions.
Correction: The imagination may falsely reverse the association, but the intellect can recognize that the relationship is not necessarily reciprocal.

Checkpoint
What is the main cause of the third fallacy (سبق الوهم إلى العكس) according to Ghazali?
A) Lack of rational thinking
B) Prior association of a quality with a perceived form
C) Deliberate deception
D) Weakness of faith

Key Takeaway
Ghazali warns that the imagination can reverse associations, leading to irrational judgments that the intellect cannot easily override.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you felt an irrational aversion or fear toward something you rationally knew was harmless. What was that thing, and why do you think your nature (tab') reacted despite your intellect? How will you train yourself to pause before your imagination reverses associations?
?
Ghazali says that even the names given to peoples can cause nature to recoil. Are there names, labels, or ethnic groups you find yourself instinctively reacting to—positively or negatively—based on associations you did not choose? Will you commit to examining those associations and letting your intellect judge fairly, even if your nature resists?
?
The example of the yellow sweet shows that even knowing the truth ('it is not feces') does not stop the stomach from turning. What false associations are you allowing to control your actions—perhaps in how you treat others or what you avoid—despite your intellect knowing better? What concrete step will you take today to break that cycle?

How Prior Associations Distort Belief Acceptance
Ghazali applies the third fallacy to show how prior associations distort belief acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how prior negative associations with a theological school can cause someone to reject a sound argument solely due to its attribution, and why Ghazali identifies this as a widespread intellectual failing.

Core Idea
Prior associations, not evidence, often guide our acceptance of beliefs.

This passage from al-Ghazali's Iqtisad examines a subtle yet powerful fallacy: allowing preconceived opinions about a scholar or school to determine whether we accept an argument. Ghazali demonstrates this through a thought experiment, showing that even clear rational arguments can be rejected simply because they are attributed to a disliked group. As you read, consider how this applies beyond theology to any area of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reversed association
Notice Ghazali's claim: people accept an argument until they learn its source, then reject it. This is the third fallacy in action.
The source is the key trigger for reversal.
2
Observe the two examples
Read the Mu'tazili and Ash'ari examples carefully. See how the same argument is accepted then rejected based on attribution.
Both examples mirror each other.
3
Consider the scholars' version
Ghazali extends the critique to those who claim learning. They add 'taqlid of the proof' to taqlid of the school.
They still seek to defend a prior belief, not truth.
4
Analyze the labeling mechanism
Notice how they call supporting evidence 'proof' and opposing evidence 'doubt'. This is a rhetorical strategy, not objective reasoning.
5
Understand the remedy
Ghazali prescribes starting with no belief, looking at the proof, and calling its implication truth. Reflect on the challenge of this method.
This requires setting aside all prior attachments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا مع وضوحه للعقل فلا ينبغي أن يُغفل عنه؛ لأن إقدام الخلق وإحجامهم في أقوالهم وعقائدهم وأفعالهم تابع لمثل هذه الأوهام. فلا يتبع العقل الصرف وأما اتباع العقل الصرف فلا يقوى عليه إلا أولياء الله الذين أراهم الله تعالى الحق حقاً وقواهم على اتباعه. وإن أردت أن تجرب هذا في الاعتقادات فأورد على فهم العامي المعتزلي مسألة معقولة جليّة فيسارع إلى قبولها، فلو قلت له : إنه مذهب الأشعري لنفر وامتنع عن القبول، وانقلب مكذَّباً بعين ما صدَّقَ به مهما كان سيء الظن بالأشعري؛ إذ كان قد قَبُحَ في نفسه ذلك منذ الصبا. وكذلك نقرر أمراً معقولاً عند العامي الأشعري ثم نقول له : إنَّ هذا قول المعتزلي فينفر عن قبوله بعد التصديق ويعود إلى التكذيب! ولست أقول : هذا طبع العوام، بل هو طبع أكثر من رأيتُه من المترسمين باسم العلم؛ فإنهم لم يفارقوا العوام في أصل التقليد، بل أضافوا إلى تقليد المذهب تقليد الدليل، فهم في نظرهم لا يطلبون الحق، بل يطلبون طريق الحيلة في نُصرة ما اعتقدوه حقاً بالسماع والتقليد. فإن صادفوا في نظرهم ما يؤكد اعتقادهم قالوا: قد ظفرنا بالدليل، وإن ظهر لهم ما يضعفُ مذهبهم قالوا: قد عرضَتْ لنا شبهةٌ، فيضعون الاعتقاد المتلقف بالتقليد أصلاً، وينبزون بالشبهة كل ما يخالفه، وبالدليل كل ما يوافقه. وإنما الحقُّ ضدُّه، وهو ألا يعتقد أصلاً شيئاً، وينظر إلى الدليل ويسمي مقتضاه حقاً، ونقيضه باطلاً. وكلُّ ذلك منشؤُه الاستحسان والاستقباحُ بتقدم الإلفِ والتخلق بأخلاق منذ الصبا.
And this, despite its clarity to the intellect, should not be neglected; because the advancing and retreating of people in their statements, beliefs, and actions follows such illusions. So they do not follow pure intellect. As for following pure intellect, none are capable of it except the friends of God (awliya') — those whom God shows the truth as truth and strengthens them to follow it. If you want to test this in beliefs: present a clear rational issue to the understanding of a common Mu'tazili; he quickly accepts it. But if you tell him it is the Ash'ari doctrine, he recoils and refuses acceptance, and turns to denying exactly what he had affirmed, if he has a bad opinion of Ash'ari, because ugliness of that was ingrained in his soul since childhood. Similarly, we present a rational matter to a common Ash'ari, then tell him this is the Mu'tazili view; he recoils from accepting it after affirmation and returns to denial! And I do not say this is the nature of commoners only; rather, it is the nature of most of those I have seen who are adorned with the name of learning. For they have not departed from commoners in the principle of taqlid, but have added to taqlid of the school (madhhab) the taqlid of the proof. In their view, they do not seek the truth, but seek a way of trickery to support what they believe true by hearsay and taqlid. If they encounter something that confirms their belief, they say 'we have found the proof.' If something appears that weakens their school, they say 'a doubt (shubha) has occurred to us.' They make the belief received by taqlid the foundation, and label as 'doubt' everything that contradicts it, and as 'proof' everything that agrees with it. The opposite is the truth: one should not believe anything initially, but look at the proof and call its implication truth, and its contrary falsehood. All of this originates from considering something good or bad based on prior familiarity and moral upbringing from childhood.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage applies the third fallacy to the realm of beliefs (اعتقادات). Ghazali argues that people's actions and beliefs are governed by such illusions, not by pure intellect. Only the friends of God (أولياء الله) can follow pure intellect. He then gives an experiment: a common Mu'tazili will accept a rational argument until told it is Ash'ari, then reject it due to prior negative association. The same occurs with an Ash'ari commoner told a view is Mu'tazili. Ghazali stresses this is not limited to commoners; many who claim learning (المترسمين باسم العلم) do the same: they follow taqlid of both the school and the 'proof', seeking to support preconceived beliefs rather than the truth. They label opposing evidence as 'doubt' (شبهة) and supporting evidence as 'proof' (دليل). The true method is to hold no initial belief but to follow the evidence. All this stems from learning to judge things as good or bad based on early familiarity and upbringing.

أولياء الله
التقليد
الشبهة
الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is condemning all scholars who engage in kalam as dishonest.
Correction: He specifically criticizes those who use labels ('proof' vs. 'doubt') to defend their school rather than seeking truth objectively. He also acknowledges that some (awliya') can follow pure intellect.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the root cause of the behavior where a person accepts a rational argument but rejects it when attributed to a disliked school?
A) Lack of intelligence
B) The third fallacy of reversing associations based on prior negative associations
C) Genuine disagreement with the argument
D) Fear of social consequences

Key Takeaway
True intellectual integrity requires setting aside prior affiliations and following the evidence wherever it leads.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you dismissed an idea simply because of its source—a scholar, a sect, or a teacher—without examining its evidence. What did you lose by that dismissal? Will you seek out that idea again with an open mind?
?
Consider a belief you hold about Islam that you have never personally verified from the Quran and Sunnah. Are you willing to test it today, even if it means discovering you were wrong? What would that cost you?
?
Ghazali says that even those who study can fall into using 'proof' only for what confirms their school. Which of your own cherished opinions are you most protective of? Can you commit to honestly seeking evidence against them this week?

Method of Learning

Overcoming Prejudice: The True Path to Knowledge
Ghazali warns against the influence of illusions and taqlid, and prescribes the correct method of inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how preconceived biases and blind following can distort your perception of truth, and learn the correct method of seeking knowledge by following proof alone.

Core Idea
Truth is obscured by prejudice; only by shedding prior commitments can we see proof clearly.

In this passage, Ghazali exposes a deep-seated psychological obstacle to true knowledge: the tendency to judge arguments based on prior allegiance rather than evidence. He illustrates this with striking examples from theological disputes. This lesson will help you recognize this tendency in yourself and others, and adopt the intellectual integrity of following proof wherever it leads.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Hidden Influence of Illusions
Note how Ghazali asserts that people's actions and beliefs follow illusions, not pure reason. This is a fundamental diagnosis of the human condition.
The term 'أوهام' (illusions) refers to false or preconceived notions.
2
Observe the Examples of Partisan Rejection
Read the examples of the Mu'tazilite and Ash'ari commoners. See how the same argument is accepted or rejected solely based on its attributed source, not its content.
Ghazali uses 'عامي' to mean a layperson, not a scholar.
3
Realize This Affects Even the Learned
Ghazali emphasizes that this behavior is not limited to commoners; many who claim knowledge also fall into it by imitating proof in a biased way.
The phrase 'مترسمين باسم العلم' refers to those who outwardly wear the garb of knowledge.
4
Identify the Flawed Method of 'Proof' and 'Doubt'
Notice how they label evidence that supports their view as 'proof' and opposing evidence as 'doubt'. This is a reversal of the correct methodology.
This inversion is a key symptom of intellectual dishonesty.
5
Embrace the Correct Method: Follow Proof Without Prejudice
Understand Ghazali's prescription: start with no belief, look at the proof, and call its implication truth and its opposite falsehood. Also note the root cause: childhood conditioning.
The goal is to resolve objections by understanding these sources of error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا مع وضوحه للعقل فلا ينبغي أن يُغفل عنه ; لأن إقدام الخلق وإحجامهم في أقوالهم وعقائدهم وأفعالهم تابع لمثل هذه الأوهام . في لا يتبع العقل الصرف وأما اتباع العقل الصرف.. فلا يقوى عليه إلا أولياء الله تعالى الذين إلا الأولي أراهم الله تعالى الحق حقاً وقواهم على اتباعِه ، وإن أردت أن تجرب هذا الاعتقادات .. فأورد على فهم العامي المعتزلي مسألة معقولة جليّة فيسارع إلى قبولها ، فلو قلت له : إنه مذهب الأشعري .. لنفر وامتنع عن القبول ، وانقلب مكذَّباً بعين ما صدَّقَ به مهما كان سيء الظن بالأشعري ; إذ كان قَبحَ في نفسه ذلك منذ الصبا ، وكذلك نقرر أمراً معقولاً عند العامي الأشعري ثم نقول له : إنَّ هذا قول المعتزلي .. فينفر عن قبوله بعد التصديق ويعود إلى التكذيب! ولست أقولُ : هذا طبع العوام ، بل هو طبع أكثر من رأيتُه من المترسمين باسم العلم ; فإنهم لم يفارقوا العوام في أصل التقليد ، بل أضافوا إلى تقليد المذهب تقليد الدليل، فهم في نظرهم لا يطلبون الحق ، بل يطلبون طريق الحيلة في نُصرة ما اعتقدوه حقاً بالسماع والتقليد فإن صادفوا في نظرهم ما يؤكد اعتقادهم .. قالوا : قد ظفرنا بالدليل ، وإن ظهر لهم ما يضعفُ مذهبَهُمْ .. قالوا : قد عرضَتْ لنا شبهةٌ ، فيضعون الاعتقاد المتلقف بالتقليد أصلاً ، وينبزون بالشبهة كل ما يخالفه ، وبالدليل كل ما يوافقه ، وإنما الحقُّ ضدُّه ، وهو ألا يعتقد أصلاً شيئاً ، وينظر إلى الدليل ويسمي مقتضاه حقاً ، ونقيضه باطلاً ، وكلُّ ذلك منشؤُه الاستحسان والاستقباحُ بتقدم الإلفِ والتخلق بأخلاق منذ الصبا فإذا وقفت على هذه المثارات .. سَهُلَ عليك دفعُ الإشكالات
And this, despite its clarity to reason, should not be neglected; because the advancement and retreat of creation in their sayings, beliefs, and deeds follow such illusions. As for following pure reason... none are able to do so except the friends of God, whom God shows the truth as truth and strengthens to follow it. And if you wish to test this regarding beliefs... then present a clear, rational issue to the understanding of a common Mu'tazilite, and he will hasten to accept it. Then if you say to him: 'This is the Ash'ari school,' he will recoil and refuse to accept, and become a denier of the very thing he affirmed, due to his bad opinion of the Ash'ari; that had become ugly in himself since childhood. Likewise, we present a rational matter to a common Ash'ari and then say to him: 'This is the Mu'tazilite view'... then he recoils from accepting it after affirmation and returns to denial! And I do not say: this is the nature of commoners, rather it is the nature of most of those I have seen among those who claim knowledge; for they have not departed from commoners in the essence of imitation (taqlid), but added to imitation of the school the imitation of proof. They, in their view, do not seek truth, but seek a way of artifice to support what they believe to be true by hearsay and imitation. If they encounter in their view what confirms their belief, they say: 'We have found the proof.' And if what weakens their school appears to them, they say: 'A doubt has occurred to us.' So they make the belief taken by imitation as the foundation, and label as 'doubt' everything that contradicts it, and as 'proof' everything that agrees with it. The truth is the opposite: that one should not originally believe anything, and should look at the proof and call its implication truth, and its opposite falsehood. And all of that originates from approval and disapproval due to prior familiarity and moral upbringing since childhood. So if you grasp these sources of error, it becomes easy for you to resolve objections.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a warning about the psychological and epistemological pitfalls of blind following (taqlid) and preconceived biases. Ghazali begins by stating that despite the clarity of certain truths to reason, people's actions and beliefs are often driven by illusions and prior attachments. He then illustrates this with examples: a common Mu'tazilite will accept a rational argument but reject it if told it is from the Ash'ari school, and vice versa. He emphasizes that this is not limited to commoners but is the nature of many who claim knowledge; they add 'imitation of proof' to imitation of the school. They do not seek truth but rather seek to defend what they already believe. When they find something that supports their belief, they call it a 'proof'; when something contradicts it, they call it a 'doubt.' The correct approach, Ghazali says, is to hold no belief initially, and then follow the evidence, calling its implication truth and its opposite falsehood. He traces this problem back to emotional conditioning from childhood. Finally, he says that understanding these sources of error helps resolve objections.

أوهام
عقل صرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is condemning all forms of following a school (taqlid).
Correction: Ghazali is condemning blind partisanship that rejects truth from other sources, not all forms of following established scholarship.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what do many 'learned' people do when they encounter evidence that weakens their school?

B) They label it as a doubt.
C) They abandon their school.
D) They seek a different teacher.

Key Takeaway
True seekers set aside prior allegiances and follow the evidence wherever it leads.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you immediately rejected a religious argument simply because it came from someone you disagree with (a different sect, scholar, or teacher). Are you willing to re-examine that argument today, setting aside your feelings about the source?
?
What is one belief you hold that you might be defending more because you grew up with it than because you have truly examined the proof? How would you go about testing it today?
?
Ghazali says that even scholars label opposing views as 'doubt' without genuine consideration. Can you recall dismissing a criticism of a view you hold as a 'weak argument' without engaging it deeply? What would it cost you to honestly wrestle with that criticism?

Without Revelation, No Obligation to Know God
Ghazali argues against Mu'tazila that reason alone cannot obligate knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that reason alone cannot impose an obligation to know God without revelation, and how he refutes the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Reason only commands what has a known benefit; without revelation, benefit is uncertain.

Ghazali continues his systematic refutation of opposing views. Here he takes on the Mu'tazila claim that reason alone obligates knowledge of God. He argues that any rational obligation must be based on a benefit, and without revelation, we cannot be sure of any benefit in this life or the next. This passage shows the careful logical structure of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Notice the compact style and the logical connectors.
The passage includes a footnote marker (1) referencing the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the claim and opponent
Ghazali states his claim clearly: without revelation, no obligation to know God. The opponent is the Mu'tazila, who assert reason alone obligates.
Contrast this with the previous claims where Ghazali argued for the necessity of revelation.
3
Follow the proof structure
The proof proceeds by examining the basis of rational obligation: reason requires a benefit. Then it considers possibilities: no benefit (vain) or benefit; benefit cannot be for God, so for the servant; present benefit is mere exertion; future benefit (reward) is uncertain. Trace each step.
Ghazali uses a dilemma: every action either has a benefit or not.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does Ghazali conclude that reason alone cannot impose obligation? Because without revelation, we cannot know if there is any benefit, especially in the afterlife.
This argument does not deny reason's value but limits its scope in establishing obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدعوى السادسة
ندعي : أنَّ الشرع لو لم يَرِدْ .. لكان لا يجب على العباد معرفة الله تعالى لا تجب المعرفة حتى يرد الشرع وشكر نعمته ، خلافاً للمعتزلة (۱) ؛ حيث قالوا : إنَّ العقل بمجرده موجب
وبرهانه : هو أن نقول : العقلُ يوجب النظر وطلب المعرفة لفائدة مرتبة عليه ، أو مع الاعتراف بأن وجوده وعدمه في حق الفوائد عاجلاً وأجلاً بمثابة
واحدة .
فإن قلتم : يقضي بالوجوب مع الاعتراف بأنه لا فائدة فيه قطعاً عاجلاً وأجلاً .. فهذا حكم الجهل لا حكم العقل ؛ فإنَّ العقل لا يأمرُ بالعبث ، وكلُّ ما هو خالٍ عن الفوائد كلّها فهو عبثّ ، وإن كان لفائدة . . فلا يخلو : إما أن ترجع إلى المعبود ، أو إلى العبد ؛ ومحال أن ترجع إلى المعبودِ تعالى وتقدَّسَ عن الفوائد ، وإن رجعت إلى العبد .. فلا يخلو : إما أن يكون في الحال ، أو في المآل ؛ أما في الحال .. فهو تعب محض لا فائدة فيه (٢) وأما في المآل . . فالمتوقَّعُ هو الثوابُ ، ومن أين علم أنه يثاب على فعله ؟ بل ربما يعاقب عليه ، فالحكم عليه بالثواب حماقة لا أصل له
The sixth claim: We claim that if revelation were not to come, then it would not be obligatory upon servants to know God; knowledge is not obligatory until revelation comes, nor is gratitude for His blessings, contrary to the Mu'tazila, who said that reason alone is obligating. Its proof: we say: Reason necessitates reflection and seeking knowledge for a benefit attached to it, or while admitting that its existence and non-existence with respect to benefits here and hereafter are equal. If you say: It decrees obligation while admitting that there is absolutely no benefit in it here or hereafter, then this is a judgment of ignorance, not a judgment of reason; for reason does not command vain action, and whatever is devoid of all benefits is vain action. And if there is a benefit, then inevitably it reverts either to the worshipped or to the servant; it is impossible for it to revert to the worshipped, exalted and sanctified above benefits. If it reverts to the servant, then it is either in the present or in the future. As for the present, it is pure exertion with no benefit in it. As for the future, what is expected is reward. How does he know that he will be rewarded for his action? Rather, perhaps he will be punished for it. So judging that he will be rewarded is foolishness with no basis.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth claim: that without revelation, there is no obligation to know God or thank Him, contrary to the Mu'tazila who held that reason alone imposes such an obligation. The proof proceeds by examining the basis of any obligation: reason only commands what has a benefit. If there is no benefit, it is vain. If there is a benefit, it cannot be for God, so it must be for the servant. In the present, there is no evident benefit; in the future, reward is uncertain because one might be punished instead. Therefore, reason cannot establish an obligation.

Key Terms
الشرع
المعرفة
الشكر
العقل
العبث
الثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is denying the value of reason entirely.
Correction: Ghazali is not rejecting reason; he is only denying that reason alone can impose an obligation without revelation.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say about a possible benefit of knowledge in the present time?
A) It is reward from God
B) It is pure exertion with no benefit
C) It is a means to avoid punishment
D) It is the basis of all obligations

Key Takeaway
Without revelation, reason cannot establish an obligation to know God because any supposed benefit is either absent or uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your duties toward Allah, do you ever base them on what 'makes sense' to you rather than on what He has revealed? What area of your life is most prone to this? Commit to checking one such duty against the Qur'an and Sunnah today.
?
This lesson says that without revelation, even gratitude for blessings is not obligatory. Are there blessings for which you neglect to thank Allah because you assume gratitude is 'obvious' and don't actively express it? Identify one blessing and turn it into a specific, immediate praise (say Alhamdulillah with meaning).
?
Ghazali argues that reason alone cannot guarantee reward; it could be punishment. Do you ever assume that your good deeds will automatically be accepted and rewarded? Where is your trust misplaced—in your own efforts rather than in Allah's mercy and promise? Repent of any self-reliance and ask Allah to accept only through His grace.

The Three Categories of What Is Not Known Necessarily
Al-Ghazali divides non-necessary knowledge into three types and explains each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ghazali's threefold division of non-necessary knowledge and the logical order of dependence between reason and revelation.

Core Idea
Reason is the foundation; revelation specifies; both confirm.

Al-Ghazali begins his discussion of what must be affirmed through revelation by categorizing all knowledge that is not immediately known by necessity. He identifies three distinct sources: pure reason, pure revelation, and the combination of both. This passage establishes the methodological framework for all subsequent arguments in the book.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the tripartite division
Read the passage and note that Al-Ghazali divides non-necessary knowledge into exactly three categories. Identify each one and the key examples he gives.
The division is a taqsīm, a standard kalam tool for classification.
2
Understand the priority of reason
Notice that Al-Ghazali places rational knowledge first and explains that revelation depends on it. Reason must establish the existence of God and His speech before revelation can be accepted.
This counters the misconception that all religious knowledge comes solely from revelation.
3
See how each category functions
For each category, ask: What does it provide? Reason gives foundational truths; revelation specifies matters beyond reason; both reason and revelation confirm matters that fall within reason's scope.
The vision of God (ru'ya) is an example of the third category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني
في بيانِ وُجُوبِ التصديق بأمورٍ وَرَدَ بها السمع
وقضى بجوازها العقل
وفيه مقدمة وفصلان :
أما
المقدمة : فهو أنَّ ما لا يعلم بالضرورة ينقسم : إلى ما يعلم بدليل العقل دون الشرع ، وإلى ما يعلمُ بالشرع دون العقل ، وإلى ما يعلم بهما أما المعلوم بدليل العقل دون الشرع .. فهو حدث العالم ، ووجود المحدث وقدرته وعلمه وإرادته ، فإنَّ كلَّ ذلك ما لم يثبت .. لا يثبت الشرع ؛ إذ الشرع ينبني على الكلام ، فإن لم يثبت كلام النفس .. لم يثبت الشرع ، فكل ما يتقدم في الرتبة على كلام النفس يستحيل إثباته بكلام النفس وما يستند إليه ، ونفس الكلام أيضاً فيما اخترناه لا يمكن إثباتُهُ بالشرع . ومن المحققين من تكلَّفَ ذلك وادّعاه كما سبقت الإشارة إليه

وأما المعلوم بمجرد السمع .. فيخصِّصُ أحد الجائزين بالوقوع ؛ فإن ذلك من مواقف العقول، وإنما يعرفُ من الله تعالى بوحي وإلهام ، ونحن نعلمه من المُوحَى إليه بسماع ؛ كالحشر والنشر والثواب والعقاب وأمثالها

وأما المعلوم بهما .. فكلُّ ما هو واقع في مجال العقل ومتأخر في الرتبة عن إثبات كلام الله تعالى ؛ كمسألة الرؤية ، وانفراد الله تعالى بخلق الحركات والأعراض كلّها وما يجري هذا المجرى
The second chapter: On explaining the necessity of affirming matters that came through revelation and which reason has ruled as permissible. It contains an introduction and two sections. As for the introduction: it is that what is not known necessarily is divided into: what is known by rational proof without revelation, what is known by revelation without reason, and what is known by both. As for what is known by rational proof without revelation: it is the temporality of the world, the existence of the Temporally Originating Agent, His power, knowledge, and will. For all of that, if not established, revelation cannot be established; because revelation is built upon speech (kalam). If the speech of the soul (kalam an-nafs) is not established, revelation is not established. So everything prior in rank to the speech of the soul is impossible to establish by the speech of the soul and what is based on it. Also, the speech itself, in our chosen view, cannot be established by revelation. Some verifiers have taken upon themselves that and claimed it, as was previously indicated. And as for what is known by mere hearing (revelation): it specifies one of two possibilities as occurring; for that is beyond the scope of intellects, and it is only known from God Almighty by revelation and inspiration, and we know it from the one inspired by hearing: such as the gathering, the scattering, reward, punishment, and the like. And as for what is known by both: it is everything that falls within the scope of reason and is posterior in rank to the establishment of God's speech: such as the issue of the vision (of God), and God's sole creation of all movements and accidents, and whatever runs in this course.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a classification (taqsīm) by al-Ghazali of all knowledge that is not necessarily known (ḍarūrī). He divides it into three categories. First, knowledge that is purely rational: it does not depend on revelation (sharʿ) and in fact must precede it, because revelation itself is based on the speech of God (kalām Allāh), which must first be established. Examples include the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world and the existence and attributes of God. Second, knowledge that is purely revelational: it concerns matters that reason cannot determine, such as the specifics of the afterlife (resurrection, reward, punishment). These are known only through divine revelation (waḥy) and inspiration (ilhām) transmitted via hearing (samāʿ). Third, knowledge that is attained by both reason and revelation: it consists of matters that reason can consider but which are confirmed by revelation and are posterior to the establishment of God's speech, such as the vision of God (ruʾya) in the afterlife and God's exclusive creation of all movements and accidents. Al-Ghazali shows the order of dependence: rational knowledge is foundational, revelational knowledge adds specifics, and combined knowledge bridges the two.

Key Terms
ضرورة
عقل
شرع
كلام النفس
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all religious knowledge is solely from revelation, with no role for reason.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that rational knowledge about God is foundational and must precede revelation; revelation builds upon reason.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's introduction, what is the first category of what is not known necessarily?
A) What is known by revelation alone.
B) What is known by reason alone.
C) What is known by both reason and revelation.
D) What is known by consensus.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali establishes that rational knowledge about God is foundational, while revelation provides specifics and confirms matters within reason's scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on the Quran and Sunnah for guidance, but al-Ghazali says that revelation depends on rational proof that came before it. Have you ever truly examined the rational foundations of your faith—proofs for God's existence, His attributes, and the necessity of prophecy? If not, what does it mean to build your life on a foundation you have not verified?
?
Al-Ghazali states that matters like the resurrection and reward are known only by hearing (samāʿ). When you affirm these doctrines, is it because you have examined the chain of transmission and the veracity of the Messenger, or is it merely habit? What would it take for you to say 'I know' rather than 'I believe'?
?
If reason is foundational, then your intellect is a gift from God that must be used to establish the truth before revelation can be accepted. Are you actively using your reason to deepen your conviction, or have you settled for blind following? What specific rational proofs for God's existence have you personally understood and embraced?

Obligation of Belief When Reason Permits
Al-Ghazali states the ruling on belief in revealed matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when reason deems a revealed matter possible, belief is obligatory, and the strength of belief matches the strength of the proof.

Core Idea
Belief is obligatory when reason permits; certainty depends on proof strength.

Al-Ghazali begins by establishing a foundational principle for dealing with revelatory texts. If reason does not reject the possibility of a matter, then we must believe it. But how firmly? That depends on whether the evidence is categorical or probable. This lesson unpacks that principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let al-Ghazali's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Ghazali uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage presents two levels of belief based on proof strength: certain (qat'i) and probable (zanni). Find where each is defined and how al-Ghazali justifies the obligation.
He compares tasdiq to other acts that are based on probable proofs.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Al-Ghazali says 'tasdiq by tongue and heart is an act based on probable proofs like all other acts.' Ask yourself why this analogy is important for establishing the validity of probable belief.
This connects tasdiq to the broader category of actions (a'mal) that require only probable evidence in Islamic law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كلُّ ما ورد السمعُ به ينظرُ : فإن كان العقل مجوزاً له .. وجب التصديقُ به قطعاً إن كانت الأدلة السمعية قاطعة في متنها وسندها لا يتطرق إليها احتمال
ووجب التصديقُ بها ظناً إن كانت ظنيةً ؛ فإن وجوب التصديق باللسان والقلب هو عمل ينبني على الأدلة الظنية كسائر الأعمال
فنحن نعلم قطعاً إنكار الصحابة على من يدَّعي كون العبد خالقاً لشيء من الأشياء وعرض من الأعراض ، وكانوا ينكرون ذلك بمجرد قوله تعالى : اللهُ خَلِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ ومعلوم أنه عام قابل للتخصيص فلا يكون عمومه إلا مظنوناً ، وإنما صارت المسألة قطعية بالبحث عن الطرق العقلية التي ذكرناها، ونعلم أنهم كانوا ينكرونَ ذلك قبل البحث عن الطرق العقلية، فلا ينبغي أن يعتقد أنهم لم يلتفتوا إلى المدارك الظنية إلا في الفقهيات، بل الاعتقاد بالظنيات اعتبروها أيضاً في التصديقات الاعتقادية والقولية
Then, every matter that has come through revelation is considered: if reason deems it permissible, then affirming it is categorically obligatory if the revelatory proofs are categorical (qāṭiʿa) in their text and chain, not susceptible to possibility; and affirming it is probable (ẓann) if they are probable; for the obligation of affirmation (taṣdīq) by tongue and heart is an act based on probable proofs like all other acts. We know for certain the Companions' rejection of anyone claiming that the servant is a creator of any thing or any accident. And they rejected that merely by His saying: 'Allah is the Creator of all things.' And it is known that this is a general (ʿāmm) statement susceptible to specification, so its generality is only probable. Rather, the issue becomes certain only by investigating the rational paths we have mentioned. And we know that they rejected that before investigating the rational paths. So one should not think that they did not pay attention to probable sources except in jurisprudential matters; rather, they considered probable belief also in creedal and verbal affirmations.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (ḥukm) on how to treat revelatory texts when reason does not find them impossible. Al-Ghazali first states a general principle: any revealed matter that reason deems possible (mujawwaz) must be believed. The strength of that belief depends on the level of the revelatory evidence. If the evidence is categorical (qāṭiʿ) in both text (matn) and chain (sanad), then belief is absolutely certain. If the evidence is only probable (ẓannī), then belief is probable—yet it is still an act of the heart and tongue, just like other acts of worship that are based on probable proofs. To illustrate that probable evidence can indeed ground belief, al-Ghazali gives the example of the Companions. They rejected the claim that a human could be a creator of anything, relying on the Quranic verse 'Allah is the Creator of all things.' This verse is general (ʿāmm) and could be specified, so taken alone it is only probable. Yet the Companions still used it as a decisive proof. Only later did rational investigation confirm its certainty. Hence, al-Ghazali concludes that probable grounds are valid in matters of belief, not only in jurisprudence.

Key Terms
قطع
ظن
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only certain proofs demand belief; probable proofs are not binding.
Correction: Al-Ghazali explicitly states that probable proofs also entail belief, albeit a probable one, because tasdiq is an act and acts can be based on probable evidence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, when must a person believe a revealed matter that reason deems possible?
A) Only if the proof is categorical
B) Always, but the strength of belief depends on the proof's strength
C) Only if the matter is rationally necessary
D) Never, unless it is proven by reason

Key Takeaway
When reason permits a revealed matter, belief is obligatory, and its certainty matches the proof's strength.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious truth that is only probable (ẓannī), do you give it the same weight in your heart as a certain truth? Or do you waver, treating it as optional? This lesson calls you to actively believe even when the evidence is not absolute. How will you train your heart to act on probable proofs as acts of worship?
?
Al-Ghazali says that tasdiq is an action built on evidence just like other deeds. Your belief is a deed. Do you treat your beliefs as something you must act upon, or as mere intellectual assent? What would change if you saw every act of belief as a form of worship?
?
Consider a revealed matter that your reason does not find impossible but your ego finds uncomfortable. Are you willing to affirm it with your tongue and heart even if your mind desires absolute proof? What is holding you back from that tasdiq, and what step will you take today to overcome it?

The Companions' Use of Probable Proofs in Creed
Al-Ghazali illustrates the use of probable proofs in creed with the Companions' example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that probable proofs are valid grounds for belief in creedal matters, as shown by the Companions' practice.

Core Idea
Companions used probable proofs for creed, proving their validity in belief.

Al-Ghazali reinforces his earlier principle with a concrete example from the Companions. They rejected the claim that a human can create, citing a Quranic verse that is general and thus only probable. This demonstrates that probable evidence is sufficient for belief, not just for legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Ghazali presents the Companions' action as evidence.
Pay attention to the verse cited and its description as 'general' (ʿāmm).
2
Analyze the example
Identify the claim the Companions rejected, the proof they used, and why that proof is only probable. Then consider how rational investigation later made it certain.
The verse 'Allah is the Creator of all things' is general but subject to specification, so it is only probable by itself.
3
Understand the implication
Al-Ghazali concludes that one should not think probable sources are only for fiqh; they are also valid for creedal affirmations. Reflect on why this is important for defending Islamic theology.
This counters the view that only certain proofs are acceptable in 'aqidah.

When Reason Deems Impossible or Suspends
Al-Ghazali rules on revealed texts when reason deems impossible or suspends.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ghazali determines the response to revealed texts when reason either deems them impossible or suspends judgment.

Core Idea
Reason's judgment of impossibility triggers interpretation; its suspension still demands belief.

Al-Ghazali completes his discussion by laying down clear rules for how a Muslim should treat revealed texts that seem to conflict with reason. He distinguishes two cases: when reason definitively says impossible, and when reason is uncertain. This passage provides the criteria for when interpretation is mandatory and when simple belief suffices.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the two conditional phrases: 'إذا استحاله' and 'فإن توقف'.
2
Identify the two scenarios
Look for the two cases: (1) reason deems impossible, (2) reason suspends. Notice the different ruling for each.
The ruling for impossibility is 'تأويل' (interpretation); for suspension it is 'تصديق' (belief).
3
Understand the reasoning
Ask why interpretation is obligatory for impossible matters. Al-Ghazali argues that revelation cannot contradict reason definitively. For suspended matters, the absence of impossibility is enough; permissibility need not be affirmed by reason.
4
Apply the lesson to an example
Consider the hadiths of anthropomorphism. Most are not authentic; authentic ones are open to interpretation. See how this fits the first scenario.
This shows the practical application of al-Ghazali's rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما قضى العقلُ باستحالته .. فيجب فيه تأويلُ ما ورد السمعُ به، ولا يتصور أن يشتمل السمعُ على قاطع مخالف للمعقول، وظواهر أحاديثِ التشبيه أكثرها غير صحيحة، والصحيح منها ليس بقاطع، بل هو قابل للتأويل
فإن توقف العقلُ في شيء فلم يقض فيه باستحالة ولا جواز .. وجبَ التصديق أيضاً لأدلة السمع، فيكفي في وجوب التصديق انفكاك العقل عن القضاء بالإحالة، وليس يشترط اشتماله على القضاء بالتجويز
As for that which reason has judged as impossible: interpretation (taʾwīl) of what has come through revelation is obligatory. And it is inconceivable that revelation includes something categorical that contradicts reason. The apparent meanings of the hadiths of anthropomorphism (tashbīh) are mostly not authentic, and what is authentic among them is not categorical; rather, it is susceptible to interpretation. Then if reason suspends judgment about something and does not rule it as impossible nor permissible, belief (taṣdīq) is still obligatory due to the proofs of revelation; the sufficiency for the obligation of belief is the removal of reason's judgment of impossibility, and it is not required that reason includes a judgment of permissibility.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage completes the discussion by covering two remaining scenarios. First, when reason judges a revealed matter to be impossible (istiḥāla), then interpretation (taʾwīl) of the revealed text is obligatory. Reason is the arbiter of impossibility, and it is impossible for a categorical revelatory text to contradict reason. Al-Ghazali gives the example of hadiths that seem to describe God in physical terms (tashbīh). Most of these are not authentic, and those that are authentic are not categorical (qāṭiʿ) but open to interpretation—so they can be interpreted metaphorically to avoid contradiction with reason. Second, when reason suspends (tawaqquf) and does not judge something as either impossible or possible, belief in the revealed proof is still obligatory. The mere absence of a rational judgment of impossibility is enough; one does not need reason to explicitly deem it possible. In other words, the default is to accept revelation unless reason decisively rules it out.

Key Terms
استحالة
تأويل
تشبيه
توقف
جواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If reason does not deem something possible, then belief is optional or not required.
Correction: Al-Ghazali explicitly says that belief is obligatory as long as reason does not deem it impossible; the absence of impossibility suffices, not the presence of permissibility.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a revealed text when reason suspends judgment and does not deem it impossible or possible?
A) It must be rejected.
B) It must be interpreted metaphorically.
C) Belief in it is still obligatory.
D) It requires further rational investigation until a judgment is made.

Key Takeaway
Belief in revealed truth is obligatory unless reason decisively proves its impossibility; uncertainty does not lift the obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious teaching that your reason finds difficult or uncertain, do you suspend judgment and still believe, or do you withhold belief until you see clear possibility? Which impulse governs your heart more often, and what does that reveal about your trust in revelation?
?
Are there areas of your life—like divine decree or unseen matters—where you demand that reason first deem something possible before you accept it? How would your faith change if you truly embraced al-Ghazali’s principle that the mere absence of impossibility is enough?
?
If you genuinely believed that no categorical revelation can contradict reason, would you still approach difficult theological passages with the humility to interpret rather than reject? What would it cost you to submit your intellect to that principle today?
