Why Ghazali Omitted Non-Essential Topics
Ghazali explains why he omitted certain theological topics and classifies them into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Ghazali chose to omit certain theological debates from his concise creed and how he classifies them into three categories.

Core Idea
Not all theological debates are essential for sound belief.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali defends his decision to leave out many commonly discussed topics from his creedal work. He explains that a concise creed should only contain what is indispensable for the soundness of belief. He then categorizes the non-essential topics into three arts: intellectual, linguistic, and juristic. This lesson explores his reasoning and the classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once for familiarity, then read the literal translation. Notice Ghazali's careful justification for omission.
The passage includes a classification, so pay attention to the three categories.
2
Identify the three categories
Find where Ghazali lists the three arts (عقلي، لفظي، فقهي). Note the examples he gives for each.
Intellectual: power; Linguistic: terms like provision, faith; Juristic: commanding good, repentance.
3
Understand the criterion for importance
Reflect on Ghazali's statement: matters that one can be ignorant of without sin are not essential. Why does he consider these topics non-essential?
The criterion is necessity for correct belief, not mere theological interest.
4
Connect to the aim of the work
Ghazali states that the truly important is removing doubt about God's essence and attributes. Recognise that this work focuses only on that core.
This explains why other topics are excluded; the creed is meant for refinement, not exhaustive debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني : في الاعتذار عن الإخلال بفصول شحنت بها المعتقدات ورأيت الإعراض عن ذكرها أولى ؛ لأن المعتقدات المختصرة حقها ألا تشتمل إلا على المهم الذي لا بد منه في صحة الاعتقاد . أما الأمور التي لا حاجة إلى إخطارها بالبال وإن خطرت بالبال .. فلا ثمة أمور بالعقيدة لا معصية في عدم معرفتها وعدم العلم بأحكامها ، فالخوض فيها بحث عن حقائق الأمور ، وهي غير لائقة بما يراد منه تهذيب الاعتقاد ، وذلك الفن تحصره ثلاثة فنون ؛ عقلي ولفظي وفقهي . أما العقلي .. فكالبحث عن القدرة الحادثة أنها تتعلق بالضدين أم لا وتتعلق بالمختلفات أم لا ، وهل يجوز قدرة حادثة تتعلق بفعل مباين لمحل القدرة ، وأمثال له . وأما اللفظية .. فكالبحث عن المسمى باسم الرزق ما هو ؟ ولفظ التوفيق والخذلان والإيمان ما حدودها ومسبباتها ؟ وأما الفقهية.. فكالبحث عن الأمر بالمعروف متى يجب ، وعن التوبة وما حكمها ؟ . . . إلى نظائر ذلك . وكل ذلك ليس بمهم ، بل المهم أن ينفي الإنسان الشك عن نفسه في المهم في العقيدة ذات الله تعالى على القدر الذي حقق في القطب الأول ، وفي صفاته
Chapter Two: On excusing the omission of topics with which the creeds are filled, and I saw that turning away from mentioning them is more appropriate; because the concise creeds have the right not to include except the important that is indispensable for the soundness of belief. As for matters which there is no need to bring to mind, even if they occur to the mind… there are matters in the creed in whose ignorance and ignorance of their rulings there is no sin. So delving into them is a search into the realities of things, and they are not suitable for what is intended from the refinement of belief. And that art is encompassed by three arts: intellectual, linguistic, and juristic. As for the intellectual: such as research into the originated power, whether it relates to opposites or not, and whether it relates to different things or not, and whether it is permissible for an originated power to relate to an act distinct from the locus of the power, and the like. As for the linguistic: such as research into what is named with the term 'provision'? And the terms 'success' and 'abandonment' and 'faith', what are their definitions and causes? As for the juristic: such as research into commanding good when it is obligatory, and repentance and what its ruling is… and similar things. And all of that is not important. Rather, the important is that a person removes doubt from himself concerning what is important in the creed: about the essence of God Almighty to the extent that was verified in the first pole, and about His attributes.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a methodological apology from Ghazali for leaving out certain topics that are commonly found in creedal works. He states that concise creeds should only contain what is necessary for sound belief. Matters that one can be ignorant of without sin are not worth including. He then classifies these non-essential topics into three categories: intellectual, linguistic, and juristic. Intellectual topics include debates about the scope of originated power (like whether it can relate to opposites). Linguistic topics include defining terms like 'provision', 'success', 'abandonment', and 'faith'. Juristic topics include rules about commanding good and repentance. Ghazali affirms that all these are not important. The truly important thing is to remove doubt about God's essence (as verified in the first pole, i.e., the section on divine essence) and His attributes.

Key Terms
مهم
عقلي
لفظي
فقهي
تهذيب الاعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All theological debates are equally essential for correct belief.
Correction: Ghazali shows that only knowledge of God's essence and attributes is necessary; debates about power, definitions, and juristic details are non-essential and can be omitted from a concise creed.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which category of topics is considered 'not important' for a concise creed?
A) Only intellectual topics.
B) Only linguistic topics.
C) Only juristic topics.
D) All three: intellectual, linguistic, and juristic topics.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali deliberately excludes non-essential intellectual, linguistic, and juristic debates, focusing only on what is necessary for correct belief about God's essence and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How much of your time is spent debating theological nuances that Ghazali calls non-essential, versus deepening your certainty in God's essence and attributes? Is your heart truly focused on what is important, or are you distracted by intellectual curiosity? What will you change starting today?
?
Ghazali says ignorance of certain matters is not sinful. Are you holding yourself to a standard of knowing everything, even though the Prophet ﷺ and the Salaf emphasized simple, sound belief? Where does your pride lie—in extensive knowledge or in humble certainty?
?
Imagine you stood before Allah tomorrow; would He ask you about the definitions of 'tawfiq' and 'khidhlan' or about your conviction in His oneness and His perfect attributes? Read the source again: what is the one thing Ghazali says is truly important? Are you giving it your full attention, or are you distracted by debates that do not purify your heart?

Ghazali's Classification of Non-Essential Creedal Discussions
Ghazali classifies unnecessary investigations into three types and states they are not essential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's threefold classification of unnecessary theological discussions and why he considers them non-essential for correct belief.

Core Idea
Not all theological debates are essential for sound creed.

Imam al-Ghazali begins Chapter Two by explaining why he omits many commonly discussed topics from his creedal works. He distinguishes between essential matters necessary for correct belief and non-essential investigations that, while possibly interesting, are not obligatory to know. He then organizes these non-essential discussions into three clear categories: rational, verbal, and legal.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ghazali's apology
Read the opening where Ghazali explains his choice to omit certain chapters. Notice his criterion: only include what is indispensable for correct belief.
This sets the methodological principle.
2
Identify the three categories
Look for the three categories: rational, verbal, and legal. Each is defined by the type of questions it includes.
3
Study the examples
For each category, examine the examples Ghazali gives. For rational: discussions about capability; for verbal: definitions of terms; for legal: rulings on commanding good and repentance.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that all such discussions are 'not important'. Ask yourself why he makes this strong claim.
This relates to the purpose of creed: practical faith, not theoretical debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني: في الاعتذار عن الإخلال بفصول شحنت بها المعتقدات ورأيتُ الإعراض عن ذكرها أولى؛ لأن المعتقدات المختصرة حقها ألا تشتمل إلا على المهم الذي لا بد منه في صحة الاعتقاد. أما الأمور التي لا حاجة إلى إخطارها بالبال وإن خطرت بالبال فلا ثمة أمور بالعقيدة لا معصية في عدم معرفتها وعدم العلم بأحكامها، فالخوض فيها بحث عن حقائق الأمور، وهي غير لائقة بما يراد منه تهذيب الاعتقاد، وذلك الفن تحصره ثلاثة فنون: عقلي ولفظي وفقهي. أما العقلي فكالبحث عن القدرة الحادثة أنها تتعلق بالضدين أم لا وتتعلق بالمختلفات أم لا، وهل يجوز قدرة حادثة تتعلق بفعل مباين لمحل القدرة، وأمثال له. وأما اللفظية فكالبحث عن المسمى باسم الرزق ما هو؟ ولفظ التوفيق والخذلان والإيمان ما حدودها ومسبباتها؟ وأما الفقهية فكالبحث عن الأمر بالمعروف متى يجب، وعن التوبة وما حكمها؟ إلى نظائر ذلك. وكل ذلك ليس بمهم.
Chapter Two: On apologizing for omitting chapters with which the creeds have been filled, and I saw that turning away from mentioning them is better; because abbreviated creeds have the right not to include except the important without which correctness of belief is not sound. As for matters that there is no need to bring to the mind, and even if they occur to the mind, they are not matters in belief for which there is sin in not knowing them and not knowing their rulings, so delving into them is a search for the realities of things, and they are not suitable for what is intended to refine belief. And that art is encompassed by three arts: rational, verbal, and legal. As for the rational, it is like research about originated power: whether it relates to opposites or not, and whether it relates to different things or not, and whether it is permissible for an originated power to relate to an action that is different from the locus of power, and the like. As for the verbal, it is like research about what is named by the word 'provision'—what is it? And the words 'success', 'abandonment', and 'faith'—what are their definitions and causes? As for the legal, it is like research about commanding the good—when is it obligatory? And about repentance and what is its ruling? And the like of that. And all of that is not important.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is an apology and classification by Ghazali. He explains why he has omitted many discussions commonly found in creed works. He states that abbreviated creeds should only contain what is indispensable for correct belief. Matters that are not necessary to know, even if they come to mind, are not sinful to ignore. He divides these unnecessary investigations into three categories: rational, verbal, and legal. For each, he gives examples: rational discussions about the nature of originated power, whether it can relate to opposites, etc.; verbal discussions about the definitions of terms like 'provision', 'success', 'abandonment', and 'faith'; legal discussions about when commanding the good is obligatory and rulings on repentance. He concludes that all such discussions are not important for the obligatory creed.

Correction: Ghazali distinguishes between essential and non-essential discussions. One is not sinful for not knowing these non-essential matters.

The Essentials of Islamic Creed According to Ghazali
Ghazali enumerates the four essential matters of creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's concise summary of the four essential matters of creed: God's essence, attributes, actions, and the Prophet's truthfulness.

Core Idea
Creed centers on four things: God's essence, attributes, actions, and the Prophet.

Imam al-Ghazali, after extensive discussion, distills the core of belief into four fundamental areas. This passage shows that the essential creed is not about endless debate but about removing doubt concerning these key elements. As you read, note how each area corresponds to a major section of his work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall structure.
Notice the four items are clearly separated by 'وفي'.
2
Identify the four essential matters
List them: 1) God's essence, 2) His attributes and rulings, 3) His actions (as possible not obligatory), 4) the Prophet's truthfulness and belief in all he brought.
Each corresponds to a 'pole' (section) of Ghazali's book.
3
Reflect on Ghazali's purpose
Ask yourself why Ghazali limits the 'important' to these four. What does this exclude?
This shows his emphasis on foundational beliefs over peripheral debates.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل المهم أن ينفي الإنسان الشك عن نفسه في المهم في العقيدة: ذات الله تعالى على القدر الذي حقق في القطب الأول، وفي صفاته وأحكامها كما حقق في القطب الثاني، وفي أفعاله بأن يعتقد فيها الجواز دون الوجوب كما في القطب الثالث، وفي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بأن يعرف صدقه ويصدقه في كل ما جاء به كما ذكرناه في هذا القطب.
Rather, the important thing is that a person remove doubt from himself regarding what is important in the creed: the essence of God Almighty to the extent that was verified in the first pole, and regarding His attributes and their rulings as verified in the second pole, and regarding His actions by believing in them as possible (jawaz) not obligatory (wujub) as in the third pole, and regarding the Messenger of God, may God bless him, by knowing his truthfulness and believing him in everything he brought, as we mentioned in this pole.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage defines the essential content of creed. Ghazali says the important thing is to remove doubt concerning four matters: first, God's essence as established in the first section (pole); second, God's attributes and their rulings as in the second; third, God's actions—that they are possible (not obligatory) as in the third; fourth, the Prophet—knowing his truthfulness and believing everything he conveyed. These are the core of belief.

?

?
Do you believe the Prophet in everything he brought, even those teachings that challenge your desires, intellect, or social norms? Can you name one teaching of the Prophet that you struggle to fully accept? Commit today to seeking understanding and surrendering your judgment to his truthfulness.

The Logical Principle of Independence
Ghazali introduces a rational issue and states the logical principle of independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical principle of independence and its application to the issue of the appointed term.

Core Idea
Independent events do not necessitate each other's existence or non-existence.

Ghazali introduces a rational issue that is not essential to creed but serves to illustrate a method. He presents the example of whether a killed person dies by his appointed term, and then establishes a general logical rule: if two things have no connection, their coexistence does not imply that one depends on the other. This passage teaches how to reason about such cases.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Then read the translation. Notice the shift from introducing the issue to stating the principle.
Ghazali calls this issue 'optional'—not necessary to believe in for salvation.
2
Identify the logical principle
Look for the sentence starting with 'فنقول: كل شيئين...'. This is the core rule: if two things have no connection, their conjunction in existence does not make one depend on the other.
The principle is expressed negatively: from supposing the negation of one, the negation of the other does not follow.
3
Apply the principle to the examples
Consider the two examples: the simultaneous deaths of Zayd and Amr, and the coincidence of death with a lunar eclipse. Ask why the conclusion holds in each case.
The connection between the events is only temporal, not causal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما خرج عن هذا فغير مهم، ونحن نورد من كل فن مما أهملناه مسألة لتعرف بها نظائرها، ويحقق خروجها عن المهمات المقصودة في المعتقدات. مثال المسائل العقلية: فكاختلاف الناس في أن من قتل: هل يقال: إنه ميت بأجله؟ والمقتول: هل مات بأجله؟ ولو قدر عدم قتله هل كان يجب موته أم لا؟ وهذا فن من العلم لا يضر تركه، ولكنا نشير إلى طريق الكشف فيه. فنقول: كل شيئين لا ارتباط لأحدهما بالآخر ثم اقترنا في الوجود فليس يلزم من تقدير نفي أحدهما انتفاء الآخر، فلو مات زيد وعمرو معاً ثم قدرنا عدم موت زيد لم يلزم منه لا عدم موت عمرو ولا وجود موته، وكذا إذا مات زيد عند كسوف القمر مثلاً، فلو قدرنا عدم الموت لم يلزم عدم الكسوف بالضرورة، ولو قدرنا عدم الكسوف لم يلزم عدم الموت؛ إذ لا ارتباط لأحدهما بالآخر.
And what falls outside this is not important. We will present from each discipline that we have omitted a question so that its analogues may be known, and that it may be confirmed that they fall outside the important objectives in beliefs. An example of rational questions: such as the disagreement among people concerning the one who is killed: Is it said that he died by his appointed term? And the killed one: Did he die by his appointed term? And if his being killed were supposed not to have occurred, would his death have been necessary or not? This is a branch of knowledge whose neglect is not harmful, but we will indicate the method of uncovering it. So we say: For any two things between which there is no connection, and then they concur in existence, it does not necessarily follow from supposing the negation of one that the other is negated. For if Zayd and Amr died together, and then we supposed that Zayd had not died, neither the non-death of Amr nor his death would necessarily follow. Likewise, if Zayd died at the time of a lunar eclipse, for example, and we supposed the absence of death, the absence of the eclipse would not necessarily follow, and if we supposed the absence of the eclipse, the absence of death would not necessarily follow, because there is no connection between them.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage introduces an illustrative rational issue that is not essential to the main creedal objectives, showing the method of analysis. Ghazali states that although such issues are not obligatory to study, he will present an example to demonstrate the approach. The example is the theological question of whether a killed person dies by his appointed term (ajal) or whether the act of killing alters it. He then lays down a general logical principle: if two things have no inherent connection but happen to coexist, the absence of one does not necessitate the absence of the other. He illustrates with two examples: two unrelated deaths occurring simultaneously, and a death coinciding with a lunar eclipse. In both cases, one can conceive of one occurring without the other.

Key Terms
أجل
ارتباط
تقدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali treats this issue as irrelevant to creed and therefore completely unimportant.
Correction: Ghazali only says it is not essential, not that it is unimportant. He still presents it to teach a method of reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the logical principle stated by Ghazali, if two things have no connection and occur together, what follows from supposing the negation of one?
A) The negation of the other necessarily follows.
B) The other's existence is necessary.
C) Neither the negation nor the existence of the other necessarily follows.
D) The other is also negated by divine decree.

Key Takeaway
The principle of independence is a logical tool that clarifies that concurrent events do not imply causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that a coincidence was a sign or cause that wasn't actually connected? How might this principle of independence change how you interpret such events?
?
Ghazali says this issue is not harmful to neglect, yet he still clarifies it. What does this reveal about the importance of precise reasoning even in matters you consider trivial?
?
Think of a time you blamed yourself for something that coincided with your action but was not caused by it. How does this passage challenge you to re-evaluate that self-blame and to trust in God's decree without false causal assumptions?

Practice Deepens Belief
Ghazali explains how practice strengthens belief with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how consistent performance of acts corresponding to a belief increases inner certainty and tranquility.

Core Idea
Actions do not just express belief—they fortify it.

Ghazali begins by describing a spiritual truth that only introspection can reveal: persistent practice of obedience does not merely express belief, it feeds back into the heart, making conviction more intimate and resistant to doubt. He illustrates with examples from everyday compassion and Islamic worship, showing that the body's motions shape the soul's states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on practice and belief
Read the Arabic text from 'التقليدي ورسوخه' to 'ليزداد بسببه تعظيم القلوب' slowly, then follow the translation. Notice how Ghazali asserts that this effect is known only through personal experience.
2
Examine the two examples
Identify the example of mercy toward an orphan and the example of humility toward another person. See how each shows that acting on a feeling increases that very feeling.
These examples bridge the general principle to the specific case of worship.
3
Connect to Islamic worship
Note how Ghazali applies the principle to the prescribed acts of bowing and prostration. Understand that these physical postures are not empty rituals but are designed to cultivate inner reverence.
This is the practical takeaway for the believer.
4
Reflect on the personal dimension
Ask yourself: Have I experienced this in my own life? How does consistent prayer or charity affect my inner state? This is what Ghazali invites the reader to observe.
The lesson is not just theoretical but experiential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقليدي ورسوخه في النفس، وهذا أمر لا يعرفه إلا من سبر أحوال نفسه، وراقبها في وقت المواظبة على الطاعة وفي وقت الفترة، ولاحظ تفاوت الحال في باطنه؛ فإنه يزداد بسبب المواظبة على العمل أنساً بمعتقداته، وتتأكد به طمأنينته، حتى إن المعتقد الذي طالت منه المواظبة على العمل بموجب اعتقاده أعصى نفساً على المحاول تغييره وتشكيكه ممن لم تطل مواظبته، بل العادات تقضي بها؛ فإن من يعتقد في قلبه الرحمة على يتيم فإن أقدم على مسح رأسه وتفقد أمره.. صادف في قلبه عند ممارسة العمل بموجب الرحمة زيادة تأكيد في الرحمة، ومن يتواضع بقلبه لغيره، فإذا عمل بموجبه ساجداً له أو مقبلاً يده. ازداد التعظيم والتواضع في قلبه؛ ولذلك تعبدنا بالمواظبة على أفعال مقتضى تعظيم القلب من الركوع والسجود؛ ليزداد بسببه تعظيم القلوب. فهذه أمور يجحدها المتحذلقون في الكلام، الذين أدركوا ترتيب العلوم بسماع الألفاظ ولم يدركوها بذوق النظر، فهذه حقيقة هذه المسألة ومن هذا الجنس اختلافهم في معنى الرزق، وقول المعتزلة: إن ذلك مخصوص بما يملكه الإنسان؛ حتى ألزموا أنه لا رزق الله تعالى على حقيقة الرزق؟ البهائم، وربما قالوا: هو ما لا يحرم تناوله، فقيل لهم: فالظلمة ماتوا وقد عاشوا طول عمرهم ولم يرزقوا! وقال أصحابنا: إنه عبارة عن المنتفع به كيف كان، واصرفه في المهم ثم هو منقسم إلى حلال وحرام، ثم طولوا في حد الرزق وحد النعمة، وتضييع الوقت بهذا اعرف لعمرك قدره وأمثاله دأب لا من يميز بين المهم وغيره، ولا يعرف قدر بقية عمره وأنه لا قيمة له، فلا ينبغي أن يضيع إلا بالأهم، وبين يدي النظار أمور مشكلة البحث عنها أهم من البحث عن موجب الألفاظ ومقتضى الإطلاقات، فنسأل الله تعالى أن يوفقنا للاشتغال بما يعنينا. وهذا القول لجماعة منهم فقط، وبعضهم وافق أهل السنة في ذلك.
The traditional and its rootedness in the soul, and this is a matter known only to one who probes the states of his soul and watches it at the time of persistence in obedience and at the time of slackness, and observes the variation of the state within him; for he increases by persistence in work intimacy with his beliefs, and his tranquility is strengthened by it, so that the belief which long persistence in work according to its requirement makes the soul more resistant to anyone trying to change it and cause doubt than one whose persistence has not been long. Rather, habits necessitate it; for he who believes in his heart mercy for an orphan, if he proceeds to wipe his head and attend to his affairs, he finds in his heart upon practicing the action according to the requirement of mercy an increase of confirmation in mercy. And he who humbles himself in his heart to another, if he acts accordingly by prostrating to him or kissing his hand, reverence and humility increase in his heart. Therefore, we are commanded to persist in actions that necessitate the heart's reverence, such as bowing and prostration, so that the heart's reverence increases thereby. These are matters denied by the pretentious in kalam, who have grasped the arrangement of sciences by hearing words but have not grasped them by the taste of reflection. So this is the reality of this issue, and from this type is their disagreement over the meaning of provision (rizq), and the statement of the Mu'tazila: that it is particular to what a person possesses; until they were forced to say that there is no provision of God Almighty in the true sense of provision for beasts? And sometimes they said: it is that which it is not forbidden to consume. So it was said to them: then the oppressors have died after living their entire lives without being provided for! And our companions said: it is an expression for that which is benefited from, however it may be, and [؟] then it is divided into lawful and unlawful. Then they prolonged concerning the definition of provision and the definition of blessing, and wasting time with this—you, I swear by your life, know its worth and its like—is the habit of one who does not distinguish between important and other matters, and does not know the value of the remainder of his life and that it is precious, so it should not be wasted except on the most important. Before the scholars there are problematic matters whose investigation is more important than investigating the implications of words and the requirements of usages. So we ask God Almighty to grant us success in occupying ourselves with what concerns us. And this statement is only for a group of them, and some of them agreed with Ahl al-Sunna in that.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is an argument about how consistent practice (muwazaba) fortifies and deepens belief (i'tiqad) in the soul, followed by a critique of those who engage in purely verbal disputes, such as the disagreement over the definition of 'rizq' (provision). The author begins by stating that this strengthening is known only through introspection: one who regularly performs acts of obedience finds his heart more at ease with his beliefs, making them harder to shake. He illustrates with examples: showing mercy to an orphan through action increases the feeling of mercy; humbling oneself physically increases inner humility. This is why Islamic worship includes repeated physical acts like bowing and prostration—they increase the heart's reverence. Then he criticizes the 'pretentious in kalam' (mutahaddiqun) who know the order of the sciences only from hearing words, not from the 'taste of reflection' (dhawq al-nazar). The dispute over rizq is an example of this: the Mu'tazila restricted it to what one owns or what is lawful, leading to absurdities like claiming oppressors were not provided for. The Ash'arites (as'habuna) define it more broadly as anything beneficial. The author laments that such definitions waste time better spent on important matters, and prays for guidance to focus on what matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Outward actions have no effect on inner belief; they are mere expressions.
Correction: Ghazali argues that outward actions performed consistently directly increase the inner state of belief and tranquility.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the effect of consistently performing acts that correspond to a belief?
A) The belief becomes weaker over time.
B) The heart becomes more intimate with the belief and more tranquil.
C) The belief is unaffected; only outward behavior changes.
D) Doubt increases because actions are mechanical.

Key Takeaway
Consistent practice is not just obedience but a means to deepen and solidify belief from within.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel spiritually lazy and stop performing a certain act of worship, have you noticed that your conviction in its importance also fades? How does Ghazali's insight explain this, and what will you do to break that cycle this week?
?
Think of one belief you hold dearly—perhaps about God's mercy or your own need for Him. How much of that belief's strength is due to repeated actions that embody it? If you let those actions slip, can you honestly say the belief would remain just as firm?
?
You recite the prayers daily, bowing and prostrating. Before this lesson, did you think of those postures as mere ritual, or as a way to actually build reverence? Starting tomorrow, what one change will you make in your prayer to let the action more deeply affect your heart?
